# The Modern Herbal Dispensatory

# Praise for The Modern Herbal Dispensatory

<!-- chunk -->

### “The Modern Herbal Dispensatory relies on the solid foundation that comes

<!-- chunk -->

### from direct interaction with living, breathing botanicals. To this it adds a

<!-- chunk -->

### specificity gleaned from twenty-first century science: clear guidelines rooted in

<!-- chunk -->

### chemistry and physiology provide definitive answers to questions of extraction

<!-- chunk -->

### method, solvent, timing, and formulation. If you’ve ever wondered which

<!-- chunk -->

### extraction method to use for a given plant, or felt reluctant to try advanced

<!-- chunk -->

### techniques like percolation, this clear and concise reference guide will become a

<!-- chunk -->

### trusted companion. A must-have for herbal educators, clinicians, and

<!-- chunk -->

### manufacturers alike!”

<!-- chunk -->

### —Guido Masé, cofounder and codirector, Vermont Center for Integrative

<!-- chunk -->

### Herbalism

<!-- chunk -->

### “Thomas Easley and Steven Horne have done it once again. In the Modern

<!-- chunk -->

### Herbal Dispensatory, historic traditions of medicine making have been captured

<!-- chunk -->

### and shared in a way that empowers the modern lay herbalist to capture the

<!-- chunk -->

### benefits of botanical medicine in the most accessible and potent form. Weaving

<!-- chunk -->

### together folk teachings and scientific perspectives, this book bridges the

<!-- chunk -->

### spectrum of herbal medicine to provide an inspiring, detailed, and practical

<!-- chunk -->

### handbook for herbalists at all levels of experience. This guide should be on the

<!-- chunk -->

### bookshelves of every herbal school, home apothecary, and herbal production

<!-- chunk -->

### facility.”

<!-- chunk -->

### —Emily Ruff, executive director, Florida School of Holistic Living

## INTRODUCTION

# On Herbal Medicine

<!-- chunk -->

### Herbal medicine is one of the most ancient of the healing arts. It is, and always

<!-- chunk -->

### will be, the medicine of the people. No matter which political party is in power,

<!-- chunk -->

### no matter what is deemed legal versus illegal, not even the FDA in all of its

<!-- chunk -->

### regulatory glory can prevent someone from stepping out their door and using

<!-- chunk -->

### nature’s free medicine. Herbal medicine exists, and always has, because we live

<!-- chunk -->

### in symbiosis with plants. As herbalist Sam Coffman says, with every breath we

<!-- chunk -->

### take we perform mouth-to-mouth resuscitation with nature. Plants have been

<!-- chunk -->

### here longer than we have. They have learned their lessons and adapted to their

<!-- chunk -->

### environment, and they have produced a beautiful language to communicate those

<!-- chunk -->

### lessons with other plants, animals, and fungi. The chemical compounds that

<!-- chunk -->

### plants produce, their biochemical language, is so complicated that we haven’t

<!-- chunk -->

### even scratched the surface in the thousand or so plants that have been

<!-- chunk -->

### researched, let alone the remaining tens of thousands of plants around the world

<!-- chunk -->

### that are used as medicine.

<!-- chunk -->

### Although we may not understand how plants act chemically in our body, we

<!-- chunk -->

### have a long and well-documented history of their use as food and medicine. As

<!-- chunk -->

### we move toward a more sustainable world with clean energy production, locally

<!-- chunk -->

### grown organic foods, and nature conservation, we must look at our current

<!-- chunk -->

### model of medicine through the same filter of sustainability. A world that remains

<!-- chunk -->

### totally dependent on high-cost chemical medicines, controlled by multinational

<!-- chunk -->

### pharmaceutical companies whose main purpose is profit, is dependent upon the

<!-- chunk -->

### very institutions that created the environmental problems that are poisoning this

<!-- chunk -->

### world.

<!-- chunk -->

### Modern medicine and the judicious use of pharmaceuticals are essential for

<!-- chunk -->

### treating many serious illnesses. But although it is a great disease-care system,

<!-- chunk -->

### modern medical care is not a health care system. Any system of medicine that is

<!-- chunk -->

### separated from the greater whole of health, including food production, ecological

<!-- chunk -->

### health, social health, and emotional health, can only put a bandage on a bullet

<!-- chunk -->

### wound, conveniently masking the real issues that are slowly bleeding us to

<!-- chunk -->

### death. Rising costs for disease care and lack of access to care are taking a toll on

<!-- chunk -->

### everyone, and minorities and underprivileged groups bear the brunt of that

<!-- chunk -->

### burden. In America, disease care is the primary cause of financial ruin: Nearly

<!-- chunk -->

### two million people file bankruptcy every year because of medical bills.

# USING HERBS AS MEDICINE

<!-- chunk -->

### Medicinal plants grow everywhere and are easily available for harvest or

<!-- chunk -->

### purchase for a fraction of the cost of modern pharmaceuticals. Learning how to

<!-- chunk -->

### make your own herbal medicines is as easy as learning how to cook. The goal of

<!-- chunk -->

### this book is to empower you to use herbs to help yourself, your family, and

<!-- chunk -->

### others.

<!-- chunk -->

### Mastering herbalism involves a lot of work. Different herbs with entirely

<!-- chunk -->

### different medicinal actions can have the same common name. Conversely, the

<!-- chunk -->

### same herb can be called many different names depending on what book you are

<!-- chunk -->

### looking at. Learning the Latin (botanical) name of a plant is a good place to start

<!-- chunk -->

### to identify the correct herb, but identifying the right plant is just the first step.

<!-- chunk -->

### Different parts of a single plant can have different actions on the body. The

<!-- chunk -->

### root of dandelion is a wonderful digestive tonic and gently stimulates phase one

<!-- chunk -->

### liver detoxification. Dandelion leaves, on the other hand, are a strong diuretic;

<!-- chunk -->

### and the flowers, prepared as a flower essence or a wine, are specific for helping

<!-- chunk -->

### overachievers who are tense and stressed to learn to go with the flow.

<!-- chunk -->

### How you use an herb makes a difference in how it affects the body. Different

<!-- chunk -->

### constituents are soluble in different mediums. Some constituents of plants are

<!-- chunk -->

### exclusively alcohol soluble, whereas others only come out in water. Yarrow is a

<!-- chunk -->

### great herb for fevers when it is prepared as a hot infusion (tea) of the flowers. A

<!-- chunk -->

### hot infusion draws out the aromatic qualities of yarrow that stimulate circulation

<!-- chunk -->

### and promote perspiration, whereas a decoction extracts more of the bitter and

<!-- chunk -->

### astringent principles. Many Native Americans used a cooled decoction of the

<!-- chunk -->

### whole herb (flowers and leaves) as a digestive tonic for weak digestion. The

<!-- chunk -->

### dried leaves make an excellent styptic for cuts and wounds, and encapsulated

<!-- chunk -->

### yarrow clears the lymphatics, stimulates innate immunity, and helps relieve

<!-- chunk -->

### urinary tract infections. Yarrow essential oil is anti-inflammatory, but most of

<!-- chunk -->

### this oil is lost when the herb is dried. Yarrow flower essence is used to help

<!-- chunk -->

### sensitive individuals who overidentify with other people’s problems (a

<!-- chunk -->

### characteristic that comes naturally to many herbalists).

<!-- chunk -->

### Some books list yarrow as being good for toothache; the Diné (Navajo) and

<!-- chunk -->

### other Native Americans use yarrow for this purpose. But swallowing a capsule

<!-- chunk -->

### or tincture won’t do anything for a toothache, nor will chewing on dried mature

<!-- chunk -->

### leaves. The part of the plant that is used for toothache is the purplish part of the

<!-- chunk -->

### young leaves, which contain a topical analgesic. The fresh young leaves are

<!-- chunk -->

### chewed to relieve tooth pain. As you can see, knowing how a plant is prepared

<!-- chunk -->

### and how that changes its usage is an important part of effective herbalism.

<!-- chunk -->

### Much of the information about how to properly prepare and administer herbs

<!-- chunk -->

### is being lost as people increasingly rely on commercial herbal preparations.

<!-- chunk -->

### Traditional herbalists keep the knowledge of how to make and use herbs alive.

<!-- chunk -->

### This book aims to present different methods for preparing and administering

<!-- chunk -->

### herbs to help you choose not only the appropriate remedy but the preparation

<!-- chunk -->

### method that brings forth the actions you want. We hope it will help you learn to

<!-- chunk -->

### use herbs in new and creative ways.

Photo by Chamille White


<!-- chunk -->

## CHAPTER ONE

# Getting Started

<!-- chunk -->

## BASIC CONCEPTS IN HERBAL MEDICINE

<!-- chunk -->

### his book is about making herbal medicines, and if you’re going to make

<!-- chunk -->

### effective herbal medicines, you need to be familiar with some basic

<!-- chunk -->

### concepts.

# HERBAL ENERGETICS

<!-- chunk -->

### The major constituents that give herbs their various actions can be detected using

<!-- chunk -->

### human senses. The effects of these basic constituents can be felt and observed in

<!-- chunk -->

### one’s own body. This observation and detection are what modern Western

<!-- chunk -->

### herbalists call energetics.

<!-- chunk -->

### Herbs can be divided into broad energetic categories based on their taste,

<!-- chunk -->

### constituents, and basic effects on the body. Learning these basic categories is

<!-- chunk -->

### like learning the alphabet or musical notes: They form the basis for

<!-- chunk -->

### understanding the language of herbalism. Just as musical notes are arranged

<!-- chunk -->

### together to create an infinite array of music, the energetic properties of herbs

<!-- chunk -->

### blend together to create thousands of unique herbal profiles.

<!-- chunk -->

### The twelve basic categories of herbs we’re about to introduce you to have

<!-- chunk -->

### some basic qualities in common. We refer to these in energetic terms, using three

<!-- chunk -->

### sets of qualities.

# HOW HERBS AFFECT ENERGY PRODUCTION

<!-- chunk -->

## ▪ Warming refers to herbs that stimulate or speed up metabolism, increase

<!-- chunk -->

### energy production and warmth, and bring blood flow and vitality to tissues that

<!-- chunk -->

### are pale and cool.

<!-- chunk -->

## ▪ Cooling refers to herbs that sedate or slow down metabolism to decrease

<!-- chunk -->

### energy production while cooling or soothing irritation and redness.

<!-- chunk -->

## ▪ Neutral describes herbs that are neither warm nor cool. Neutral herbs do not

<!-- chunk -->

### have a strong effect on circulation or cellular metabolism.

# HOW HERBS AFFECT THE DENSITY OF TISSUES

<!-- chunk -->

## ▪ Moistening refers to herbs that increase the moisture content of tissues, which

<!-- chunk -->

### means they lubricate and soften dry, brittle, or hardened tissues.

<!-- chunk -->

## ▪ Drying refers to herbs that remove excess fluid from tissue, causing it to

<!-- chunk -->

### become more firm and dense, relieving conditions of dampness and swelling.

<!-- chunk -->

## ▪ Balancing is the term we use for herbs that normalize tissues that are either

<!-- chunk -->

### damp or dry, helping to balance the amount of moisture and solids (minerals)

<!-- chunk -->

### within the tissues.

# HOW HERBS AFFECT MUSCLE TONE, FLOW, AND

# SECRETION

<!-- chunk -->

## ▪ Constricting refers to herbs that increase the tone or tension within muscles

<!-- chunk -->

### and other tissues, which stops excess flow and secretion. These herbs tone up

<!-- chunk -->

### tissues that have become overly relaxed or weak and that are leaking or

<!-- chunk -->

### secreting fluids such as blood or mucus.

<!-- chunk -->

## ▪ Relaxing refers to herbs that relax muscle cramps and spasms, relieving excess

<!-- chunk -->

### tension in the tissues. This promotes easier flow and movement and can help to

<!-- chunk -->

### increase deficient secretion.

<!-- chunk -->

## ▪ Nourishing is the term we use for herbs that provide essential nutrients that aid

<!-- chunk -->

### tissue healing, improving tissue structure and function.

# THE TWELVE CATEGORIES OF HERBS

<!-- chunk -->

### With this basic understanding of energetic terms, let’s look at the twelve

<!-- chunk -->

### categories of herbs referred to in this book. Keep in mind that any individual

<!-- chunk -->

### herb may fit into more than one category.

# PUNGENT HERBS

<!-- chunk -->

### Pungent herbs have a spicy or hot taste and typically have a very sharp aroma.

<!-- chunk -->

### These are plants that are used to add spiciness to dishes, such as capsicum

<!-- chunk -->

### (cayenne pepper), ginger, mustard, and onions. The pungent flavor of these herbs

<!-- chunk -->

### is due to the presence of resins, alkamides, allyl sulfides, or monoterpene

<!-- chunk -->

### essential oils.

<!-- chunk -->

### Herbs with a pungent taste are warming and drying. They move blood and

<!-- chunk -->

### energy upward toward the head and outward from the interior of the body to the

<!-- chunk -->

### skin and mucous membranes. This means they help to dispel stagnation, induce

<!-- chunk -->

### perspiration, and stimulate blood circulation. They stimulate production of

<!-- chunk -->

### digestive secretions, which enhances appetite, expels gas, and increases

<!-- chunk -->

### intestinal peristalsis.

<!-- chunk -->

### Overuse of pungent herbs depletes the body’s energy reserves and cools the

<!-- chunk -->

### body. Some people find these herbs irritating to their digestive tract. Pungent

<!-- chunk -->

### herbs are contraindicated for people who tend to be hot, flushed, and irritable

<!-- chunk -->

### and who have a reddish complexion.

# AROMATIC HERBS

<!-- chunk -->

### Aromatic herbs contain volatile oils (also called essential oils). Volatile oils

<!-- chunk -->

### evaporate when they are exposed to heat and light. Like pungent herbs, many

<!-- chunk -->

### aromatics are used as seasonings in food. The mint and carrot families contain

<!-- chunk -->

### many aromatic herbs, including dill, peppermint, and lemon balm.

<!-- chunk -->

### Aromatic herbs are also normally warming and drying, but they have a milder

<!-- chunk -->

### action than pungent herbs. They tend to have strong effects on the nervous

<!-- chunk -->

### system, either calming or stimulating. Many essential oils are antimicrobial,

<!-- chunk -->

### which makes aromatic herbs helpful in fighting infections. Aromatics can induce

<!-- chunk -->

### perspiration when taken as hot tea, and they stimulate blood circulation and

<!-- chunk -->

### expel intestinal gas.

<!-- chunk -->

### Aromatic herbs are very safe. However, pure essential oils should be used

<!-- chunk -->

### almost exclusively in topical applications; even then, they need to be well

<!-- chunk -->

### diluted. Essential oils are highly concentrated extracts, and they are far more

<!-- chunk -->

### likely than whole herbs to create bad reactions.

# SIMPLE (NONALKALOIDAL) BITTERS

<!-- chunk -->

### Simple bitters are herbs that are bitter due to what old herbal textbooks called

<!-- chunk -->

### bitter principles. Today we know these compounds as diterpenes and various

<!-- chunk -->

### glycosides. Anthraquinone glycosides are responsible for the action of stimulant

<!-- chunk -->

### laxatives, which are a subcategory of simple bitters. Nonalkaloidal bitters

<!-- chunk -->

### include artichoke leaf, gentian, wild lettuce, kale, and hops. Stimulant laxatives

<!-- chunk -->

### include cascara sagrada, Turkey rhubarb, buckthorn, butternut bark, and aloe leaf

<!-- chunk -->

### (not the gel).

<!-- chunk -->

### Most nonalkaloidal bitters are cooling and drying. A few contain aromatic

<!-- chunk -->

### compounds that make them warming and drying, including dong quai and

<!-- chunk -->

### turmeric.

<!-- chunk -->

### Bitters cause energy to move downward (toward the eliminative organs) and

<!-- chunk -->

### inward (toward the digestive organs). Nonalkaloidal bitters tend to be

<!-- chunk -->

### detoxifying. Some have sedative or calming effects and a few are anodynes,

<!-- chunk -->

### which means they help to relieve pain. One of their primary uses is to stimulate

<!-- chunk -->

### the production of hydrochloric acid, bile, and pancreatic enzymes. This happens

<!-- chunk -->

### only when the bitters are tasted; bitter herbs that are sweetened or swallowed in

<!-- chunk -->

### capsules do not stimulate digestive secretions.

<!-- chunk -->

### Cooling bitters can deplete digestion over time. Traditional digestive tonics

<!-- chunk -->

### include warming bitters, aromatic or pungent herbs to modulate the depleting

<!-- chunk -->

### effects of cooling bitters. Bitters should be avoided by thin, weak, emaciated,

<!-- chunk -->

### and dry people.

# ALKALOIDAL BITTERS

<!-- chunk -->

### Alkaloidal bitters taste bitter due to the presence of alkaloids. These compounds

<!-- chunk -->

### have names ending in -ine, such as caffeine, nicotine, and berberine. Coffee and

<!-- chunk -->

### chocolate are alkaloidal bitters. Herbs that contain alkaloids include goldenseal,

<!-- chunk -->

### Oregon grape, and California poppy.

<!-- chunk -->

### Like nonalkaloidal bitters, alkaloidal bitters tend to be cooling and drying.

<!-- chunk -->

### Many are detoxifying and used to stimulate the digestive system and liver.

<!-- chunk -->

### Alkaloidal bitters that contain berberine, such as goldenseal and Oregon grape,

<!-- chunk -->

### are used to fight infections. Alkaloids have very specific effects on the nervous

<!-- chunk -->

### and glandular systems and can mimic hormones and neurotransmitters,

<!-- chunk -->

### stimulating or sedating specific body processes.

<!-- chunk -->

### Alkaloidal bitters have the same general contraindications that nonalkaloidal

<!-- chunk -->

### bitters do. They should be avoided by thin, weak, emaciated, and dry people, and

<!-- chunk -->

### can be drying and depleting when excessively used. Pay attention to the specific

<!-- chunk -->

### indications and contraindications for each herb in this category.

# FRAGRANT BITTERS

<!-- chunk -->

### Fragrant bitters are a cross between simple bitters and aromatics. Their primary

<!-- chunk -->

### constituents are sesquiterpene lactones and triterpenes. Examples of fragrant

<!-- chunk -->

### bitters include elecampane, black walnut hulls, wormwood, tansy, and

<!-- chunk -->

### wormseed.

<!-- chunk -->

### Fragrant bitters are warming and drying. They are used in small amounts to

<!-- chunk -->

### stimulate appetite and digestion. Many are used to expel parasites. Most fragrant

<!-- chunk -->

### bitters are contraindicated in pregnancy, and many are not suitable for long-term

<!-- chunk -->

### use. They also have the same general contraindications as the other two classes

<!-- chunk -->

### of bitters.

# ACRID HERBS

<!-- chunk -->

### Acrid herbs are characterized by a bitter, nasty, burning taste that is much like

<!-- chunk -->

### the taste of throwing up bile. These herbs contain resins (like pungent herbs) and

<!-- chunk -->

### alkaloids (like alkaloidal bitters). The best examples of this taste are lobelia and

<!-- chunk -->

### kava-kava, but this characteristic is found to a lesser degree in black cohosh,

<!-- chunk -->

### skunk cabbage, and blue vervain.

<!-- chunk -->

### Acrid herbs tend to be relaxing, which means they are diffusive, opening up

<!-- chunk -->

### the flow of blood, lymph, and energy. They may also be cooling and drying.

<!-- chunk -->

### Their primary action tends to be antispasmodic, which means they relax cramps.

<!-- chunk -->

### They are used to relieve what are known as wind disorders in some traditional

<!-- chunk -->

### systems of medicine. These are disorders that involve alternating symptoms like

<!-- chunk -->

### fever and chills or diarrhea and constipation. Pains that migrate from one part of

<!-- chunk -->

### the body to another are also part of the pattern of wind disorders. Acrid herbs

<!-- chunk -->

### will often induce vomiting in large doses, and large doses or long-term use may

<!-- chunk -->

### adversely affect the nerves.

# ASTRINGENT HERBS

<!-- chunk -->

### Astringent herbs are herbs that contain tannins. Tannic acid gives plants a

<!-- chunk -->

### slightly bitter taste and produces a drying, slightly puckering sensation in the

<!-- chunk -->

### mouth. Green tea is astringent. Other astringent herbs include white oak bark,

<!-- chunk -->

### uva ursi, and sage.

<!-- chunk -->

### Astringent herbs constrict and dry tissues. They are used to arrest excessive

<!-- chunk -->

### secretions, tighten loose tissue, reduce swelling, and help blood coagulate. They

<!-- chunk -->

### are antivenomous when applied topically to bites and stings. Internally, they

<!-- chunk -->

### slow intestinal peristalsis (counteracting loose, watery stool) and tone up

<!-- chunk -->

### intestinal membranes.

<!-- chunk -->

### Because they tend to inhibit digestive secretions, and may interfere with

<!-- chunk -->

### mineral absorption, astringents are best taken between meals. Large doses can

<!-- chunk -->

### cause constipation, and long-term use can be irritating to the skin and mucous

<!-- chunk -->

### membranes.

# SOUR HERBS

<!-- chunk -->

### Many berries and fruits have a sour taste due to the presence of various fruit

<!-- chunk -->

### acids (citric, malic, and ascorbic acid), which are accompanied by flavonoids.

<!-- chunk -->

### Flavonoids are antioxidant and fever reducing.

<!-- chunk -->

### Sour herbs are cooling and nourishing. They may be balancing, slightly

<!-- chunk -->

### moistening, or slightly drying. They are used to reduce tissue inflammation and

<!-- chunk -->

### irritation and reduce free radical damage (thought to cause aging and

<!-- chunk -->

### degenerative disease). They can strengthen capillary integrity and tone weak

<!-- chunk -->

### tissues. Sour herbs are considered good for the liver and eyes, two organs that

<!-- chunk -->

### use more antioxidants than any other organs. They are safe foods and have no

<!-- chunk -->

### contraindications.

# SALTY HERBS

<!-- chunk -->

### The salty taste in plants isn’t like the taste of table salt. It’s a more subtle flavor

<!-- chunk -->

### that is sort of a grassy or green taste. Think of the flavor of celery or spinach.

<!-- chunk -->

### The subtle salty flavor in these foods is due to the presence of mineral salts:

<!-- chunk -->

### Magnesium, potassium, sodium, and calcium. Salty herbs are green herbs like

<!-- chunk -->

### alfalfa, mullein, and seaweeds.

<!-- chunk -->

### Salty herbs are balancing and nourishing. They can moisten dry tissues and

<!-- chunk -->

### can dry damp tissues. They are nutritive because they supply minerals that help

<!-- chunk -->

### to tone and heal tissues. They clear the lymphatics, promote lymph flow, loosen

<!-- chunk -->

### mucus, and soften swollen lymph nodes. Many salty herbs are nonirritating

<!-- chunk -->

### diuretics that nourish and support kidney function. They are generally mild in

<!-- chunk -->

### action and have no contraindications.

# SWEET HERBS

<!-- chunk -->

### Sweet herbs aren’t sweet in the same way that sugar or honey is sweet. It’s more

<!-- chunk -->

### like the sweetness of a bar of dark chocolate. This sweetness is due to the

<!-- chunk -->

### presence of polysaccharides or saponins. Obvious examples of sweet herbs

<!-- chunk -->

### include licorice and stevia, and many tonic and adaptogenic remedies such as

<!-- chunk -->

### American or Korean ginseng, codonopsis, and astragalus are sweet as well.

<!-- chunk -->

### Sweet herbs tend to be moistening and neutral, but they may be slightly

<!-- chunk -->

### warming or cooling. Sweet herbs are used to build up weakened conditions,

<!-- chunk -->

### counteract wasting, strengthen glands, and replenish energy reserves. They

<!-- chunk -->

### counteract dryness and aging in tissues and often act as immune tonics to either

<!-- chunk -->

### stimulate or balance immune functions.

<!-- chunk -->

### Most sweet herbs are very benign and suitable for long-term use in small

<!-- chunk -->

### doses. Larger doses can overstimulate the body and are abused in the same way

<!-- chunk -->

### as stimulants like coffee, especially when used by younger people. They often

<!-- chunk -->

### work better as part of a formula than taken as single remedies.

# MUCILANT HERBS

<!-- chunk -->

### Most books call these herbs mucilaginous or demulcent herbs, but we use the

<!-- chunk -->

### word “mucilant.” Mucilants have a bland or slightly sweet taste, but their most

<!-- chunk -->

### important distinguishing feature is their texture. When moistened, they have a

<!-- chunk -->

### slippery, slimy texture. This is due to water-loving polysaccharides or

<!-- chunk -->

### mucopolysaccharides, such as gums, mucilage, or pectin. They may also contain

<!-- chunk -->

### glycosaminoglycans.

<!-- chunk -->

### Okra is a mucilant. Herbal examples include aloe vera, slippery elm, and kelp.

<!-- chunk -->

### Mucilants are moistening, cooling, and nourishing. They are used to soothe

<!-- chunk -->

### tissues that are hot, red, dry, and irritated. Taken internally, they add water-

<!-- chunk -->

### soluble fiber to the stool; they are bulk laxatives when they are taken with plenty

<!-- chunk -->

### of water. They can help to arrest diarrhea, too. Mucilants feed and support

<!-- chunk -->

### friendly gut bacteria and promote general intestinal health. They absorb bile

<!-- chunk -->

### from the gallbladder and liver to help reduce cholesterol and remove toxins from

<!-- chunk -->

### the body. Mucilants protect mucous membranes and are applied topically as

<!-- chunk -->

### poultices to soothe irritated or damaged skin and promote healing.

<!-- chunk -->

### Because they are absorbent, mucilants should be taken apart from nutrients

<!-- chunk -->

### and medications. Excessive use can slow down and cool gastrointestinal

<!-- chunk -->

### function, but this can easily be counteracted by adding a small amount of an

<!-- chunk -->

### aromatic or pungent herb. Mucilants must be taken with plenty of water in order

<!-- chunk -->

### to work properly.

# OILY HERBS

<!-- chunk -->

### Oily herbs, mostly seeds, have an oily taste and texture due to the presence of

<!-- chunk -->

### fatty acids. Oily herbs include flaxseed, evening primrose seed, and coconut.

<!-- chunk -->

### Oily herbs are nourishing and cooling. They provide the body with fatty acids

<!-- chunk -->

### that are used for energy production and immune, nerve and glandular function.

<!-- chunk -->

### (Borage and evening primrose oils are marketed as remedies to influence

<!-- chunk -->

### prostaglandin function.) Oily herbs moisten dry tissues and promote tissue

<!-- chunk -->

### flexibility. Some are mild laxatives, lubricating the stool for easier elimination.

<!-- chunk -->

### Oily herbs have no real contraindications.

# THE SIX TISSUE STATES

<!-- chunk -->

### The six tissue states model originated with clinical herbalist Matthew Wood. The

<!-- chunk -->

### six tissue terrains refer to the tissues of the body and complement the basic

<!-- chunk -->

### energetics of herbs we introduced in the beginning of this chapter. Tissue terrain

<!-- chunk -->

### imbalances may be systemic or tissue- or organ-specific. Identifying terrain

<!-- chunk -->

### states and using remedies that restore balance to the tissues will achieve far

<!-- chunk -->

### better results than trying to match herbs with diseases.

<!-- chunk -->

### The tissue states include three basic tissue qualities that are divided into

<!-- chunk -->

### opposing imbalances. The first of these tissue factors is the metabolic rate, which

<!-- chunk -->

### is how fast or slow the tissues generate energy. Tissues can be overactive

<!-- chunk -->

### (hyperactive or hyperfunctioning) or they can be underactive (hypoactive or

<!-- chunk -->

### hypofunctioning). These correspond to the qualities of heat (overactive) and cold

<!-- chunk -->

### (underactive). We call the hyperactive state irritation, and the hypoactive state

<!-- chunk -->

### depression.

<!-- chunk -->

### The second tissue quality is density, which relates to the ratio of solid

<!-- chunk -->

### (mineral) elements to fluid elements (water and fats). When fluids exceed solids,

<!-- chunk -->

### the tissues are in a state of stagnation, as in a swamp. When solids outbalance

<!-- chunk -->

### fluids, the tissues get hard and dry, a state that is referred to as atrophy. These

<!-- chunk -->

### two tissue states are also called dampness (stagnation) and dryness (atrophy).

<!-- chunk -->

### The third tissue quality is tension. This has to do with the tone of the tissues in

<!-- chunk -->

### general, and specifically with muscle tone. Excess tone results in constriction,

<!-- chunk -->

### and a lack of tone results in an atonic state of excess relaxation.

<!-- chunk -->

### These six basic imbalances—irritation (heat), depression (cold), stagnation

<!-- chunk -->

### (dampness), atrophy (dryness), constriction (tension), and relaxation (atony)—

<!-- chunk -->

### can occur in isolation or in combination (such as irritation and constriction or

<!-- chunk -->

### dampness and depression). Start with the basic imbalances and eventually you

<!-- chunk -->

### will be able to identify the more complex mixtures of imbalance.

# ENERGY PRODUCTION

<!-- chunk -->

## Irritation and depression have to do with metabolism and energy generation. In

<!-- chunk -->

### irritation energy production is excessive; in depression it is deficient.

<!-- chunk -->

## Irritation has a strong correlation to oxidation, inflammation, and fever,

<!-- chunk -->

### which is why it is often referred to as “heat” in traditional herbalism. Tissues that

<!-- chunk -->

### are irritated are red and warm to the touch. Irritation is often accompanied by

<!-- chunk -->

### sharp pains. A red tongue, a rapid pulse, a ruddy (or reddish) complexion, and

<!-- chunk -->

### hyperactivity are also signs of heat or irritation.

<!-- chunk -->

### Irritation can be caused by chemical, infectious, or metabolic irritants. Tissues

<!-- chunk -->

### in the body increase their production of energy to try to overpower the irritant.

<!-- chunk -->

### Irritation is the mechanism by which cells initiate the healing process through

<!-- chunk -->

### inflammation. Part of the job of the inflammatory response is to destroy

<!-- chunk -->

### damaged cells and draw immune and stem cells to the area for tissue repair.

<!-- chunk -->

### Acute irritation isn’t a bad thing; it’s when that irritation becomes chronic that

<!-- chunk -->

### problems arise.

<!-- chunk -->

### Irritation is balanced with cooling and moistening herbs. Sour herbs are the

<!-- chunk -->

### primary remedy for reducing irritation, but mucilants, oily herbs, sweet tonics,

<!-- chunk -->

### and some bitters are also cooling to irritated tissues.

<!-- chunk -->

## Depression is the opposite of irritation. Tissues that are depressed are cool to

<!-- chunk -->

### the touch and pale, which is why depression is often referred to in traditional

<!-- chunk -->

### herbalism as “cold.” If pain is present, it tends to be dull and achy. Other signs of

<!-- chunk -->

### tissue depression are hypoactive function, a pale complexion, a pale tongue, and

<!-- chunk -->

### a slow pulse rate.

<!-- chunk -->

### Tissue depression is tricky to treat because it can be mimicked by false cold

<!-- chunk -->

### caused by low thyroid function and anemia. Infection in depressed tissues can

<!-- chunk -->

### also give rise to false heat. True tissue depression presents with a general feeling

<!-- chunk -->

### of fatigue, a pale or dark-purple tongue, and a slow pulse rate.

<!-- chunk -->

### Tissue depression is balanced herbally with warming remedies. Aromatic and

<!-- chunk -->

### pungent herbs are the primary category used here, but warming fragrant bitters

<!-- chunk -->

### may also be helpful.

<!-- chunk -->

### Pay attention to the energetics of the condition and use remedies with the

<!-- chunk -->

### appropriate properties. Treating hot (irritated) conditions with warming herbs, or

<!-- chunk -->

### cold (depressed) conditions with cooling herbs, will not balance the body.

# MINERALS AND FLUIDS

<!-- chunk -->

## Stagnation and atrophy have to do with the balance between the solid (mineral)

<!-- chunk -->

### components of the body and the fluid (water and fat) components in the body. In

<!-- chunk -->

### stagnation, there is too much fluid with insufficient minerals to move it. In

<!-- chunk -->

### atrophy, the mineral content is too high and there is not enough fluid to keep it in

<!-- chunk -->

### solution.

<!-- chunk -->

## In stagnation, fluid accumulates in tissues. This can take the form of edema,

<!-- chunk -->

### swollen lymph nodes, and sluggish flow of body fluids. In traditional herbalism,

<!-- chunk -->

### stagnation is often called “dampness.” Tissues that have stagnation are soft and

<!-- chunk -->

### spongy or hard and swollen to the touch. The tongue is pale and damp and the

<!-- chunk -->

### pulse feels congested or slippery.

<!-- chunk -->

### Stagnation was called torpor by the Eclectic physicians of the mid-1800s and

<!-- chunk -->

### early 1900s. Herbs that relieved torpor were called alteratives or blood purifiers.

<!-- chunk -->

### Alteratives alter the extracellular fluid around the cells by stimulating immunity

<!-- chunk -->

### or improving blood and lymph flow. Bitter herbs break up stagnation. Aromatic,

<!-- chunk -->

### pungent, and astringent herbs help to dry up the dampness of stagnation.

<!-- chunk -->

## Atrophy is seen when tissues become hard and brittle. When tissues are

<!-- chunk -->

### deficient in good fats and water, they become hard and inflexible or brittle.

<!-- chunk -->

### Traditional herbalism refers to this tissue state as “dryness.” Many of the

<!-- chunk -->

### diseases of aging are dry, including bone spurs, arterial plaque, the stiffness of

<!-- chunk -->

### arthritis, and the loss of elasticity in the lungs in emphysema. Dry, wrinkled skin

<!-- chunk -->

### associated with aging and the brittle bones of osteoporosis are examples of

<!-- chunk -->

### atrophy. The tongue tends to be dry and withered, and the pulse becomes thin

<!-- chunk -->

### and weak.

<!-- chunk -->

### Herbs used to treat conditions of atrophy are sometimes called tonics, and

<!-- chunk -->

### they help to revitalize people when they become weak or sickly. Sweet,

<!-- chunk -->

### mucilant, and oily herbs help to balance atrophy.

<!-- chunk -->

### Drying herbs will not help atrophy, and moistening herbs will not help

<!-- chunk -->

### stagnation. There are neutral remedies that can be used in atrophy or stagnation

<!-- chunk -->

### to balance the solids and liquids within the body. We refer to them as balancing.

# TISSUE TONE

<!-- chunk -->

### The final pair of opposing imbalances has to do with muscle tension or tone.

<!-- chunk -->

### Muscles control the flow of energy and fluids in the body. When muscles tense,

<!-- chunk -->

### flow is reduced or obstructed. When muscle tone becomes too relaxed or tissues

<!-- chunk -->

### are damaged, fluids can drain or leak from tissues. In constriction, muscles are

<!-- chunk -->

### tight, and relaxation occurs when muscles are relaxed or tissues are damaged and

<!-- chunk -->

### leaking.

<!-- chunk -->

## Constriction typically happens because muscles get tired from overuse.

<!-- chunk -->

### Muscles expend energy when they contract, and rebuild energy when they relax.

<!-- chunk -->

### When muscles fatigue, either due to excessive use or to nutritional deficiencies,

<!-- chunk -->

### they spasm. This can cause sharp pain and constricted movement. Constriction

<!-- chunk -->

### occurs in some cases of high blood pressure, tension headaches, asthma attacks,

<!-- chunk -->

### and a spastic colon.

<!-- chunk -->

### Constriction can periodically relax, causing the dam to break and excessive

<!-- chunk -->

### flow or secretion to occur. Alternating diarrhea and constipation, alternating

<!-- chunk -->

### fever and chills, and migrating pains are examples of this. These are called wind

<!-- chunk -->

### disorders in some traditional systems of medicine.

<!-- chunk -->

### Antispasmodic herbs are used in conditions of constriction. Antispasmodics

<!-- chunk -->

### are primarily acrid in nature, but some are aromatic, relaxing nervines.

<!-- chunk -->

## Relaxation occurs when tissues are unable to hold fluids due to damage or

<!-- chunk -->

### loss of muscle tone. Relaxation occurs in diarrhea, leaky gut, excessive mucus

<!-- chunk -->

### production, bleeding, urinary incontinence, and excessive sweating. Astringent

<!-- chunk -->

### herbs counteract relaxation.

<!-- chunk -->

### You can learn more about the tissue states in Matthew Wood’s The Practice of

<!-- chunk -->

### Traditional Western Herbalism and in his two-volume work The Earthwise

<!-- chunk -->

### Herbal.

# GETTING THE RESULTS YOU WANT

<!-- chunk -->

### To get good results with any health care, you need a correct diagnosis or

<!-- chunk -->

### assessment, and you need to select the right remedy, which includes

<!-- chunk -->

### administering it in the correct way and at an effective dose.

# STEP ONE: GET A CORRECT ASSESSMENT

<!-- chunk -->

### The rising interest in holistic health has led to a huge increase in the number of

<!-- chunk -->

### people using herbal medicine. This use is divorced from traditional systems of

<!-- chunk -->

### diagnosis. Western medical diagnosis is useful, but it is often centered around

<!-- chunk -->

### symptomatic relief rather than addressing the root cause for true healing.

<!-- chunk -->

### People who use herbs as symptom-relieving remedies will often find

<!-- chunk -->

### themselves disappointed with the results. Herbs are not isolated chemical

<!-- chunk -->

### compounds or magic bullets, and most herbs don’t target specific biochemical

<!-- chunk -->

### reactions to create rapid changes in symptoms like pharmaceuticals do. Herbs,

<!-- chunk -->

### like foods, contain thousands of chemical compounds that interact with the body

<!-- chunk -->

### in extremely complex ways. There are herbs that do act in the body in strong

<!-- chunk -->

### ways, including some toxic botanicals, but even these remedies have more

<!-- chunk -->

### complex actions than the singular chemical compounds used in modern

<!-- chunk -->

### medicine.

<!-- chunk -->

### The good news is that this makes them relatively free of side effects. The bad

<!-- chunk -->

### news is that herbs aren’t very good at relieving symptoms, which actually isn’t

<!-- chunk -->

### so bad when you think about it. Herbs bring about balance in the body, as we’ve

<!-- chunk -->

### just explained in our discussion of basic herbal categories and biological terrain.

<!-- chunk -->

### Herbs are best used in conjunction with diet and lifestyle changes that address

<!-- chunk -->

### underlying causes of disease.

<!-- chunk -->

### This leads us to more good news. All traditional systems of medicine, such as

<!-- chunk -->

### traditional Chinese medicine (TCM), Ayurvedic medicine from India, indigenous

<!-- chunk -->

### traditions of medicine, and even traditional Western herbalism, have assessment

<!-- chunk -->

### systems that were designed to identify broad, underlying patterns of imbalance.

<!-- chunk -->

### This means that their diagnostic systems were matched to the way herbs work. A

<!-- chunk -->

### well-trained herbalist will understand these concepts and can help you figure out

<!-- chunk -->

### what herbal remedies will work best for your situation.

<!-- chunk -->

### The primary goal of an herbalist (or any other well-trained natural healer) is to

<!-- chunk -->

### look at each person’s health problems through a wide lens. Herbalists usually

<!-- chunk -->

### spend more than an hour talking with each client, looking at their overall health

<!-- chunk -->

### and health history, and examining their diet and lifestyle and their mental and

<!-- chunk -->

### emotional state. Identifying the underlying imbalances that are causing a

<!-- chunk -->

### person’s health problems is a complicated and time-consuming process, but it is

<!-- chunk -->

### essential to getting good results.

<!-- chunk -->

### There is no specific herbal remedy for chronic fatigue. This is because fatigue

<!-- chunk -->

### has many causes, which could include problems like stress, emotional

<!-- chunk -->

### depression, nutrient deficiencies, or mitochondrial dysfunction. The same

<!-- chunk -->

### principle applies to all complex health problems, including depression, anxiety,

<!-- chunk -->

### and autoimmune disorders. In order to provide anything other than symptomatic

<!-- chunk -->

### relief, the underlying causes must be determined.

<!-- chunk -->

### Many people learn to use herbs as first aid remedies or to speed recovery from

<!-- chunk -->

### ordinary self-limiting diseases. If you have a serious health issue, we

<!-- chunk -->

### recommend you consult with a professional herbalist for assistance. One way to

<!-- chunk -->

### find a good herbalist is to look for a professional member of the American

<!-- chunk -->

### Herbalists Guild (AHG). Herbalists who are registered with the AHG have

<!-- chunk -->

### passed a peer-review process to ensure their competency and character. Go to

<!-- chunk -->

### www.americanherbalistsguild.com to learn more. Findanherbalist.com lists

<!-- chunk -->

### herbalists who are registered with the AHG in addition to competent herbalists

<!-- chunk -->

### who are not registered. You can also find herbalists listed at herbrally.com.

<!-- chunk -->

### Find an herbalist you trust. The relationship between healer and patient is an

<!-- chunk -->

### important part of the healing process. Herbalists don’t have to worry about huge

<!-- chunk -->

### overheads or how many clients they see in an hour, and they devote a lot of time

<!-- chunk -->

### to developing a relationship with each client.

<!-- chunk -->

### All of this is not to suggest that you avoid medical doctors. We work with, not

<!-- chunk -->

### against, modern medicine. Although the medical system forces most doctors into

<!-- chunk -->

### a model of practice that doesn’t give them enough time to explore the root

<!-- chunk -->

### causes of a person’s health problems, medical doctors have advanced diagnostic

<!-- chunk -->

### tools and other equipment at their disposal. Serious health problems should be

<!-- chunk -->

### monitored by a competent medical practitioner.

# STEP TWO: SELECT THE PROPER REMEDY

<!-- chunk -->

### Once you have a correct assessment, it’s time to select the appropriate remedy.

<!-- chunk -->

### The right remedy may not be an herb. Herbs aren’t going to make up for a lack

<!-- chunk -->

### of sleep, dehydration, or a diet filled with processed and refined foods. Blue

<!-- chunk -->

### vervain isn’t the right remedy for anxiety caused by a deficiency of magnesium.

<!-- chunk -->

### Find the gentlest approach that will get the results you desire. Modern

<!-- chunk -->

### medicine is focused on strong, fast-acting remedies. These produce rapid

<!-- chunk -->

### changes in symptoms, but negatively impact health in the long term. People who

<!-- chunk -->

### are used to rapid results may choose a strong herb over a mild one, or may think

<!-- chunk -->

### that if a little is good, more will be better.

<!-- chunk -->

### This is not the way traditional herbalism works. Physicians in ancient

<!-- chunk -->

### Baghdad had their market license revoked if they used a strong medicine when a

<!-- chunk -->

### gentle one would suffice, a gentle medicine when food would suffice, or food

<!-- chunk -->

### when simple advice about lifestyle would suffice.

<!-- chunk -->

### The correct remedy must be administered in the right way. Many books about

<!-- chunk -->

### herbs do not provide information about dosage forms or methods of application.

<!-- chunk -->

### Swallowing a capsule of licorice root won’t help a sore throat. Licorice must

<!-- chunk -->

### coat the throat, which means consuming the powder, sipping the tea, or taking

<!-- chunk -->

### small, frequent doses of an extract.

<!-- chunk -->

### Dosage is a controversial topic among herbalists. Some herbalists use drop

<!-- chunk -->

### doses. In contrast, practitioners of traditional Chinese medicine usually

<!-- chunk -->

### administer herbs in large doses. Some people react quickly to small doses, and

<!-- chunk -->

### others need larger amounts to effect a change. Finding the dose that works for

<!-- chunk -->

### you may require some experimentation. Consult Chapter Eleven for more

<!-- chunk -->

### information about formulas and dosages.

# W

<!-- chunk -->

## CHAPTER TWO

# Herbal Preparations

<!-- chunk -->

## UNDERSTANDING THE MANY WAYS TO PREPARE AND USE

<!-- chunk -->

## HERBS

<!-- chunk -->

### hether you decide to make your own herbal medicines, or to purchase

<!-- chunk -->

### ready-made herbal products, it helps to understand the many ways herbs

<!-- chunk -->

### can be prepared and administered. Each of the following methods are

<!-- chunk -->

### appropriate for certain herbs and situations. Each has its advantages and

<!-- chunk -->

### disadvantages. So, let’s start with an overview of the subject.

# FRESH HERBS

<!-- chunk -->

### The most basic way to use herbs is to use the fresh plant material. Herbs can be

<!-- chunk -->

### harvested from the wild or grown in a garden. Some fresh herbs, like garlic,

<!-- chunk -->

### ginger, basil, and other culinary herbs, can even be purchased fresh from the

<!-- chunk -->

### grocery store. Using herbs in this manner is not unlike using fresh fruits and

<!-- chunk -->

### vegetables. We know that the fresher the produce, the more nutritional value it

<!-- chunk -->

### tends to have. The same thing is usually, but not always, true for medicinal

<!-- chunk -->

### plants. The fresher the plant material, the more medicinally potent it tends to be.

<!-- chunk -->

### There are exceptions to this. Some herbs can be too potent, or even mildly

<!-- chunk -->

### toxic, when fresh, but mellow in action with drying or aging. The laxative herb

<!-- chunk -->

### Cascara sagrada is a good example of this. It is violently emetic and cathartic

<!-- chunk -->

### when fresh, meaning the fresh bark will make a person throw up and get severe

<!-- chunk -->

### diarrhea. Cascara must be dried and aged for at least a year to mellow its action.

<!-- chunk -->

### The general rule, however, is that fresh plant material is always better.

<!-- chunk -->

### The problem is that very few herbs are available fresh all year round. So, to be

<!-- chunk -->

### able to have year-round availability, fresh plant material must be processed in

<!-- chunk -->

### some way to preserve it, either by drying the plant material, or by extracting it in

<!-- chunk -->

### some kind of medium to preserve it.

<!-- chunk -->

### You can learn more about using fresh herbs in Chapter Three.

<!-- chunk -->

### Drying is the oldest and easiest method of preparing herbs for storage. It

<!-- chunk -->

### preserves most plant constituents well, so most medicinal qualities of plants are

<!-- chunk -->

### still retained. Dried herbs can be easily stored by placing them in an airtight

<!-- chunk -->

### container and keeping them away from light, heat, and moisture, all of which

<!-- chunk -->

### break down plant material. Most dried herbs will retain their potency for at least

<!-- chunk -->

### 1–2 years, some longer than that.

<!-- chunk -->

### Generally speaking, flowers, leaves, and other delicate plant parts deteriorate

<!-- chunk -->

### faster after drying than do harder plant parts like barks and roots. Aromatic herbs

<!-- chunk -->

### deteriorate the most, as essential oils evaporate over time and are lost, so it is

<!-- chunk -->

### best to use aromatics within about 1 year. On the other hand, astringent barks

<!-- chunk -->

### and roots may be potent for 10 years or longer.

<!-- chunk -->

### Dried herbs can be used in a variety of ways. As explained below, they can be

<!-- chunk -->

### used in bulk form or made into capsules or tablets. You can learn more about

<!-- chunk -->

### drying herbs and using them in dried form in the “Harvesting and Drying Herbs”

<!-- chunk -->

### section of Chapter Three.

# DRIED HERBS

<!-- chunk -->

### Bulk dried herbs are easily obtained from a variety of suppliers in packages

<!-- chunk -->

### ranging from a couple of ounces to a pound or more. Depending on the herb, you

<!-- chunk -->

### may be able to get the dried herb in several forms, the most common being

<!-- chunk -->

### powdered, cut and sifted, and whole. The term “cut and sifted” means that the

<!-- chunk -->

### plant material has been cut into small pieces and sifted to keep the pieces at a

<!-- chunk -->

### relatively uniform size.

<!-- chunk -->

### If you are going to store the herb for a while, we recommend you get cut and

<!-- chunk -->

### sifted or whole herbs rather than powders. The more surface area that is exposed

<!-- chunk -->

### to the air, the faster the plant material tends to degrade. This is especially true for

<!-- chunk -->

### aromatic herbs, as essential oils dissipate more rapidly from powders. If you

<!-- chunk -->

### need to powder the herb later, you can do so with a good grinder.

<!-- chunk -->

### Dried herbs can be used in numerous ways. Various powdered herbs can be

<!-- chunk -->

### mixed into food and consumed or made into topical preparations like poultices.

<!-- chunk -->

### Herbal powders can also be made into capsules or tablets. Dried herbs can also

<!-- chunk -->

### be used for teas or for making various herbal extracts.

# CAPSULES

<!-- chunk -->

### Capsules have become a very popular way of taking herbs because they are an

<!-- chunk -->

### easy and convenient dosage form. Dried herbal powders are placed in gelatin

<!-- chunk -->

### capsules, so the herbal powders can be swallowed. One of the major advantages

<!-- chunk -->

### of capsules is that they are tasteless. This makes them particularly valuable for

<!-- chunk -->

### nasty-tasting, bitter or acrid, herbs. You are also getting the whole plant material,

<!-- chunk -->

### complete with the plant fibers.

<!-- chunk -->

### Most gelatin capsules are made from animal by-products, which may be an

<!-- chunk -->

### issue for vegetarians or vegans, but all-plant vegetarian capsules can be

<!-- chunk -->

### purchased, too. If you’re willing to put a little effort into it, you can even

<!-- chunk -->

### purchase the capsules and the powders and fill your own capsules.

<!-- chunk -->

### Capsules do have some drawbacks, however. First, capsules make it harder to

<!-- chunk -->

### regulate dosages, especially with stronger botanicals. When taking an alcohol

<!-- chunk -->

### tincture you can adjust the dose by drops, but it’s hard to take ¼ or ½ of a

<!-- chunk -->

### capsule. So, herbs like lobelia, black cohosh, and other strong-acting or mildly

<!-- chunk -->

### toxic botanicals are best taken in a liquid dosage form to allow more precise

<!-- chunk -->

### dosing. Conversely, if you need several teaspoons of an herbal powder (such as

<!-- chunk -->

### psyllium hulls or slippery elm) for an effective dose, that’s a lot of capsules to

<!-- chunk -->

### swallow.

<!-- chunk -->

### The inability to taste and smell the herb also makes regulating dosage more

<!-- chunk -->

### difficult. Your sense of taste and sense of smell are sentinels that are designed to

<!-- chunk -->

### help you regulate what you put into your body. Although refined foods trick

<!-- chunk -->

### these sentinels with flavoring agents, sugar, salt, and processed fats,

<!-- chunk -->

### consumption of all-natural foods is well regulated by your senses. Once you’ve

<!-- chunk -->

### eaten a certain amount of a natural food like apples or carrots, you suddenly find

<!-- chunk -->

### it’s very difficult to eat more. Your body says enough is enough.

<!-- chunk -->

### The same is true of taking an herbal tea or extract. Even if the taste is

<!-- chunk -->

### unpleasant you’ll be able to make yourself take a certain amount, but once you

<!-- chunk -->

### have enough in your system, the body resists and it’s tough to make yourself

<!-- chunk -->

### take any more. Thus, taste and smell help you regulate the precise dose your

<!-- chunk -->

### body needs, something you can’t do with capsules.

<!-- chunk -->

### There is one other fairly unknown disadvantage to using capsules (and

<!-- chunk -->

### tablets). Herbs have a primary action that works via the senses to have an

<!-- chunk -->

### immediate effect on the body via the nervous system. For instance, as soon as

<!-- chunk -->

### spicy food or pungent herbs like capsicum are detected by your taste buds, there

<!-- chunk -->

### is an almost immediate warming sensation in the body. There may be a flushing

<!-- chunk -->

### of the face and perspiration forming on your forehead. You may also notice some

<!-- chunk -->

### increased sinus drainage or mucus clearing from the lungs.

<!-- chunk -->

### These are not reactions based on digesting, absorbing, and utilizing

<!-- chunk -->

### constituents in the plant. They are nervous system reactions that occur because

<!-- chunk -->

### of the input from your senses. If you swallow those same pungent herbs in a

<!-- chunk -->

### capsule, these primary effects will not occur. You will only get the secondary

<!-- chunk -->

### effects that come after the body has absorbed the plant constituents. Secondary

<!-- chunk -->

### effects occur anywhere from 15 minutes to many hours after you take the herb.

<!-- chunk -->

### Certain benefits of herbs depend almost entirely on primary effects. For

<!-- chunk -->

### example, the taste of bitter stimulates digestive secretions, such as hydrochloric

<!-- chunk -->

### acid in the stomach. Because of this, simple bitters, like gentian, have been used

<!-- chunk -->

### as digestive tonics to improve digestive function when taken in liquid form 15–

<!-- chunk -->

### 20 minutes prior to eating. You cannot get the same response by taking gentian

<!-- chunk -->

### in a capsule.

<!-- chunk -->

### Likewise, in order to induce perspiration, diaphoretics like yarrow work best

<!-- chunk -->

### when taken as hot infusions, or by taking extracts with ample amounts of warm

<!-- chunk -->

### water. Swallowing yarrow in a capsule will not have the same effect.

<!-- chunk -->

### Primary effects are one of the reasons many herbs seem to work faster when

<!-- chunk -->

### taken in a liquid form. Another reason is that it takes time for the body to break

<!-- chunk -->

### down a capsule, rehydrate the dried powders, and absorb the plant constituents.

<!-- chunk -->

### Liquid herbs are available for immediate absorption.

<!-- chunk -->

### One way to maintain the primary effects of an herb, while still taking them in

<!-- chunk -->

### capsules, is to break open several capsules of herb powder, then dump the

<!-- chunk -->

### contents into the container with the capsules. Put the lid on the container, and

<!-- chunk -->

### rotate the container slowly in several different directions to coat the capsules

<!-- chunk -->

### with the powder. This way, when you take a capsule, you will taste a little of the

<!-- chunk -->

### herb powder so you can activate the taste receptors on your tongue and gain the

<!-- chunk -->

### benefits of the primary effects, while the majority of the herb is ingested without

<!-- chunk -->

### having to taste it.

# TABLETS

<!-- chunk -->

### Although tablets are more frequently used for nutritional supplements than they

<!-- chunk -->

### are for herbs, some herbs are available in tablet form. It is still the preferred

<!-- chunk -->

### dosage form for many patent Chinese herbal products. To make tablets, herbal

<!-- chunk -->

### powders are mixed with a substance that acts as a binder. They are then rolled or

<!-- chunk -->

### pressed to form the tablets.

<!-- chunk -->

### In China, they make round herbal tablets by putting herbal powders into a

<!-- chunk -->

### rotating drum. A syrup is then drizzled into the drum, which causes the herbal

<!-- chunk -->

### powders to stick together, forming small round “tablets.” These are then sorted

<!-- chunk -->

### by size and dried. In the United States, the powders are generally mixed together

<!-- chunk -->

### and then pressed to form oblong-shaped tablets.

<!-- chunk -->

### Pressed tablets are often coated with something to help protect them from

<!-- chunk -->

### moisture and preserve them. The coating is often a clear vegetable shellac.

<!-- chunk -->

### Capsules and tablets have pretty much the same advantages and

<!-- chunk -->

### disadvantages. Again, you can avoid the nasty tastes, but it’s harder to regulate

<!-- chunk -->

### dose. Depending on the binders and coatings used, tablets can be harder to digest

<!-- chunk -->

### than capsules. People have been known to pass tablets largely intact.

<!-- chunk -->

### Furthermore, fillers, binding agents, and coatings may not be listed on the label.

# LIQUID HERBS (EXTRACTIONS)

<!-- chunk -->

### As soon as you harvest a plant, some of its components start to break down.

<!-- chunk -->

### Because humans lack the enzyme cellulase necessary to break down the cell wall

<!-- chunk -->

### structure of plants, it could be argued that even if you are munching on a freshly

<!-- chunk -->

### picked plant you’re not getting the full medicinal activity. This is why we

<!-- chunk -->

### typically extract herbs into some form of liquid for medicine, traditionally using

<!-- chunk -->

### water to make infusions (teas) and decoctions.

<!-- chunk -->

### The liquid you use to extract the herb is called the solvent or menstruum.

<!-- chunk -->

### Menstruum is an old alchemical term for a substance that dissolves a solid. The

<!-- chunk -->

### solvents or menstruums we will discuss in this book are water, syrup (water and

<!-- chunk -->

### sugar), alcohol, glycerin, vinegar, and oil. Industrial processes used to make

<!-- chunk -->

### standardized extracts may also make use of chemical solvents like acetone.

<!-- chunk -->

### Every menstruum has particular constituents it is very effective at extracting

<!-- chunk -->

### and other constituents that it doesn’t extract well. Water is a poor solvent of

<!-- chunk -->

### resins, but a great solvent for carbohydrates; alcohol is a great solvent for many

<!-- chunk -->

### medicinal constituents, but is a poor solvent of mucilage; and glycerin by itself

<!-- chunk -->

### (without heat and water) is a poor solvent of most constituents except volatile

<!-- chunk -->

### oils.

<!-- chunk -->

### What follows is an introduction to preparations made with the solvents we’ll

<!-- chunk -->

### discuss in this book. More information about solvents and solubility can be

<!-- chunk -->

### found in Chapter Five, and instructions on how to make these various extractions

<!-- chunk -->

### can be found in Chapter Six.

# WATER EXTRACTIONS

<!-- chunk -->

### Water is the oldest extraction medium for herbs and is still one of the simplest

<!-- chunk -->

### and easiest ways to convert herbs into liquid form. There are two basic water

<!-- chunk -->

### extractions—infusions and decoctions.

<!-- chunk -->

### Infusions are also known as herbal teas or tisanes (a French term). They are

<!-- chunk -->

### usually made by pouring boiling water over an herb and allowing it to steep for a

<!-- chunk -->

### determined length of time before straining. There are also cold infusions, where

<!-- chunk -->

### you just soak the herb in water, as in making a sun tea. Infusions are generally

<!-- chunk -->

### used on more delicate plant parts like flowers and leaves. They are primarily

<!-- chunk -->

### used for aromatic and more pleasant-tasting herbs.

<!-- chunk -->

### Decoctions are different from infusions, because the herb is simmered in the

<!-- chunk -->

### water. They are made by bringing water to a boil, adding the herbs, then

<!-- chunk -->

### reducing the heat and simmering them for a determined period of time.

<!-- chunk -->

### Decoctions draw out constituents that do not release from the plant material as

<!-- chunk -->

### readily. Decoctions extract more minerals, tannins, and bitter principles than

<!-- chunk -->

### infusions.

<!-- chunk -->

### Both infusions and decoctions are inexpensive and easy to make. They are

<!-- chunk -->

### also an effective way to take most herbs. They can be stored in the refrigerator

<!-- chunk -->

### for several days and consumed as needed, and because one is sipping and

<!-- chunk -->

### actually tasting the plant, it is easy for the body’s senses to help regulate the

<!-- chunk -->

### appropriate dosage.

<!-- chunk -->

### Teas and decoctions do not work well for nasty-tasting herbs, and some

<!-- chunk -->

### constituents are not water soluble. So, they don’t work great in every situation,

<!-- chunk -->

### but they are one of the most basic of all herbal extractions.

# SYRUPS

<!-- chunk -->

### A syrup is a water-based extraction, made in the same way as a decoction, but is

<!-- chunk -->

### done with a mixture of water and a sweetener, typically raw sugar or honey. The

<!-- chunk -->

### ratio is usually 50% sugar or honey and 50% water. Syrups are a good way to

<!-- chunk -->

### cover up the taste of bitter herbs and are usually used as cold and cough

<!-- chunk -->

### remedies. They are nourishing and moistening and are an excellent dosage form

<!-- chunk -->

### for sore throats, coughs, and digestive upset.

<!-- chunk -->

### Honey has healing properties of its own and adds to the medicinal value of the

<!-- chunk -->

### syrup. However, honey should not be used for children under the age of one.

<!-- chunk -->

### Syrups are an excellent dosage form for children and the elderly, but their high

<!-- chunk -->

### sugar content is problematic with diabetes or other situations where sugar needs

<!-- chunk -->

### to be avoided. Syrups also have a short shelf life. Refrigerated, they can last for

<!-- chunk -->

### about one month. However, the shelf life of a syrup can be extended by adding

<!-- chunk -->

### alcohol.

# ALCOHOL EXTRACTIONS (TINCTURES)

<!-- chunk -->

### Alcohol is an excellent medium for extracting herbs, and alcohol extracts or

<!-- chunk -->

### tinctures are the most popular dosage form used by professional herbalists.

<!-- chunk -->

### Alcohol is an excellent solvent for most herb constituents and can be used to

<!-- chunk -->

### make tinctures from either fresh or dried plants. Alcohol preparations have the

<!-- chunk -->

### longest shelf life of any herbal extract, so they can be stored for years, in most

<!-- chunk -->

### cases, without losing potency.

<!-- chunk -->

### Alcohol extracts are great for more potent or potentially toxic botanicals, as

<!-- chunk -->

### dosages can be regulated by the drop. Taking herbs as liquids allows the senses

<!-- chunk -->

### to regulate the dose via the taste. It also rapidly delivers herbal constituents into

<!-- chunk -->

### the bloodstream, making tinctures a fast-acting dosage form. Alcohol tinctures

<!-- chunk -->

### are also a great dosage form for applying herbs topically.

<!-- chunk -->

### There are a few drawbacks to alcohol extracts. In large doses, alcohol is a

<!-- chunk -->

### toxin to the body. However, the amount of alcohol in a dose of tincture is fairly

<!-- chunk -->

### small. If you assume a dose of 1 milliliter with an average alcohol content of

<!-- chunk -->

### 50%, that’s only ½ milliliter of alcohol per dose. In one glass of commercial

<!-- chunk -->

### orange juice there is as much as 1½ milliliters of naturally occurring alcohol.

<!-- chunk -->

### Because we are exposed to small amounts of alcohol in foods, the liver has

<!-- chunk -->

### pathways to detoxify alcohol and turn it into sugar. We also recommend avoiding

<!-- chunk -->

### alcohol preparations with children under two. In acute situations or emergencies,

<!-- chunk -->

### we have used alcohol preparations with young children, but it is best to remove

<!-- chunk -->

### some of the alcohol before administering the herb. You can do this by bringing

<!-- chunk -->

### an ounce of water to a boil. Remove it from the heat and add the alcohol extract.

<!-- chunk -->

### Letting this stand for about 5 minutes allows the heat to evaporate about 15% of

<!-- chunk -->

### the alcohol. It takes almost 4 hours of simmering alcohol in water to remove all

<!-- chunk -->

### of the alcohol. Adding hot water, or simply diluting tinctures in water, makes

<!-- chunk -->

### them more appropriate for use with young children.

<!-- chunk -->

### For many herbs, tinctures are the preferred preparation. However, some

<!-- chunk -->

### people’s religious beliefs prohibit the use of water, and tinctures aren’t

<!-- chunk -->

### recommended for children, or for recovering alcoholics or people with liver

<!-- chunk -->

### disorders.

<!-- chunk -->

### Alcohol doesn’t extract all constituents, and some herbs are much better in

<!-- chunk -->

### other menstruums.

# GLYCERIN EXTRACTIONS (GLYCERITES)

<!-- chunk -->

### Glycerin is used less frequently than alcohol as an extraction medium, but it is

<!-- chunk -->

### gaining in popularity. Glycerin is a semiclear, nontoxic, sweet-tasting liquid

<!-- chunk -->

### produced from vegetable or animal fats. Fats are composed of fatty acids and

<!-- chunk -->

### glycerin (also known as glycerol), and our digestive process breaks fats down

<!-- chunk -->

### into these two components. This means glycerin is something our body

<!-- chunk -->

### processes every time we eat fats.

<!-- chunk -->

### There are both advantages and disadvantages to using glycerin instead of

<!-- chunk -->

### alcohol to make herbal extracts. One advantage is that glycerin is nontoxic and

<!-- chunk -->

### may be used by young children, alcoholics, and other people who can’t use

<!-- chunk -->

### alcohol. A disadvantage is that glycerites are generally not as potent as tinctures,

<!-- chunk -->

### so larger doses are required.

<!-- chunk -->

### Another advantage of glycerin is that it tastes sweet, so it can be used to mask

<!-- chunk -->

### the bitter taste of many herbs. In this sense, a glycerite is similar to a syrup;

<!-- chunk -->

### however, unlike syrups, glycerites do not spike blood sugar levels in most

<!-- chunk -->

### people. Glycerin is not a sugar and is metabolized via different pathways, so it

<!-- chunk -->

### doesn’t appear to raise blood sugar levels in healthy people. Some research

<!-- chunk -->

### indicated that diabetics, however, may still need to be cautious when using

<!-- chunk -->

### glycerites.

<!-- chunk -->

### Alcohol is a better preservative than glycerin and is also less expensive.

<!-- chunk -->

### However, glycerites have a reasonably long shelf life. We have extensive

<!-- chunk -->

### experience in making glycerites and have found that properly made glycerites

<!-- chunk -->

### have a shelf life of at least 3 years. We’ve even had glycerites that were still

<!-- chunk -->

### effective after 10 years or more.

<!-- chunk -->

### A disadvantage of glycerin over alcohol is that heat must be used in making

<!-- chunk -->

### glycerites. Without heat, glycerin is a poor solvent. It is also more difficult to

<!-- chunk -->

### make fresh plant glycerites. So, plant compounds damaged by heat or drying will

<!-- chunk -->

### not be effectively extracted using glycerin.

<!-- chunk -->

### Glycerin has some therapeutic properties of its own. It is antifungal and

<!-- chunk -->

### antimicrobial. It is also soothing and emollient, so glycerites are very good for

<!-- chunk -->

### applications where tissues need to be soothed and moisturized. Glycerin will not

<!-- chunk -->

### extract resins or oils, so use alcohol to extract resinous or oily herbs. It is also

<!-- chunk -->

### difficult, if not impossible, to make a glycerite preparation of most mucilaginous

<!-- chunk -->

### herbs, but these don’t extract in alcohol either.

# HERBAL VINEGARS OR ACID TINCTURES

<!-- chunk -->

### Vinegar is not widely used as an extraction medium, but it is useful in some

<!-- chunk -->

### applications. An herbal extract made with vinegar is usually known as an herbal

<!-- chunk -->

### vinegar or acid tincture. This method is most commonly used with culinary

<!-- chunk -->

### herbs for use in cooking or as a salad dressing, although some medicinal plants

<!-- chunk -->

### have also been made into acid tinctures.

<!-- chunk -->

### Vinegar preparations are generally not as therapeutically strong as alcohol or

<!-- chunk -->

### glycerin preparations, as vinegar is not a great solvent for many herbal

<!-- chunk -->

### constituents. It also has a shorter shelf life, and the flavor of vinegar extracts is

<!-- chunk -->

### very disagreeable to some people. Others love the taste of vinegar and may find

<!-- chunk -->

### this a very appealing extraction medium.

<!-- chunk -->

### There are two applications where vinegar is very good. The first is in

<!-- chunk -->

### extracting minerals from herbs that contain calcium and other alkaline minerals.

<!-- chunk -->

### The second is for extracting aromatic and pungent herbs, as in making Fire

<!-- chunk -->

### Cider.

# OIL EXTRACTIONS

<!-- chunk -->

### Some herbs may be extracted in some kind of oil or fat, such as olive oil,

<!-- chunk -->

### coconut oil, or lard. Oil extraction is done to create remedies for topical

<!-- chunk -->

### application or to be used as a base for making salves and ointments.

<!-- chunk -->

### Oil is a very poor solvent for most herbal constituents, which is why some

<!-- chunk -->

### formulas for making ointments and salves begin with a water- or alcohol-based

<!-- chunk -->

### extraction to which oil is later added. There are, however, some plants that

<!-- chunk -->

### extract well in oil, such as St. John’s wort flowers and mullein flowers.

<!-- chunk -->

### Herbal oils are very soothing, softening, and emollient, making them good for

<!-- chunk -->

### minor skin problems such as abrasions, burns, rashes, and dry skin. They should

<!-- chunk -->

### not be used on new cuts or wounds, however, because the oil can trap bacteria in

<!-- chunk -->

### the wound and help the infection to spread.

# SPECIALIZED PREPARATIONS

<!-- chunk -->

### In addition to the basic methods for preparing herbs that we’ve listed above,

<!-- chunk -->

### there are some additional, more specialized preparations of which you should be

<!-- chunk -->

### aware. Most of these preparations are industrial applications beyond what the

<!-- chunk -->

### average person could make at home. Nevertheless, you should be aware of these

<!-- chunk -->

### preparations and their uses.

# LIQUID CAPSULES

<!-- chunk -->

### An interesting new technology in making herbal products is to put herbal

<!-- chunk -->

### extracts into softgel capsules. This dosage form provides some of the benefits of

<!-- chunk -->

### capsules combined with some of the benefits of liquid extracts. These products

<!-- chunk -->

### can be very effective, but they are more costly than capsules or extracts. At this

<!-- chunk -->

### point, only a few herbs are available in this form, and it is not a dosage form you

<!-- chunk -->

### can make at home.

# STANDARDIZED EXTRACTS

<!-- chunk -->

### Quality herbal products have always been standardized, meaning that the way

<!-- chunk -->

### they were produced was consistent; that is, made according to some standard

<!-- chunk -->

### manufacturing process. Herbal products that were part of the United States

<!-- chunk -->

### Pharmacopoeia in times past were manufactured to USP standards. This

<!-- chunk -->

### designation still appears on some over-the-counter products at the drugstore.

<!-- chunk -->

### Standardization meant that the material was harvested correctly and verified

<!-- chunk -->

### as being the correct plant part. The raw material also met specific quality control

<!-- chunk -->

### standards. The material was then extracted using the same proportions of herbal

<!-- chunk -->

### material and menstruum each time. The tincturing or processing time was also

<!-- chunk -->

### standardized to a specific period of time.

<!-- chunk -->

### Today, the term “standardized” usually has a very different meaning. Modern

<!-- chunk -->

### standardization started in 1992 as a result of laws in Germany that required

<!-- chunk -->

### manufacturers to standardize their products to create a guaranteed potency of a

<!-- chunk -->

### particular constituent. This form of standardization grows out of the orthodox

<!-- chunk -->

### scientific view that herbs owe their efficacy to specific chemical compounds,

<!-- chunk -->

### usually referred to as “active constituents.”

<!-- chunk -->

### Many people think that the standardization movement is a step forward for

<!-- chunk -->

### herbal medicine, but the people who tend to be most resistant to it are

<!-- chunk -->

### professional clinical herbalists. These people, who have the most practical

<!-- chunk -->

### experience with herbs, often find that these standardized extracts don’t work as

<!-- chunk -->

### well as whole herbs. In fact, standardization flies in the face of traditional herbal

<!-- chunk -->

### wisdom, which says that the whole is greater than the sum of the parts.

<!-- chunk -->

### Whole plants contain thousands of chemical compounds put together in

<!-- chunk -->

### extremely complex ways. Most herbalists believe that the synergy of these

<!-- chunk -->

### compounds often creates a better effect than any single compound or group of

<!-- chunk -->

### compounds in the plants. In contrast, a standardized extract uses various solvents

<!-- chunk -->

### to isolate or at least concentrate specific compounds considered to be “active,”

<!-- chunk -->

### leaving other compounds, which may have synergistic effects, behind.

<!-- chunk -->

### So, standardized extracts are more like modern pharmaceutical drugs than

<!-- chunk -->

### whole herbal products. This does not mean they are not useful; it just means they

<!-- chunk -->

### should not be viewed as the equivalent of the actual herb. One cannot extrapolate

<!-- chunk -->

### that a single compound or group of compounds found in a plant will have all of

<!-- chunk -->

### the actions of the original herb.

<!-- chunk -->

### Examples of this type of extract include curcumin, a compound from turmeric,

<!-- chunk -->

### and berberine, an antimicrobial compound found in plants like goldenseal or

<!-- chunk -->

### Oregon grape. These types of extracts have the same relationship to medicinal

<!-- chunk -->

### herbs that vitamin and mineral supplements have to food. They are not

<!-- chunk -->

### equivalent.

<!-- chunk -->

### Standardized extracts have their place. In fact, some botanicals need to be

<!-- chunk -->

### used in this manner for proper safety and dosage. For example, all the research

<!-- chunk -->

### done on the benefits of ginkgo biloba was done on a standardized extract. The

<!-- chunk -->

### whole leaf was not used in traditional herbal medicine and does not have the

<!-- chunk -->

### benefits of the standardized extract.

<!-- chunk -->

### Standardization can be used to reduce toxicity in botanicals that may have

<!-- chunk -->

### other compounds that are undesirable. It can also make dosing of strong

<!-- chunk -->

### botanical compounds more precise, such as using the acetogenins from paw paw

<!-- chunk -->

### as an anticancer remedy.

<!-- chunk -->

### There are a number of problems with these products that the consumer should

<!-- chunk -->

### consider. First, standardization increases the cost of herbal products to the

<!-- chunk -->

### consumer, without necessarily resulting in a more effective product.

<!-- chunk -->

### Second, there is not always agreement as to which compounds should be

<!-- chunk -->

### standardized, and compounds left behind may also be therapeutically valuable.

<!-- chunk -->

### For example, a curcumin-free extract of turmeric was found to still have anti-

<!-- chunk -->

### inflammatory properties, demonstrating that it isn’t just the curcumin in the

<!-- chunk -->

### turmeric that is anti-inflammatory.

<!-- chunk -->

### Furthermore, modern standards are not created by an objective third party like

<!-- chunk -->

### the United States Pharmacopoeia, nor are they based on clinical experience from

<!-- chunk -->

### practicing herbalists. The standards are created by the pharmaceutical and

<!-- chunk -->

### nutraceutical companies, often reflecting research done on the constituents rather

<!-- chunk -->

### than the whole herbs.

<!-- chunk -->

### There is also the issue of the chemical solvents that are used to selectively

<!-- chunk -->

### extract and concentrate active constituents at the expense of others. Are traces of

<!-- chunk -->

### these compounds still present in the product?

<!-- chunk -->
