### In summary, standardized extracts create a more specific, more medicine-like

<!-- chunk -->

### action by providing a standardized level of specific plant constituents. Although

<!-- chunk -->

### this may help with dosage regulation and consistency, the product is no longer an

<!-- chunk -->

### herb. Just as an orange and a vitamin C pill are very different, a whole herb is

<!-- chunk -->

### very different from a standardized extract. The synergy of ingredients found in

<!-- chunk -->

### the original plant is lost, which in many cases means the product may not be as

<!-- chunk -->

### effective as the less-expensive whole herb.

# ESSENTIAL OILS

<!-- chunk -->

### An essential oil, also known as a volatile oil, is a concentrated extract of the

<!-- chunk -->

### volatile constituents in herbs. These are the compounds that evaporate from a

<!-- chunk -->

### plant to give it its characteristic aroma. We discuss aromatherapy in more detail

<!-- chunk -->

### in Chapter Ten, but here we will just introduce them.

<!-- chunk -->

### Like a standardized extract, an essential oil is not the same as the herb from

<!-- chunk -->

### which it comes. It is a concentrated component of the herb, but does not contain

<!-- chunk -->

### all of the other compounds found in the plant. Again, this is important to

<!-- chunk -->

### understand, as many people will attribute therapeutic properties to an essential

<!-- chunk -->

### oil that are found only in the actual herb.

<!-- chunk -->

### Essential oils are highly concentrated substances, which means they do not

<!-- chunk -->

### have the same degree of safety that a whole plant has. Essential oils can irritate

<!-- chunk -->

### sensitive skin or mucous membranes. Many are toxic and should never be used

<!-- chunk -->

### internally, and even oils that are generally recognized as safe (GRAS) may have

<!-- chunk -->

### toxic properties when ingested in larger quantities (with an essential oil, one

<!-- chunk -->

### drop is a fairly large dose).

<!-- chunk -->

### Like the taste of a plant, the aroma of essential oils has a very direct and

<!-- chunk -->

### immediate impact on body functions via the nervous system. This means that it

<!-- chunk -->

### is usually not necessary to ingest an essential oil to obtain its benefits. The safest

<!-- chunk -->

### way to use them is to smell them or dilute them and use them topically. These

<!-- chunk -->

### highly concentrated substances have a fairly long shelf life, tend to be antiseptic

<!-- chunk -->

### and healing, and are useful for emotional healing and mood alteration. Their

<!-- chunk -->

### growing popularity attests to their benefits, and to the marketing of companies

<!-- chunk -->

### selling them. Making essential oils at home requires specialized equipment, with

<!-- chunk -->

### basic setups starting at $500 or more. Many people find that the art of distilling

<!-- chunk -->

### essential oils is a fun and worthwhile investment. Some oils in the marketplace

<!-- chunk -->

### may be extracted or adulterated with chemical solvents, so it is very important to

<!-- chunk -->

### seek out both quality oils and education on how to use them safely.

<!-- chunk -->

<!-- chunk -->

### the twelve major categories of herbs. Of course each herb is unique, so for more

<!-- chunk -->

### specific information about the best dosage forms for specific single herbs,

<!-- chunk -->

### consult Chapter Thirteen.

## CHAPTER THREE

# Using Fresh Plants

<!-- chunk -->

## HARVESTING, DRYING, AND USING FRESH HERBS

<!-- chunk -->

### ature freely provides medicines that can help you solve your health

<!-- chunk -->

### problems and stay healthy. You don’t need to go to the wilderness to find

<!-- chunk -->

### these healing plants. Most common lawn and garden weeds have medicinal

<!-- chunk -->

### value. Many medicinal herbs can be found at garden nurseries, and you can plant

<!-- chunk -->

### them in your yard or grow them in pots as houseplants. There are even a few

<!-- chunk -->

### fresh medicinal herbs at your local grocery store.

<!-- chunk -->

### It isn’t necessary to harvest your own herbs in order to make your own herbal

<!-- chunk -->

### medicines, but it is fun and empowering. It deepens your connection with nature

<!-- chunk -->

### so that you start to see it as an ally, filled with living things ready to share their

<!-- chunk -->

### gifts with you.

<!-- chunk -->

### This book isn’t a gardening book or a plant identification guide, but we want

<!-- chunk -->

### to acquaint you with some of the basics you’ll need to know to obtain your own

<!-- chunk -->

### medicinal plants. We’ll start with growing them yourself, discuss harvesting

<!-- chunk -->

### them from the wild, and finish with talking about some of the ways you can use

<!-- chunk -->

### fresh plants as medicine.

# GROWING HERBS

<!-- chunk -->

### Throughout history, it was common to have a few medicinal herbs in the kitchen

<!-- chunk -->

### garden. Many herbs, especially culinary herbs, are easy to grow. If you have

<!-- chunk -->

### limited space, you can grow your herbs in a planter box or another small

<!-- chunk -->

### container.

<!-- chunk -->

### You can find basic culinary herb plants at most local nurseries, and some

<!-- chunk -->

### plants that are sold as flowers or vegetables are also medicinal. Start by looking

<!-- chunk -->

### for basil, cilantro, dill, fennel, garlic, oregano, parsley, rosemary, sage, and

<!-- chunk -->

### thyme. Mints, such as spearmint or peppermint, are usually easy to grow and

<!-- chunk -->

### spread like weeds if you don’t contain them. You can be exotic and grow orange

<!-- chunk -->

### mint, pineapple mint, chocolate mint, and ginger mint.

<!-- chunk -->

### Medicinal flowers like yarrow, roses, calendula, or chamomile can easily be

<!-- chunk -->

### planted in a garden. Herbs like borage, bee balm, catnip, and horehound are easy

<!-- chunk -->

### to grow. Searching online for herb plants and seeds will yield even more

<!-- chunk -->

### possibilities. If you have the space, get a good book on growing herbs and plant

<!-- chunk -->

### a medicinal garden for yourself.

# WILDCRAFTING HERBS

<!-- chunk -->

### Ralph Waldo Emerson said that weeds are plants “whose virtues have yet to be

<!-- chunk -->

### discovered.” If you have a yard, there are probably many medicinal plants

<!-- chunk -->

### already growing on your property, and if they are not on your property, they are

<!-- chunk -->

### probably growing around the neighborhood.

<!-- chunk -->

### Before heading out into the wilds to learn to identify medicinal plants, start by

<!-- chunk -->

### becoming acquainted with the medicinal weeds growing in your own backyard.

<!-- chunk -->

### Common medicinal weeds that may grow nearby include burdock, dandelion,

<!-- chunk -->

### red clover, plantain, mullein, and wild lettuce.

<!-- chunk -->

### Before you start harvesting anything, you need to make sure you have

<!-- chunk -->

### properly identified the plant. It is easy to get some plants confused with other

<!-- chunk -->

### plants, especially in plant families such as the carrot family. Plants should be

<!-- chunk -->

### identified by their Latin names rather than their common names, as the same

<!-- chunk -->

### common name is often given to more than one species of plant.

<!-- chunk -->

### On rare occasions, people have consumed toxic plants by accident and died

<!-- chunk -->

### from it (although usually you’ll just get very sick). If you are not absolutely sure

<!-- chunk -->

### of the plant, don’t harvest it. Look for books on local plants at your local

<!-- chunk -->

### bookstore. Most plant identification books do not contain information about how

<!-- chunk -->

### to gather and use the plant, and most herbal guides don’t have good information

<!-- chunk -->

### on identification, so you’ll want to consult both herb texts and field guides. Have

<!-- chunk -->

### several of each on hand when identifying new plants.

<!-- chunk -->

### If you want to gather any herbs other than common garden weeds or those you

<!-- chunk -->

### have grown yourself, take a series of herb walks with a skilled herbalist or take a

<!-- chunk -->

### few classes in field botany. Learn to identify the poisonous plants in your area so

<!-- chunk -->

### you do not confuse any other plant with them.

# ETHICAL WILDCRAFTING

<!-- chunk -->

### The overharvesting of certain medicinal plants has resulted in some plants

<!-- chunk -->

### becoming rare or endangered. Check with United Plant Savers

<!-- chunk -->

### (www.unitedplantsavers.org) for a list of plants that are at-risk, overharvested,

<!-- chunk -->

### and suffering from habitat loss, and do not harvest those plants.

<!-- chunk -->

### Even if the plant you want is not endangered or at-risk, it’s important to

<!-- chunk -->

### follow some simple guidelines to ensure that these plants will be there for

<!-- chunk -->

### generations to come: Gather medicinal plants only where the plants are

<!-- chunk -->

### abundant. Never take more than 10% of the plants in a given area. Always leave

<!-- chunk -->

### plenty of plants so that nature can replenish the supply.

<!-- chunk -->

### It helps to know the plant’s reproductive habits before you harvest it. A plant

<!-- chunk -->

### that reproduces from underground rhizomes should be thinned, whereas a plant

<!-- chunk -->

### that reproduces from seeds should be gathered sporadically, with flowers left to

<!-- chunk -->

### turn to seed. When possible, gather leaves, seeds, and flowers, which grow back

<!-- chunk -->

### easily, rather than barks and roots, the loss of which may kill the plant. Harvest

<!-- chunk -->

### bark with respect for the needs of the tree, and preferably from recently downed

<!-- chunk -->

### branches or prunings.

<!-- chunk -->

### When harvesting roots and bulbs, replant the crown portion of the root or

<!-- chunk -->

### rhizomes and fill in holes so you don’t leave gaping holes in the earth. When you

<!-- chunk -->

### harvest plants on a hill, leave the plants at the top of the hill untouched to help

<!-- chunk -->

### replenish the downhill slope.

<!-- chunk -->

### Many indigenous traditions teach that you should ask the plants for

<!-- chunk -->

### permission before gathering them and leave an offering in exchange for the

<!-- chunk -->

### plant. Sit with the plant in a quiet, meditative state for at least 15 minutes before

<!-- chunk -->

### harvesting it. Think about what use you want to make of the plant material, and

<!-- chunk -->

### ask the plant for permission to gather it.

<!-- chunk -->

### Native people often looked for what appeared to be the “eldest” plant in the

<!-- chunk -->

### area or plant “tribe.” They would make an offering of cornmeal or tobacco to

<!-- chunk -->

### this “grandfather” plant, asking permission to harvest some of the tribe.

<!-- chunk -->

### Even if you choose not to gather plants in these traditional ways, gather the

<!-- chunk -->

### plants in a reverent, thankful manner. Honor the fact that these living plants are

<!-- chunk -->

### giving their lives or part of themselves to help you and others heal.

# HARVESTING AND DRYING HERBS

<!-- chunk -->

### Make sure the plants you gather have not been sprayed with chemicals, and that

<!-- chunk -->

### they are not growing next to busy streets where they are exposed to exhaust

<!-- chunk -->

### fumes. Don’t harvest plants from other people’s property without their

<!-- chunk -->

### permission. If you are harvesting from government land, make sure you know

<!-- chunk -->

### what the laws and regulations are (it’s illegal to harvest plants from national

<!-- chunk -->

### parks in the United States).

<!-- chunk -->

### Harvest only one species of plant at a time to avoid confusion. Individual

<!-- chunk -->

### plant parts like leaves are easy to get mixed up. Label everything carefully as

<!-- chunk -->

### soon as you get it home.

<!-- chunk -->

### Know what you will be using the plant for before you gather it. Harvest only

<!-- chunk -->

### the part of the plant that you intend to use medicinally, and gather only as much

<!-- chunk -->

### as you need. Try not to gather from wet ground, where your footprints can

<!-- chunk -->

### damage the soil and make future growth more challenging; wait for a stretch of

<!-- chunk -->

### good weather before collecting in wetland areas. Leave the area in better shape

<!-- chunk -->

### than it was when you found it.

<!-- chunk -->

### Dry or process herbs into an extract as quickly as possible. If you have to

<!-- chunk -->

### wait, keep the plant material cool until you’re ready to dry or process it.

# HARVESTING AT THE RIGHT TIME

<!-- chunk -->

### Like fruits and vegetables, herbs have a season and time of day when they ripen

<!-- chunk -->

### and are at peak strength. Harvest herbs when they are at the peak of maturity and

<!-- chunk -->

### the concentration of active ingredients is highest.

<!-- chunk -->

### Here are some general guidelines; consult other herbal texts for more specific

<!-- chunk -->

### information. Barks are usually harvested in the spring (and in the morning)

<!-- chunk -->

### because that is when the sap is rising and the bark is most active. This is also the

<!-- chunk -->

### time to harvest young stems, buds, and leaves.

<!-- chunk -->

### Flowers are usually harvested during the day when the energy of the plant is

<!-- chunk -->

### the most yang, or outward. Flowers are best harvested shortly after they open.

<!-- chunk -->

### (Flowers that open at night should be gathered in the evening when they start to

<!-- chunk -->

### open.) Essential oils rise up the stems and concentrate in flowers in the early

<!-- chunk -->

### morning hours, then dissipate as the day wears on.

<!-- chunk -->

### As the plant dies back in the fall, the energy and nutrient stores are returned to

<!-- chunk -->

### the root. Roots, therefore, are usually gathered in the fall, and are often more

<!-- chunk -->

### potent when gathered in the evening hours when the plant is pulling its energies

<!-- chunk -->

### downward again.

# HARVESTING THE RIGHT PARTS OF AN HERB

<!-- chunk -->

### Many plants have different uses depending on the plant part. It is important to

<!-- chunk -->

### know which part of the plant you want. Although one part of the plant may be

<!-- chunk -->

### completely safe, other parts may be toxic.

<!-- chunk -->

### Elder flowers and elder berries are both antiviral. Elder flowers are used to

<!-- chunk -->

### treat topical irritations of the skin, and are used internally as a mild refrigerant to

<!-- chunk -->

### relieve fevers and reduce inflammation. Elder berries are used as a potassium-

<!-- chunk -->

### rich food, and can be added to slippery elm gruel to treat wasting conditions.

<!-- chunk -->

### Elder berries are also used to enhance tonic cardiovascular herbal blends, and

<!-- chunk -->

### have a mild decongestant effect. Elder leaves are astringent and are used

<!-- chunk -->

### primarily as a topical preparation, whereas the bark is bitter, astringent, and

<!-- chunk -->

### mildly toxic, suitable for use only by experienced practitioners.

Photo by Nicole Conner

<!-- chunk -->

### Using the wrong part of a plant can cause toxic reactions, from simple

<!-- chunk -->

### stomach upset to coma and even death. The fruit of the tomato and the tuber of

<!-- chunk -->

### the potato are edible and nutritionally dense, but the leaves and stems of these

<!-- chunk -->

### plants are toxic. The reverse can also hold true: Leafy parts of some plants are

<!-- chunk -->

### edible, whereas the fruit or seeds are toxic.

# SPECIFIC HARVESTING DIRECTIONS

<!-- chunk -->

### Here are some more detailed guidelines for harvesting various plant parts.

<!-- chunk -->

### Again, these are general guidelines. Each herb has its own unique characteristics.

<!-- chunk -->

### As you learn more about each plant, you will also discover the best time to

<!-- chunk -->

### harvest it.

<!-- chunk -->

## Flowers

<!-- chunk -->

### Harvesting flowers varies from plant to plant, because different plants flower at

<!-- chunk -->

### different times of the year. Sometimes the part used is the bud; in this case,

<!-- chunk -->

### collect the flowers just before they open. Blossoms should be harvested when

<!-- chunk -->

### they are fully open, just as they reach the peak of their strength, but before they

<!-- chunk -->

### start to turn (wilt, waste, or dry out). Shortly after this, the blossom will begin to

<!-- chunk -->

### wilt at the edges and go limp, and the vibrancy of the color will start to fade.

<!-- chunk -->

### It is usually best to harvest flowers in mid- to late morning after the dew has

<!-- chunk -->

### dried. Cut or pick them carefully. If the stems are woody, like lavender, it is

<!-- chunk -->

### easiest to use clippers and harvest the stem along with the flowers. If the stem is

<!-- chunk -->

### large or fleshy, remove the flowers from the stems before drying. Keep fresh

<!-- chunk -->

### flowers loose and in open containers so they do not congeal and mold. Remove

<!-- chunk -->

### obvious dirt, soil, and insects, and spread the flowers on a paper-lined tray or

<!-- chunk -->

### newspaper to dry. Flowers should be dried in a well-ventilated area away from

<!-- chunk -->

### sunlight and excessive heat; paper sacks suspended from the ceiling make great

<!-- chunk -->

### drying units. When the flowers are dry (when they crumble when rubbed

<!-- chunk -->

### between your fingers), store them whole in dark, airtight containers.

<!-- chunk -->

### When the plant part used is the flowering herb, leaves and stems are gathered

<!-- chunk -->

### along with the flowers. Tie bunches of stems together with string or twine, and

<!-- chunk -->

### hang them upside down to dry. Dry them away from direct sunlight and

<!-- chunk -->

### excessive heat.

<!-- chunk -->

## Leaves and Aerial Parts

<!-- chunk -->

### Leaves are usually best when harvested in the early and mid-summer when their

<!-- chunk -->

### chlorophyll and their aromatic and medicinal constituents are at their peak.

<!-- chunk -->

### Collect leaves in the morning after the dew has evaporated. As the day warms up

<!-- chunk -->

### and photosynthesis gets under way, essential oils are concentrated in the leaves

<!-- chunk -->

### and evaporate in midday heat. Harvest the leaves after some warmth has drawn

<!-- chunk -->

### up the oils but before they have been released in the heat.

<!-- chunk -->

### Gather leaves when they are tender and showing new growth, either before the

<!-- chunk -->

### flower buds appear or after seeding when new fall growth appears. Treat all

<!-- chunk -->

### leaves gently, taking care not to bruise or crush them. Pick only healthy whole

<!-- chunk -->

### leaves that are not torn, discolored, or insect infested.

<!-- chunk -->

### According to Nicholas Culpeper’s English Physician and Complete Herbal,

<!-- chunk -->

### “The leaves of such herbs as run up to seed are not so good when they are in

<!-- chunk -->

### flower as before” (p. 200). Upon flowering, the plant’s priorities change and its

<!-- chunk -->

### energy goes into reproduction. At this point the leaves can become bitter and

<!-- chunk -->

### sometimes astringent. Whether you gather the leaves before the plant flowers or

<!-- chunk -->

### after will depend on what components you are looking for.

<!-- chunk -->

### Pick the succulent leaves of sorrel, bistort, good King Henry, angelica, and all

<!-- chunk -->

### salad herbs when young. Succulent herbs are not typically harvested for drying

<!-- chunk -->

### and should be frozen in cooked food or preserved in oil or vinegar.

<!-- chunk -->

### Large leaves such as burdock and mullein can be harvested and dried

<!-- chunk -->

### individually; smaller leaves, such as mints, are best left on the stem. Gather the

<!-- chunk -->

### leaves of deciduous herbs just before flowering, and gather evergreen herbs,

<!-- chunk -->

### such as rosemary, throughout the year. If you are using all of the aerial parts,

<!-- chunk -->

### harvest when there are both flowers and seed heads on the plant. Treat all leaves

<!-- chunk -->

### gently, taking care not to bruise or crush them. Pick only healthy whole leaves

<!-- chunk -->

### that are not torn, discolored, or insect infested.

<!-- chunk -->

### There are several ways to dry leaves. Tie them in small bunches of about 8–12

<!-- chunk -->

### stems, depending on the size of the stems, and hang them upside down to dry.

<!-- chunk -->

### When the leaves are brittle to the touch, but not so dry that they turn to powder,

<!-- chunk -->

### rub them from the stem onto paper to dry, and discard the stem, if the stem is not

<!-- chunk -->

### required. Leaves can also be spread on screens or placed loosely in small paper

<!-- chunk -->

### bags and hung to dry. Dry leaves away from sunlight. Store in airtight containers

<!-- chunk -->

### when dry.

Photo by Unsplash / CC BY

<!-- chunk -->

## Seeds

<!-- chunk -->

### Seeds should be harvested late in fall before the weather turns wet or the snow

<!-- chunk -->

### starts to fall. The plant should be thoroughly dead and dried up when you harvest

<!-- chunk -->

### the seeds. Some seeds, such as poppy and oat, are harvested when they are green

<!-- chunk -->

### due to the concentration of certain medicinal alkaloids. (Note that the use of

<!-- chunk -->

### green poppy seed is illegal.)

<!-- chunk -->

### The best time of day to harvest the seeds of a plant is midmorning. Pick seeds

<!-- chunk -->

### on a warm, dry day when the seeds are fully ripe but before they have fallen off

<!-- chunk -->

### the plant. The seeds should be tan, brown, or black, not green at all, and they

<!-- chunk -->

### should be hard, with paper-dry pods.

<!-- chunk -->

### Harvest the entire seed head with about 15–25 centimeters (6–10 inches) of

<!-- chunk -->

### stalk. Hang seed heads upside down over a paper-lined tray or in a paper bag

<!-- chunk -->

### away from direct sunlight. Seeds will usually dry within 2 weeks. Keep all seeds

<!-- chunk -->

### separate and remember to label and date everything.

<!-- chunk -->

## Roots and Rhizomes

<!-- chunk -->

### Most roots are gathered in the late fall when the aerial parts of the plant have

<!-- chunk -->

### wilted and before the ground becomes too hard to dig easily. In the fall, the

<!-- chunk -->

### fluids and vitality of the plant drop into the roots or rhizomes. There are

<!-- chunk -->

### exceptions to this rule: Dandelion root, for example, should be gathered in the

<!-- chunk -->

### spring before it becomes too bitter and woody.

<!-- chunk -->

### Dig up annuals when their growing cycle has been completed at the end of the

<!-- chunk -->

### year. Pick perennial roots in their second or third year of growth or later, when

<!-- chunk -->

### the active components have developed. Because sap rises and falls with the sun,

<!-- chunk -->

### gather roots in the early morning or late afternoon while most of the potency of

<!-- chunk -->

### the plant is in the root.

<!-- chunk -->

### In most cases you should wash the roots thoroughly to remove soil and dirt.

<!-- chunk -->

### Use an old toothbrush to scrub small roots. Most roots, such as horseradish and

<!-- chunk -->

### comfrey, can be scrubbed clean and have their fibrous hairs removed. Others,

<!-- chunk -->

### such as valerian, should not be stripped of their tiny root hairs, as their precious

<!-- chunk -->

### constituents are contained in the epidermal (surface) cells. For these roots, brush

<!-- chunk -->

### the dirt off with a dry toothbrush or wash very gently.

<!-- chunk -->

### Roots can be difficult or even impossible to cut when dry, so chop large roots

<!-- chunk -->

### into small pieces while still fresh. Spread the pieces of root on a screen or a tray

<!-- chunk -->

### lined with paper, and dry in a warm place away from direct sunlight. Some

<!-- chunk -->

### succulent roots like dandelion and burdock need a bit more heat than normal to

<!-- chunk -->

### kill off small insect eggs; these do best cut into small pieces when fresh, and

<!-- chunk -->

### then dried at 150° in a food dehydrator, or in the oven on the lowest setting with

<!-- chunk -->

### the door propped open. Some roots reabsorb moisture from the air, so discard

<!-- chunk -->

### them if they become soft.

<!-- chunk -->

## Sap and Resin

<!-- chunk -->

### The best time to harvest resin from a tree is in autumn when the sap is falling.

<!-- chunk -->

### Sap is usually harvested in the spring when it is rising. In either case, make a

<!-- chunk -->

### deep incision in the bark and wait for the resin or sap to leak out. Sap can be

<!-- chunk -->

### collected by drilling a hole and collecting the sap in a cup or bucket tied to the

<!-- chunk -->

### tree. Sometimes a sizable bucket is needed: a large amount of birch sap, for

<!-- chunk -->

### example, can be collected overnight at certain times of year. If you tap (drill) a

<!-- chunk -->

### tree, place a wood cork in the hole after you’re done to prevent insects from

<!-- chunk -->

### invading the tree.

<!-- chunk -->

### You can squeeze sap from latex plants like wild lettuce or desert poppy into a

<!-- chunk -->

### container. Some saps are corrosive (including celandine and fig sap), so wear

<!-- chunk -->

### protective gloves before squeezing. To harvest aloe, slice along the center of the

<!-- chunk -->

### leaf and peel back the edges, then use the blunt edge of a knife to scrape the gel

<!-- chunk -->

### from the leaf.

<!-- chunk -->

## Fruits

<!-- chunk -->

### Harvest fruits only when they are completely ripened. This can be anywhere

<!-- chunk -->

### from summer to late fall, depending on the plant. One of the best indicators that

<!-- chunk -->

### a fruit has ripened is how easily it can be harvested from the plant. The fruit

<!-- chunk -->

### should almost fall off as it is touched, and should not have to be pulled with

<!-- chunk -->

### force from the stem that attaches it to the plant.

<!-- chunk -->

### Harvest berries and other fruits just as they become ripe, before the fruit

<!-- chunk -->

### becomes too soft to dry. Lay them out on covered screens to dry. Turn fleshy

<!-- chunk -->

### fruits frequently to help them dry evenly, and get rid of fruit that show signs of

<!-- chunk -->

### mold. A dehydrator is an excellent way to dry medicinal fruits.

<!-- chunk -->

## Bark

<!-- chunk -->

### Both outer and inner bark should be harvested in the late fall, winter, or early

<!-- chunk -->

### spring. This is when they are the most potent, and, depending on where you live,

<!-- chunk -->

### there should be no bug or pest invasion during that time of year. To minimize the

<!-- chunk -->

### damage to the tree, harvest in the autumn when the sap is falling back into the

<!-- chunk -->

### roots. As spring approaches and the fluids rise in the plant, the tree will heal any

<!-- chunk -->

### damage (including the stripped bark). For most plants this is the only time they

<!-- chunk -->

### can heal without environmental complications.

<!-- chunk -->

### Outer bark is the part of the bark that people cut into when they carve their

<!-- chunk -->

### initials in a tree. The inner bark is the thin layer underneath the hard outer bark

<!-- chunk -->

### that still adheres to the tree. Collect bark from young branches or trunks. Look

<!-- chunk -->

### for recently downed trees before harvesting bark from live ones. When working

<!-- chunk -->

### with live trees, it is best to saw off a branch and then strip it. Bark will peel off

<!-- chunk -->

### easily in wet weather. Be careful to cut carefully so the bark does not tear down

<!-- chunk -->

### the trunk, exposing the tree to insect invasion. If you are harvesting a small

<!-- chunk -->

### amount of bark, take small, lengthwise strips from one of the branches; if you

<!-- chunk -->

### cut around the circumference of the tree you will kill it. Brush off the bark to

<!-- chunk -->

### remove moss or insects, and break or cut the bark into small pieces (1–2 inches

<!-- chunk -->

### square), lay them out on a tray, and let dry.

<!-- chunk -->

### Sometimes twigs can be used instead of bark. The pruned branches of fruit

<!-- chunk -->

### trees such as peach and apple can be put to good use as herbal medicine.

<!-- chunk -->

## Bulbs

<!-- chunk -->

### Harvest bulbs after the aerial parts have wilted. Collect garlic bulbs quickly; they

<!-- chunk -->

### tend to sink downward once the leaves have wilted and may be difficult to find.

<!-- chunk -->

### Garlic and onions are examples of medicinal bulbs.

Photo by Thomas Ulrich / CC BY

# USING FRESH PLANTS

<!-- chunk -->

### Fresh plants can be used in a number of ways. You can chew the fresh herb and

<!-- chunk -->

### eat it, or add it to soups, stews, or other dishes. You can also extract fresh herbs

<!-- chunk -->

### in water (infusions and decoctions), alcohol, glycerin, or vinegar. Here are some

<!-- chunk -->

### specific preparations that can be made from fresh herbs.

# FRESH PLANT POULTICES

<!-- chunk -->

### Fresh herbs can be mashed and applied directly to the skin for burns, insect bites,

<!-- chunk -->

### bee stings, cuts, and other minor injuries. You can also chew the plant material a

<!-- chunk -->

### little, crushing it with the teeth and mixing it with saliva, to form a fresh plant

<!-- chunk -->

### poultice. (Spit poultices contain lots of unhealthy bacteria from the mouth and

<!-- chunk -->

### shouldn’t be used on broken skin.) Examples of fresh herbs that can be used as

<!-- chunk -->

### poultices include the following:

<!-- chunk -->

### ▪ Plantain (bee stings, insect bites, snake bites, burns, minor cuts and abrasions;

<!-- chunk -->

### dirty wounds containing sand, dirt, grit, or other impurities)

<!-- chunk -->

### ▪ Lily of the valley (draws out slivers and pus)

<!-- chunk -->

### ▪ Yarrow (cuts, bruises, crushed tissues, insect bites)

<!-- chunk -->

### ▪ Grindelia (bee stings and insect bites, cuts, infected wounds)

<!-- chunk -->

### ▪ Jewelweed (poison ivy)

# INSTANT PASTES AND OINTMENTS

<!-- chunk -->

### Fresh herbs can be made into pastes and ointments. Make a paste by mixing

<!-- chunk -->

### crushed fresh herbs with a little honey or glycerin. These can be eaten or used as

<!-- chunk -->

### a base for a poultice. You can make an instant ointment by mashing fresh herbs

<!-- chunk -->

### into butter, ghee, or oil.

# GREEN DRINKS

<!-- chunk -->

### Green drinks are prepared with a blender. Pour fruit juice (pineapple juice is

<!-- chunk -->

### delicious) into a blender, add fresh herbs, and blend the mixture. Strain and

<!-- chunk -->

### drink. Drink green drinks within a couple of minutes to guarantee the full

<!-- chunk -->

### potency of all of the plant’s nutrients and constituents. Plants that can be used to

<!-- chunk -->

### make green drinks include dandelion greens, plantain, parsley, wheat grass, and

<!-- chunk -->

### barley grass.

Photo by Natalie Gi / CC BY

# JUICING

<!-- chunk -->

### Herb juices are commonly made with parsley, garlic, and onions, and fruits such

<!-- chunk -->

### as lemons and limes. Juicing fresh herbs separates the plant fiber from the plant

<!-- chunk -->

### fluids. Herb juices can be made with a food processor or a juicer. The most

<!-- chunk -->

### antiquated method for juicing, still used by the more adventurous among us, is

<!-- chunk -->

### the mortar and pestle. Filter the mashed plant material through a muslin cloth

<!-- chunk -->

### and squeeze through a nylon sieve or jelly bag to obtain the juice.

<!-- chunk -->

### Juicing requires large quantities of fresh herb: A 10-liter bucket of fresh herb

<!-- chunk -->

### may yield only 100 milliliters of juice.

<!-- chunk -->

## Dehydrated Herb Juice

<!-- chunk -->

### After the juice has been expressed from the plant fiber it can be dehydrated.

<!-- chunk -->

### Dehydrating must be done with care and cleanliness to avoid microbial

<!-- chunk -->

### contamination. Dry the juice quickly, but not at such a high temperature that the

<!-- chunk -->

### nutritional factors or therapeutic constituents are degraded. Dry juices in a

<!-- chunk -->

### standard food dehydrator using fruit leather trays. If you do not use heat, you can

<!-- chunk -->

### dry juice on waxed paper or glass as well: Spread a thin layer of juice on the

<!-- chunk -->

### waxed paper or glass container and cover with a muslin cloth to prevent mold

<!-- chunk -->

### and keep flies and pests off. Place in a warm place to dry. Once the juice is

<!-- chunk -->

### thoroughly dried, scrape it off, powder it, and package. If kept in a cool, dark,

<!-- chunk -->

### dry place, this type of preparation will keep for about 3–4 months.

# RAW GARLIC

While most herbs work well when dried, there are some herbs that are best used fresh. Garlic is one of them. Garlic bulbs are available at any grocery store, and raw garlic is a good remedy to keep around the house. Garlic is often sold in capsules and extracted in oil, but allicin, an antimicrobial constituent found in raw garlic, degrades rapidly into inactive compounds, so fresh is best. There are many ways to use fresh garlic. For ear infections and earaches, cut a slice of a garlic clove and place it on the outer ear covering the ear canal. Do not put the garlic into the ear canal. If the raw garlic irritates the skin, coat it with a little olive oil before applying. For an abscessed tooth, cut a slice of garlic, coat it in olive oil, and place it next to the affected tooth to fight the infection. This will temporarily ease the pain and infection; you still need to see a dentist. Raw garlic works internally against infections in the intestines and lungs, but it has little effect on systemic infections. To get it down, chop raw garlic into small pieces and take it in a spoonful of honey. There is a formula for Garlic Lemon Aid in Chapter Twelve, as well as a formula for Fire Cider, which includes raw garlic. Photo by Steven Horne

# D

<!-- chunk -->

## CHAPTER FOUR

# Dried Herbs

<!-- chunk -->

## BULK HERBS, CAPSULES, AND TABLETS

<!-- chunk -->

### rying is one of the oldest methods of preparing herbs for storage and use,

<!-- chunk -->

### and is easy to do at home. Most herbs maintain a reasonable potency when

<!-- chunk -->

### dried, whereas some lose potency, and the strength of a few others is enhanced

<!-- chunk -->

### by drying. Dried herbs are often powdered. As they are powdered, plant material

<!-- chunk -->

### is exposed to the air, which causes more constituents to be lost. This means that

<!-- chunk -->

### powdered herbs lose potency faster than herbs that are cut and sifted or whole.

<!-- chunk -->

### Powders may be encapsulated or tableted, and both powders and bulk dried

<!-- chunk -->

### herbs can be used to make extracts and various topical preparations.

# PURCHASING DRIED HERBS

<!-- chunk -->

### When you purchase bulk herbs you depend upon the supplier to ensure that the

<!-- chunk -->

### herb was properly identified and harvested. Be discerning when you choose an

<!-- chunk -->

### herb vendor.

<!-- chunk -->

### We favor organically grown or wildcrafted herbs. Organically grown herbs are

<!-- chunk -->

### grown without chemical pesticides, herbicides, or fertilizers. Wildcrafted herbs

<!-- chunk -->

### have been gathered from the wild, so unless they are harvested near roadways or

<!-- chunk -->

### farms, they won’t have chemical pesticides, herbicides, or fertilizers.

<!-- chunk -->

### Using cultivated herbs contributes to plant conservation because doing so does

<!-- chunk -->

### not deplete wild stands of plants. On the other hand, plants that grow in their

<!-- chunk -->

### native habitat may be more potent than herbs that are cultivated. Many of the

<!-- chunk -->

### medicinal compounds in plants are produced in response to environmental stress,

<!-- chunk -->

### so a wild-grown plant may have higher concentrations of medicinal compounds

<!-- chunk -->

### than a cultivated plant.

<!-- chunk -->

### Dried herbs may be whole, sliced, cut and sifted, or powdered. As mentioned

<!-- chunk -->

### above, the more surface area that is exposed to light and air, the quicker plants

<!-- chunk -->

### deteriorate. Keep your herbs whole or cut and sifted until ready for use. A small

<!-- chunk -->

### coffee grinder will reduce most cut and sifted herbs (with the exception of very

<!-- chunk -->

### hard roots and barks) to powder, or you can use a Vitamix, as discussed in

<!-- chunk -->

### Chapter Five.

# QUALITY CONTROL ISSUES

<!-- chunk -->

### When purchasing herbs, here are the quality control issues of which you should

<!-- chunk -->

### be aware. Cheaper brands are not necessarily a good buy. Even today there is

<!-- chunk -->

### adulteration in herbal products. Some products contain the wrong plant species.

<!-- chunk -->

### Others include plant parts that are not considered medicinally valuable.

<!-- chunk -->

### Therefore, purchase from companies that ensure they use the right species and

<!-- chunk -->

### parts of the plant.

<!-- chunk -->

### Make sure that plants are being harvested at the right time of the year. Potency

<!-- chunk -->

### may also vary with the location where the plant was grown.

<!-- chunk -->

### It is also important that herbs be harvested sustainably. Avoid companies that

<!-- chunk -->

### are supporting wildcrafters who are endangering species of medicinal plants.

<!-- chunk -->

### Cleanliness of the plant material is also important. Is the herb free of dirt and

<!-- chunk -->

### insect parts? Is it also free of pesticides and herbicides, heavy metals, and other

<!-- chunk -->

### chemical contaminants? This is especially important for herbs coming from

<!-- chunk -->

### heavily polluted countries like China. Remember that just because an herb is

<!-- chunk -->

### organic doesn’t mean that it’s free of dirt or insect parts.

<!-- chunk -->

### These are all reasons why we recommend you stick to purchasing herbal

<!-- chunk -->

### products, including bulk herbs, only from reputable manufacturers. See

<!-- chunk -->

### Appendix Two for a list of suppliers we recommend.

# USING DRIED HERBS

<!-- chunk -->

### Bulk dried herbs can be used in a variety of ways. They can be extracted as

<!-- chunk -->

### discussed in Chapter Five or used in topical applications as described in Chapter

<!-- chunk -->

### Eight. They can also be encapsulated or tableted or made into herbal nut butter

<!-- chunk -->

### balls. The following are some of the dosage forms you can make using bulk

<!-- chunk -->

### dried herbs.

# CAPSULES

<!-- chunk -->

### The herb industry received a huge boon when herbs began to be put into

<!-- chunk -->

### capsules. Encapsulated herbs are easy to consume, and they are one of the most

<!-- chunk -->

### popular ways of taking herbs in the United States today. Encapsulated herbs

<!-- chunk -->

### rarely act as quickly or effectively as traditional teas and tinctures, but for some

<!-- chunk -->

### unpalatable herbs needed in high doses they are very convenient.

<!-- chunk -->

### Many capsules are made from animal by-products (gelatin) and may not be

<!-- chunk -->

### suitable for people on strict vegetarian diets, but there are an increasing number

<!-- chunk -->

### of products being made with all-vegetable capsules. Most companies that

<!-- chunk -->

### encapsulate herbs add materials like magnesium stearate to lubricate their

<!-- chunk -->

### machinery. For most people the addition of minute amounts of magnesium

<!-- chunk -->

### stearate doesn’t cause any problems, but some people with sensitive digestive

<!-- chunk -->

### tracts should avoid it. Several companies, including Pure Encapsulations and

<!-- chunk -->

### Thorne, don’t use magnesium stearate.

<!-- chunk -->

### Home capsule filling machines can be bought for around $30, or you can fill

<!-- chunk -->

### capsules by hand. To do this manually, you need a saucer or flat dish, empty

<!-- chunk -->

### capsules, and the powdered herb you want to encapsulate. Pour the powdered

<!-- chunk -->

### herb into the saucer. Separate the two halves of the capsule case and scoop the

<!-- chunk -->

### powder into both sides of the capsule. Fit the two halves of the capsule together.

<!-- chunk -->

### Store the finished capsules in a cool place in a dark glass jar or container.

<!-- chunk -->

### Different capsule sizes hold different amounts of herb powder, and machines

<!-- chunk -->

### are more efficient than hand filling. Hand filling a size 00 capsule will result in

<!-- chunk -->

### 200–250 milligrams of powdered herb per capsule. Machine-filled capsules

<!-- chunk -->

### usually hold 45 milligrams of herb powder per a standard size 00 capsule.

# TABLETS

<!-- chunk -->

### Before capsules became popular, many herbs were sold in tablet form. Herbal

<!-- chunk -->

### tablets can still be found in the marketplace. Tablets have the same advantages as

<!-- chunk -->

### capsules, but they can be harder to digest. Some tablets are made with fillers to

<!-- chunk -->

### bind the herbs together, and these ingredients aren’t always disclosed on the

<!-- chunk -->

### label.

<!-- chunk -->

### Tablets contain powdered herbs mixed with filling agents, binding agents,

<!-- chunk -->

### flowing agents, and disintegration agents. Filling agents are designed to make

<!-- chunk -->

### each tablet the exact same size. A binding agent is an ingredient that holds the

<!-- chunk -->

### tablet together. Sticky botanicals such as slippery elm and acacia gum are

<!-- chunk -->

### sometimes used as binding agents. Flowing agents are added to help the herbs go

<!-- chunk -->

### through the numerous manufacturing processes smoothly. Compounds such as

<!-- chunk -->

### magnesium stearate are often used as flowing agents. Most people won’t have

<!-- chunk -->

### issues with magnesium stearate, but some people do seem sensitive to it.

<!-- chunk -->

### Disintegration agents make the tablet dissolve once it is in the digestive

<!-- chunk -->

### system. Corn and potato starch are commonly used as disintegration agents.

<!-- chunk -->

### Cellulose compounds will make the tablet disintegrate after around 15 minutes

<!-- chunk -->

### in a digestive tract. Some mechanisms rely on the pH of the stomach and are

<!-- chunk -->

### therefore dependent on adequate stomach acid for breakdown.

<!-- chunk -->

### Because of the shape, smell, and taste, tablets are often coated with a thin

<!-- chunk -->

### clear vegetable shellac, a color-coded dye substance, or a sugar coating.

<!-- chunk -->

### Tablets are made with high-speed machinery. They are not practical to make at

<!-- chunk -->

### home, but you can mix herbal powders together in the same way you would to

<!-- chunk -->

### form a bolus (see Chapter Eight) and form the mixture into small round balls.

<!-- chunk -->

### Lay the balls on a flat surface and allow them to dry for a few days so that they

<!-- chunk -->

### harden slightly. These herbal pills can be swallowed like tablets. If allowed to

<!-- chunk -->

### thoroughly dry, they will store for a few weeks in an airtight container.

# HERBAL NUT BUTTER BALLS

Herbal nut butter balls, popularized by herbalist Rosemary Gladstar, are a fun way to prepare tonic and nutritive herbs for consumption. Herb balls allow a higher dosage than what you can get from a few capsules or tablets. It’s also a delicious way to prepare herbs for children. 16 ounces (1 pint) almond butter, peanut butter, or other nut butter 1½ cups maple syrup or honey 6–8 ounces powdered herbs ½ cup cocoa powder Chopped nuts, raisins, or shredded coconut (optional) Mix the ingredients together and roll them into balls. Roll the balls in coconut or in cocoa powder to coat them so they are less sticky. Tonic and adaptogenic herbs like maca, astragalus, ashwagandha, ginseng, eleuthero, licorice, ginkgo, hawthorn, reishi, shiitake, and codonopsis all work well in this formula. You can also use herbal spices like cardamom or ginger for flavoring. For even more ideas, look up “Rosemary Gladstar’s Zoom Balls” online, or get her great book Rosemary Gladstar’s Herbal Recipes for Vibrant Health.

# TOPICAL USE OF BULK HERBS

<!-- chunk -->

### Always apply herbs as close as possible to the problem. If you have a problem

<!-- chunk -->

### with your toe, put comfrey right on the toe. This simple concept forms the basis

<!-- chunk -->

### for treating many problems, and is often more effective than ingesting an herb

<!-- chunk -->

### and expecting it to act systemically. When herbs don’t work, it is often as much

<!-- chunk -->

### the fault of improper application as it is poor choice of herbs.

<!-- chunk -->

### There are a number of ways to apply herbs to the tissues that need them.

<!-- chunk -->

### Poultices, plasters, suppositories, and more are discussed in Chapter Eight.

# USING MUCILAGINOUS HERBS

Highly mucilaginous herbs are best used in bulk. They do not work well as any form of extract. The water-soluble fibers they contain are known as mucilage, which does not extract in alcohol, vinegar, or oil. Mucilage is water soluble, but it absorbs the water, turning it into a slippery, slimy, unpalatable mass. So, although small amounts can be made into teas, syrups, and glycerites, the herbs simply work better when taken in bulk form. Three popular herbs that fall into this category are slippery elm, marshmallow, and psyllium hulls. Slippery elm is on the United Plant Savers at-risk list, and marshmallow is a fully interchangeable and inexpensive alternative. Taking the bulk powder is the best way to obtain the benefits of marshmallow, because an effective dose is about a heaping teaspoon or more, which is difficult to obtain by taking capsules. Fortunately, its bland, slightly sweet taste is not unpleasant. Marshmallow does not readily mix with water, so it’s easiest to mix it with juice in the blender. For example, add a heaping teaspoon of marshmallow to a cup of apple or orange juice and blend them together. You can drink it right away before the mucilage absorbs the water and turns the liquid slimy. However, if you’re trying to coat an irritated esophagus, let the mixture turn gelatinous before drinking it. Marshmallow may also be mixed with applesauce or a hot cereal such as oatmeal. Another herb that is best used in bulk is psyllium hulls, a bulk laxative. A therapeutic dose can be anywhere from ¼ teaspoon to 2 heaping teaspoons. This makes taking it in capsules impractical. It also is impossible to make an extract of it, again because the mucilage in it is not soluble in alcohol, vinegar, oil, or glycerin. Psyllium rapidly absorbs water, turning into a slimy, gelatinous mass that is not very appealing. The best way to take psyllium is to mix it into a cup of water or juice (apple is the best). Stir it until it becomes suspended, then drink it before it has a chance to gel. When taking psyllium, start with a small amount (¼–½ teaspoon) and then work up to 1–2 teaspoons daily. Psyllium can also be blended with other herbs. See the Gentle Fiber Blend in Chapter Twelve for directions on making your own. Photo by Thinkstock


<!-- chunk -->

## CHAPTER FIVE

# Introduction to Extractions

<!-- chunk -->

## TERMS, EQUIPMENT, SOLVENTS, AND CALCULATIONS

<!-- chunk -->

### his chapter covers the basic terms associated with extracting herbs, the

<!-- chunk -->

### equipment you’ll need, how to determine which solvent you should use, and

<!-- chunk -->

### how to determine potency.

# EXTRACTION TERMS

<!-- chunk -->

### Herbalism has its own unique language. It is the language of our art, and like

<!-- chunk -->

### many professions that use jargon, it invokes a bit of nostalgia and appreciation

<!-- chunk -->

### for the rich history of herbalism. Many of the terms we use in herbal medicine

<!-- chunk -->

### making date back to ancient times and are part of a rich heritage that teaches us

<!-- chunk -->

### how to turn crude plant material into effective herbal medicines. Here are some

<!-- chunk -->

### of the important terms to know.

<!-- chunk -->

## Garbling is the process of removing stems, diseased plant parts, and critters

<!-- chunk -->

### from freshly harvested plant material. This improves the quality of the medicine

<!-- chunk -->

### being made. Try it by yourself with good music playing in the background and

<!-- chunk -->

### you might find that magical Zen-like state that just seems to make everything

<!-- chunk -->

### better.

<!-- chunk -->

## Menstruum or solvent: Menstruum is an old alchemical term for a substance

<!-- chunk -->

### that dissolves a solid or holds it in suspension. The modern scientific term is

<!-- chunk -->

### solvent. Although many herbalists confine the term “menstruum” to alcohol

<!-- chunk -->

### extractions, technically anything that you use as a solution to extract the herbs is

<!-- chunk -->

### a menstruum, including water, alcohol, glycerin, vinegar, and simple syrups.

<!-- chunk -->

### Menstruums are measured in fluid volume. The choice to use metric units (liters,

<!-- chunk -->

### milliliters), fluid ounces, or the more standard US measures (cups, pints, quarts)

<!-- chunk -->

### is a personal one. Choose one and stick with it.

<!-- chunk -->

## Marc is the solid matter (plant material) in your preparation. This marc is mixed

<!-- chunk -->

### with the menstruum for extraction. After the extraction process, the plant

<!-- chunk -->

### material is called the exhausted marc. Marc is measured by weight. Again, you

<!-- chunk -->

### can use metric or US measures; just make sure you use the same standard of

<!-- chunk -->

### measurement for the marc that you’re using for the menstruum.

<!-- chunk -->

## Solubility is the ability of the menstruum to extract various constituents from a

<!-- chunk -->

### plant into a liquid form. Solubility depends on many factors, including the

<!-- chunk -->

### polarity (electrical charge) of the substance, pH, temperature, and the presence

<!-- chunk -->

### of other constituents or compounds in the plant. Solubility is difficult to

<!-- chunk -->

### determine, but herbalists have the cumulative experience of other herbalists as

<!-- chunk -->

### well as some science to determine the best solvents for particular plants. We’ll

<!-- chunk -->

### cover this in greater detail later in this chapter.

<!-- chunk -->

## Maceration is a way to make tinctures. Macerating herbs means you soak the

<!-- chunk -->

### marc in the menstruum for a set period of time that varies from process to

<!-- chunk -->

### process. Maceration allows the menstruum to become saturated with constituents

<!-- chunk -->

### from the marc.

<!-- chunk -->

## The maceration container is a leak-proof container, usually glass but

<!-- chunk -->

### sometimes stainless steel, that is used in the maceration process. Glass canning

<!-- chunk -->

### jars are commonly used by home medicine makers. They come in a wide

<!-- chunk -->

### assortment of sizes (half pints, pints, quarts, and even half gallons) and are

<!-- chunk -->

### relatively inexpensive. When using mason jars place a single layer of parchment

<!-- chunk -->

### paper between the lid and jar to keep your extract from interacting with the metal

<!-- chunk -->

### in the lid. Many solvents can cause metal corrosion, which will adversely affect

<!-- chunk -->

### your extract.

<!-- chunk -->

## Succussion is an old word for shaking. Maceration extracts are succussed to

<!-- chunk -->

### ensure complete contact between the marc and the menstruum. In dry herb

<!-- chunk -->

### macerations, succussion is an essential part of the process. Agitation moves

<!-- chunk -->

### particles that clump at the bottom of the container back into contact with the

<!-- chunk -->

### menstruum. Succussion isn’t normally required for fresh herb macerations; the

<!-- chunk -->

### high-proof alcohol used in fresh plant preparations dehydrates the plant cells,

<!-- chunk -->

### pulling all of the constituents out of the plant and into solution.

<!-- chunk -->

## Decanting is the process of pouring off the menstruum from one container to

<!-- chunk -->

### another, often through a filtering cloth or paper, without disturbing the sediment

<!-- chunk -->

### at the bottom of the container.

<!-- chunk -->

## Precipitation occurs when solid particles fall out of solution in a tincture or

<!-- chunk -->

### extract. This most commonly occurs when a tincture hasn’t been filtered

<!-- chunk -->

### properly, or when the extract is exposed to light or drastic temperature changes.

<!-- chunk -->

### Precipitation will occur in most tinctures as they age, but if you’ve made the

<!-- chunk -->

### tincture or extract properly, it should not occur for many years. Mild

<!-- chunk -->

### precipitation can be fixed by decanting the tincture into a clean container. If

<!-- chunk -->

### massive precipitation occurs along with a change in color or taste, it’s time to

<!-- chunk -->

### discard the tincture and start again.

# MEDICINE-MAKING EQUIPMENT

<!-- chunk -->

### In order to make herbal medicines, you’ll need some equipment. Here are the

<!-- chunk -->

### items we recommend.

<!-- chunk -->

## Jars. You will need jars—lots of them—if you get into medicine making. An

<!-- chunk -->

### assortment of mason jars is helpful: half pint, pint, and quart jars are all most

<!-- chunk -->

### home medicine makers need. If you’re making medicine for an herbal practice,

<!-- chunk -->

### invest in half-gallon mason jars. One-gallon pickle jars work nicely for larger

<!-- chunk -->

### batches of tinctures. Gallon mason jars are no longer manufactured but can be

<!-- chunk -->

### found occasionally on eBay.

<!-- chunk -->

## Rubber spatulas in an assortment of sizes will help get every last bit of

<!-- chunk -->

### medicine out of your jars.

<!-- chunk -->

## Mortar and pestle. Although this seems like an antiquated tool, a mortar and

<!-- chunk -->

### pestle still comes in handy in medicine making. Most of the small marble sets

<!-- chunk -->

### are appropriate for displays and powdering small amounts of resins like myrrh.

<!-- chunk -->

### You can use larger sets to powder small batches of tea concentrates. (They are

<!-- chunk -->

### also good for powdering small amounts of whole spices for cooking.) If you

<!-- chunk -->

### have to powder roots and barks by hand, a tall cylindrical mortar and pestle is

<!-- chunk -->

### best.

<!-- chunk -->

### Every herbalist should powder a pound of burdock root with a mortar and

<!-- chunk -->

### pestle at least once in their life, just for the experience. Okay, maybe not a whole

<!-- chunk -->

### pound, but it’s well worth it to learn how to use a mortar and pestle to powder

<!-- chunk -->

### small batches of herbs as part of the experience of making your own medicines.

<!-- chunk -->

## Coffee grinders are the home herbalist’s best friend when it comes to powdering

<!-- chunk -->

### herbs. Buy them new or get them cheaply at thrift stores. They have to be

<!-- chunk -->

### babysat while you use them, and even then they don’t last for a long time, but

<!-- chunk -->

### that’s better than spending thousands of dollars on a commercia l herb grinder.

<!-- chunk -->

## Vitamix. A Vitamix with a dry blade is even better than a coffee grinder. The

<!-- chunk -->

### adjustable-speed Vitamix has been around since about 1995, and although the

<!-- chunk -->

### look changes every few years, the motor stays the same. The dry blade container

<!-- chunk -->

### makes quick work of all but the hardest roots. The old-school Vitamix 3600 with

<!-- chunk -->

### the metal containers can grind hard roots, but can’t powder as well as the models

<!-- chunk -->

### that support a dry blade container. The 3600 model is wonderful for making

<!-- chunk -->

### fresh plant extracts. In a pinch you can put your plant material and alcohol into a

<!-- chunk -->

### Vitamix, leave it running for an hour, and produce a passable tincture. (The

<!-- chunk -->

### constant agitation speeds up the maceration process, and the heat from the motor

<!-- chunk -->

### produces a hot ethanol extract.) The new Vitamix models have polycarbonate

<!-- chunk -->

### containers, which tend to get scratched by hard herbs. Keep one container

<!-- chunk -->

### dedicated to herb grinding and another for food.

<!-- chunk -->

### Both the newer and older models can be found on eBay for $60–$150. At one

<!-- chunk -->

### time, Vitamix 3600 metal containers were produced without the juice spout, but

<!-- chunk -->

### they are impossible to find now. Disassemble and clean the juice spout after each

<!-- chunk -->

### use to prevent cross-contamination of herbs.

<!-- chunk -->

## Strainers and filters in a variety of sizes are essential to good medicine making.

<!-- chunk -->

### Winco makes a stainless steel 8-inch bouillon strainer that can be found on

<!-- chunk -->

### Amazon for $23. After an initial straining with a fine mesh strainer, pour the

<!-- chunk -->

### tincture through an unbleached paper coffee filter or two. Don’t get cone filters

<!-- chunk -->

### with glued bottoms; the glue might be soluble in high-proof alcohol. Place the

<!-- chunk -->

### coffee filters, or lab filter paper if you must, into the cone strainer to filter.

<!-- chunk -->

### You can also filter with paint strainer bags, available at most hardware stores,

<!-- chunk -->

### or with muslin tea bags. In either case, suspend the bag from the mouth of a jar.

<!-- chunk -->

### The lid band for the canning jar will fit snugly over the bags to hold them in

<!-- chunk -->

### place. Extra-fine gold mesh coffee filters are also nice, and can be reused much

<!-- chunk -->

### longer than cotton bag filters. Extracts made with cut and sifted herbs can be

<!-- chunk -->

### strained using several layers of unbleached, natural cheesecloth or organic cotton

<!-- chunk -->

### or flannel cloth. Don’t use stainless or gold mesh filters to strain resinous herbs.

<!-- chunk -->

### It is exceedingly difficult to clean a metal mesh filter that was used to filter

<!-- chunk -->

### boswellia gum.

<!-- chunk -->

## Mixing bowls. A dedicated set of stainless steel or glass bowls in a variety of

<!-- chunk -->

### sizes is essential. We prefer the stainless steel bowls with a pour spout, but even

<!-- chunk -->

### plastic bowls will work in a pinch.

<!-- chunk -->

## Herb press. An herb press isn’t necessary for the beginning herbalist or home

<!-- chunk -->

### medicine maker, but if you make medicines long enough you’ll tire of squeezing

<!-- chunk -->

### herbs by hand, which is time-consuming and inefficient, and leaves a lot of good

<!-- chunk -->

### medicine behind in the marc.

<!-- chunk -->

### A potato ricer is a nice starting herb press, and is much cheaper than most herb

<!-- chunk -->

### presses. Keep the batch size fairly small to avoid overexertion and cursing. The

<!-- chunk -->

### Norpro 469 Deluxe Cast Aluminum Jumbo Potato Ricer is the one we use.

<!-- chunk -->

### We’ve only broken one between us, and that took years of use. It is well worth

<!-- chunk -->

### the $30.

<!-- chunk -->

### If you are pressing batches of herbs larger than a quart, and you can afford it,

<!-- chunk -->

### upgrade to a good hydraulic or screw press. One of the best herb presses is an

<!-- chunk -->

### 1890s Enterprise sausage stuffer, wine press, and cider press. It’s more effective

<!-- chunk -->

### than most of the herb presses available on the market, and it looks really cool.

<!-- chunk -->

### The Enterprise name has changed to Chop-Rite and they still make replacement

<!-- chunk -->

### parts for the old models, which can be found on eBay for $150–$300, and you

<!-- chunk -->

### can find them at garage sales and flea markets for a lot less.

<!-- chunk -->

### If you know how to weld, you can modify a 1-ton hydraulic ball bearing press

<!-- chunk -->

### into an herb press for less than $200. That’s a lot cheaper than the thousands-of-

<!-- chunk -->

### dollars hydraulic herb presses that are sold commercially. Large hydraulic

<!-- chunk -->

### presses are not efficient for batches that are smaller than 5 gallons.

<!-- chunk -->

## Scale. Buy a scale that measures in grams in addition to ounces and pounds. For

<!-- chunk -->

### the average herbalist a $20 kitchen scale from a big box store is as accurate as

<!-- chunk -->

### you need. To be more precise, buy a triple beam scale with a big basket, or buy a

<!-- chunk -->

### 100-gram tester weight to make sure your scale is balanced.

<!-- chunk -->

## A digital thermometer helps manage temperatures for oil extracts or when

<!-- chunk -->

### cooking off alcohol from glycerin extracts. If you have the budget, get an

<!-- chunk -->

### infrared thermometer; if not, a digital candy thermometer works well.

<!-- chunk -->

## Measuring cups are helpful when making weight-to-volume extracts. Graduated

<!-- chunk -->

### cylinders are great when you need precise measurements. Glass graduated

<!-- chunk -->

### cylinders look good on shelves, but the first time you break a $40 Pyrex cylinder

<!-- chunk -->

### you’ll invest in a few cheap polypropylene ones (the solvent only comes into

<!-- chunk -->

### contact with the plastic momentarily). You can get a seven-piece polypropylene

<!-- chunk -->

### set with cylinders ranging from 10 to 1,000 milliliters for less than $35 from

<!-- chunk -->

### Amazon or a lab supply store.

<!-- chunk -->

## Funnels in a variety of sizes are helpful in pouring liquid extracts and powders

<!-- chunk -->

### from container to container. Canning funnels are great, and you can check out

<!-- chunk -->

### your local automotive or kitchen supply store for a bigger variety of sizes.

<!-- chunk -->

## Dehydrator. Although not essential, a dehydrator is a useful tool for an

<!-- chunk -->

### herbalist. Dehydrators dry small batches of herbs quickly, and can be used to

<!-- chunk -->

### dehydrate herbal extracts to make tea concentrates and fluid extracts. It’s also a

<!-- chunk -->

### wonderful investment if you want to dehydrate food like flaxseed crackers, fruit

<!-- chunk -->

### leathers, kale chips, or jerky.

<!-- chunk -->

### A horizontal dehydrator, like the one pictured here, is a good addition to your

<!-- chunk -->

### apothecary, but even a cheap vertical dehydrator is better than no dehydrator. (A

<!-- chunk -->

### space heater and a fan are not quite as efficient.)

# PICKING A SOLVENT AND EXTRACTION METHOD

<!-- chunk -->

### Throughout history, and especially since the 1600s, herbalists, alchemists, and

<!-- chunk -->

### physicians have been experimenting with various methods of extracting herbs.

<!-- chunk -->

### One of the great pioneers of this science was John Uri Lloyd (1849–1936), who

<!-- chunk -->

### was widely regarded as the best pharmacist of his time and the maker of the

<!-- chunk -->

### highest-quality herbal extracts in history. Lloyd approached every plant

<!-- chunk -->

### differently, using up to eight solvents on one plant to extract what he considered

<!-- chunk -->

### the essence of the plant.

<!-- chunk -->

### Every menstruum has particular constituents that it extracts effectively and

<!-- chunk -->

### other constituents that it doesn’t extract well. Water is a poor solvent of resins,

<!-- chunk -->

### but a great solvent for carbohydrates; alcohol is a great solvent for many

<!-- chunk -->

### medicinal constituents, but is a poor solvent of mucilage; and glycerin by itself

<!-- chunk -->

### (without heat and water) is a poor solvent of most constituents except volatile

<!-- chunk -->

### oils. Oil and fat can be used as menstruums to make extracts, but these are

<!-- chunk -->

### normally only used externally. Steam and carbon dioxide are used to extract

<!-- chunk -->

### essential oils.

<!-- chunk -->

### Commercial manufacturing and home extractions often use mixtures of

<!-- chunk -->

### menstruums (water, alcohol, glycerin, or vinegar). Standardized extracts may

<!-- chunk -->

### involve the use of chemical solvents to concentrate specific constituents, but that

<!-- chunk -->

### is not necessary for herbal products made at home.

<!-- chunk -->

### Some molecules, like water, are polar. That means that, just as a magnet has a

<!-- chunk -->

### north pole and a south pole, the molecule has a positively charged region and a

<!-- chunk -->

### negatively charged region. This means that polar molecules can attach to both

<!-- chunk -->

### positively charged elements and negatively charged elements. Water is highly

<!-- chunk -->

### polar, which is why it is a nearly universal solvent. On the other hand, a nonpolar

<!-- chunk -->

### molecule will be more electrically neutral and therefore a less-effective solvent.

<!-- chunk -->

### Water and glycerin are polar molecules; water is the most polar of the two.

<!-- chunk -->

### Alcohol is also polar and can be used to extract polar constituents when the

<!-- chunk -->

### percentage of alcohol in water is low. A higher alcohol content is better for

<!-- chunk -->

### extracting nonpolar constituents. Fats and oils are nonpolar solvents.

<!-- chunk -->

### Constituents that are best extracted using water, glycerin, or lower percentages

<!-- chunk -->

### of alcohol include carbohydrates (sugars and starches), mucilage and gums, most

<!-- chunk -->

### glycosides, and water-soluble vitamins. Some alkaloids can be extracted with

<!-- chunk -->

### polar substances. Oil and higher percentages of alcohol are better at extracting

<!-- chunk -->

### essential oils, terpenoids, carotenoids, fat-soluble vitamins, lipids, resins, and

<!-- chunk -->

### most alkaloids.

<!-- chunk -->

### Chapter Thirteen lists numerous single herbs and suggests a number of ways

<!-- chunk -->

### to prepare them. Table 2 provides a basic guide to the best extraction medium for

<!-- chunk -->

### the major categories of herbs. The primary constituents of each category of herb

<!-- chunk -->

### are listed in parentheses.

# POTENCIES

<!-- chunk -->

### A critical consideration when extracting any herb is how much liquid to use in

<!-- chunk -->

### proportion to herbs. This ratio depends on how potent you want your

<!-- chunk -->

### preparation. The commonly accepted way to standardize the potency of a

<!-- chunk -->

### formula is to calculate the ratio of the amount of herb to the amount of solvent.

<!-- chunk -->

### This is written with the amount of herb first and the amount of liquid second

<!-- chunk -->

### (1:5).

<!-- chunk -->

## Solvent

<!-- chunk -->

## TABLE 2. Basic Categories of Herbs and the Best Solvents

<!-- chunk -->

### Amounts are always measured by weight, not by volume, as the weight of

<!-- chunk -->

### various solvents is different. When measuring water, weight and volume are

<!-- chunk -->

### closely related. One fluid ounce of water weighs about 1 ounce. Sixteen fluid

<!-- chunk -->

### ounces of water (which is 1 pint or 2 cups by volume) weighs about 1 pound (16

<!-- chunk -->

### ounces by weight). The same applies in metrics. One liter of water weighs

<!-- chunk -->

### kilogram, and 100 milliliters of water weighs about 100 kilograms. The exact

<!-- chunk -->

### weight of the water depends on its temperature and other factors, but for home

<!-- chunk -->

### preparations these differences are not critical.

<!-- chunk -->

### As shown in Table 3, alcohol has the same weight-to-volume relationship as

<!-- chunk -->

### water, but other extraction mediums, such as vinegar, glycerin, and oil, do not.

<!-- chunk -->

### Once again, these differences are not so critical that you can’t measure the

<!-- chunk -->

### liquids by volume.

<!-- chunk -->

## SubstanceWeight per GallonWeight per QuartWeight per Pint

## TABLE 4. 1:8 Weight-to-Volume Extraction Ratio

<!-- chunk -->

### Most home preparations are between 1:2 and 1:10 in potency. Table 4 shows

<!-- chunk -->

### the amount of menstruum needed to make a 1:8 potency with various amounts of

<!-- chunk -->

### herb.

<!-- chunk -->

### The strength of the extract depends partially on the density of the herb you are

<!-- chunk -->

### extracting. It is usually easy to make a 1:4 or 1:5 extract of heavy pieces of bark

<!-- chunk -->

### or roots at home. When making a Simpler’s maceration with lighter fresh plant

<!-- chunk -->

### materials like dill weed or chamomile flowers, you’re more likely to end up with

<!-- chunk -->

### a 1:8 or even 1:10 extract, unless you really pack the plant material into your jar.

<!-- chunk -->

### Table 5 shows some standard potencies.

<!-- chunk -->

### Ratios are important in determining dosage. A 1:2 extract will be four times

<!-- chunk -->

### stronger than a 1:8 extract, which means you’ll need to take four times more of

<!-- chunk -->

### the 1:8 extract to get the same results as you would taking a 1:2 extract. You can

<!-- chunk -->

### read more about dosages in Chapter Eleven.

<!-- chunk -->

### These guidelines are based on the assumption that you are extracting all of the

<!-- chunk -->

### available constituents from the plant material, but plants vary greatly in their

<!-- chunk -->

### chemical composition. Plants that are wild and plants that are harvested when

<!-- chunk -->

### they are under environmental stress tend to be more chemically active than

<!-- chunk -->

### cultivated plants. Some plant constituents in the same plant can vary by up to

<!-- chunk -->

### 10,000% within a 2-week period. While we advocate using weight-to-volume

<!-- chunk -->

### ratios to help determine potency, we also suggest tasting tinctures and extracts

<!-- chunk -->

### and comparing them to previous batches. You might also compare them to

<!-- chunk -->

### commercial extracts or to the extracts of other herbalists. Assess the taste, smell,

<!-- chunk -->

### texture, and visual appearance of each preparation to determine its potency.

<!-- chunk -->

## TABLE 5. Standard Weight-to-Volume Ratios

# WORKING WITH ALCOHOL

<!-- chunk -->

### Different herbs extract better in different percentages of alcohol. Proof is a

<!-- chunk -->

### measurement of the percentage of alcohol in an alcoholic solution and is double

<!-- chunk -->

### the percentage of alcohol: 80 proof is 40% alcohol, and 160 proof is 80%

<!-- chunk -->

### alcohol.

<!-- chunk -->

### Most home tinctures are made with a weak solution of 40% alcohol like

<!-- chunk -->

### brandy. Some herbs need to be extracted with a higher alcohol content. A

<!-- chunk -->

### moderate-strength solution can be made with 60% alcohol, and a strong solution

<!-- chunk -->

### with 80% alcohol.

<!-- chunk -->

### The strongest alcohol solution commonly available is grain alcohol (the brand

<!-- chunk -->

### Everclear is the most widely available), which is usually 190 proof or 95%

<!-- chunk -->

### alcohol. You can purchase large quantities (55 gallons) of organic cane or grape

<!-- chunk -->

### alcohol for about the same price as Everclear. Consider going in with other

<!-- chunk -->

### herbalists and splitting an order. Organic cane or grape alcohol has a smoother

<!-- chunk -->

### taste and a more pleasant action than grain alcohol.

<!-- chunk -->

### Unless you are making a fresh plant extract, 95% alcohol needs to be diluted

<!-- chunk -->

### to reach the desired concentration. If you have 95% alcohol and need 40%

<!-- chunk -->

### alcohol, put 40 parts of alcohol in a container and add enough water to make

<!-- chunk -->

### parts. (A part is any volume of liquid measurement that you choose, such as

<!-- chunk -->

### fluid ounce.) To dilute 95% alcohol to 60%, put 60 parts of alcohol in a container

<!-- chunk -->

### and add enough water to make 95 parts. Tables 6, 7, and 8 will help you with

<!-- chunk -->

### these conversions.

<!-- chunk -->

### Now that we’ve gotten all the technical stuff out of the way, we can get to the

<!-- chunk -->

### fun part: Making your own herbal extracts.

<!-- chunk -->

## Alcohol Proof95% (190 Proof)90% (180 Proof)80% (160 Proof)

Amount of Alcohol60 ml or fl oz60 ml or fl oz60 ml or fl oz Amount of Water to Add35 ml or fl oz30 ml or fl oz20 ml or fl oz Total Amount of Solution95 ml or fl oz90 ml or fl oz80 ml or fl oz

<!-- chunk -->

# M

## CHAPTER SIX

# Making Basic Extracts

<!-- chunk -->

## EXTRACTING HERBS IN WATER, ALCOHOL, GLYCERIN, AND

<!-- chunk -->

## VINEGAR

<!-- chunk -->

### aking your own herbal extracts is enjoyable and educational. The process

<!-- chunk -->

### of extracting herbs will teach you a lot about different kinds of herbs and

<!-- chunk -->

### help you understand how to use them better. You’ll learn how to use your own

<!-- chunk -->

### senses to understand the effects herbs have on the body, especially if you taste

<!-- chunk -->

### what you make during the process (like a good chef does with her food).

<!-- chunk -->

### The key to making good extracts is to start with high-quality plant material.

<!-- chunk -->

### You don’t have to start with fresh plants to make high-quality extracts, but you

<!-- chunk -->

### can’t make good extracts out of a bag of 5-year-old, dried-out, cheap, imported

<!-- chunk -->

### herbs either. Use only reputable suppliers, or look for independent herbalists

<!-- chunk -->

### who sell or barter cultivated or wildcrafted plants from their region. Check out

<!-- chunk -->

### Appendix Two for companies that sell high-quality herbs and for groups that

<!-- chunk -->

### facilitate the swapping or selling of herbs from independent bioregional

<!-- chunk -->

### herbalists.

<!-- chunk -->

### Once you have the plant material you need, you can turn it into liquid extracts

<!-- chunk -->

### using a variety of solvents and extraction methods.

# WATER-BASED EXTRACTIONS

<!-- chunk -->

### Water extraction is the oldest and most basic method for making herbal

<!-- chunk -->

### medicine.

# INFUSIONS

<!-- chunk -->

### Herbal teas are easy to make and are a good start to learning to use herbal

<!-- chunk -->

### remedies. A hot or cold infusion can be made from fresh, dried, or powdered

<!-- chunk -->

### herbs. An infusion is sometimes called a tisane, which is a French word for tea.

<!-- chunk -->

### Teas are cheap, simple, and effective, and require only a short time to prepare.

<!-- chunk -->

### Infusions are a pleasant way to take some herbs, but are less palatable when

<!-- chunk -->

### the herb has a disagreeable flavor. Many constituents, especially the more bitter

<!-- chunk -->

### and astringent principles, are not released into the water in a simple tea but need

<!-- chunk -->

### to be extracted by more potent infusions or decoctions. Aromatic principles are

<!-- chunk -->

### lost if the container is left uncovered while the herbs are steeping.

<!-- chunk -->

### Infusions are used for topical applications as rinses, compresses, and foot

<!-- chunk -->

### soaks. An infusion may have a single ingredient or many. It may be taken hot or

<!-- chunk -->

### cold, inhaled as a vapor, used to perfume a pillow, or enjoyed in a bath.

<!-- chunk -->

## Hot Infusion

<!-- chunk -->

### Hot infusions are best for leaves and flowers whose medicinal properties,

<!-- chunk -->

### including vitamins and volatile oils, are easily released into water. To make a hot

<!-- chunk -->

### infusion, pour boiled water (boil water, take off the heat, and wait 30 seconds)

<!-- chunk -->

### over the herb. Cover the container. Use a canning jar, a covered pan, or a china,

<!-- chunk -->

### glass, or enamel teapot, and the purest water you can get. Let the infusion steep

<!-- chunk -->

### for at least 15 minutes, depending on the desired strength (see below), then strain

<!-- chunk -->

### the infusion into a cup. Drink it lukewarm or cool, but take it hot to break up a

<!-- chunk -->

### cold or cough. Teas can be sweetened with honey or raw sugar, or flavored with

<!-- chunk -->

### herbs such as lemon verbena or spearmint. Make infusions fresh each day, or

<!-- chunk -->

### refrigerate unused tea for up to three days.

<!-- chunk -->

### Water with a high lime content (very hard water) can impede the extraction of

<!-- chunk -->

### active constituents. Adding a little vinegar or lemon juice will help with the

<!-- chunk -->

### extraction of alkaloidal bitters, such as plants with berberine, or minerals like

<!-- chunk -->

### calcium.

<!-- chunk -->

### Some herbs take up more space than others and have to be packed down into

<!-- chunk -->

### the steeping container. The more surface area that is exposed, the more potent

<!-- chunk -->

### the tea, so don’t be afraid to chop the herb finely or even powder it before

<!-- chunk -->

### making tea.

<!-- chunk -->

### Most people steep tea a few minutes. This isn’t enough time when using

<!-- chunk -->

### plants for medicines. Here are the different steeping times depending on what

<!-- chunk -->

### strength you need.

<!-- chunk -->

## Weak Infusion Use 15 grams of herb per liter of water, or ½ ounce per quart.

<!-- chunk -->

### The steeping time varies depending on what part of the plant is used. Steep

<!-- chunk -->

### leaves for 1 hour, flowers for 30 minutes, crushed seeds for 15 minutes, and bark

<!-- chunk -->

### and roots for 4 hours.

<!-- chunk -->

## Standard Infusion Steep 30 grams of herb in 1 liter of hot water, or 1 ounce per

<!-- chunk -->

### quart, for 30–60 minutes. Strain well, pressing as much water out of the herb as

<!-- chunk -->

### possible, and drink the tea. Use within a day. Cut the formula down if you are

<!-- chunk -->

### drinking less than a liter of tea daily; use 15 grams of herb to 500 milliliters of

<!-- chunk -->

### water (½ ounce per pint).

<!-- chunk -->
