### This medicinal infusion represents about 1 gram of herb for every ounce of tea

<!-- chunk -->

### and is much stronger than normal tea. In most cases, the required dose is

<!-- chunk -->

### significantly less than a whole cup, which is wonderful for those not-so-pleasant-

<!-- chunk -->

### tasting teas.

<!-- chunk -->

## Strong Infusion Make as a standard infusion and steep for 8 hours.

<!-- chunk -->

## Cold Infusion

<!-- chunk -->

### A cold infusion is best for herbs with highly volatile constituents, or constituents

<!-- chunk -->

### that are damaged by heat. Soak 15 grams of herb in 1 liter of cold water (½

<!-- chunk -->

### ounce per quart) for 8–12 hours. Strain and drink. Make a fresh infusion daily.

# DECOCTIONS

<!-- chunk -->

### When your medicinal plant is tough, as in the bark, roots, rhizomes, seeds, nuts,

<!-- chunk -->

### or woody stems, you need to use the decoction method to extract the plant

<!-- chunk -->

### constituents. Decoct green plant parts if you are trying to extract mineral salts. A

<!-- chunk -->

### decoction is like a tea, except that the herb is simmered in water until some of

<!-- chunk -->

### the liquid has evaporated. Simmering evaporates volatile principles, so the

<!-- chunk -->

### decoction method is not a good technique for aromatic herbs.

<!-- chunk -->

### Like infusions, decoctions may contain one ingredient or many. They may be

<!-- chunk -->

### ingested hot or cold or applied externally. Make decoctions fresh daily and

<!-- chunk -->

### refrigerate unused tea. A decoction should keep for up to 3 days in the

<!-- chunk -->

### refrigerator.

<!-- chunk -->

## Standard Decoction

<!-- chunk -->

### Put 30 grams of herb and 1 liter of water (1 ounce per quart) in a pot, cover, and

<!-- chunk -->

### bring to a low simmer for 10–20 minutes. Turn off the heat and steep for an

<!-- chunk -->

### additional hour.

<!-- chunk -->

## Strong Decoction

<!-- chunk -->

### Place 30 grams of herb and 1 liter of water (1 ounce per quart) in a pot, cover,

<!-- chunk -->

### and bring to a low simmer until the water is reduced by half.

<!-- chunk -->

## Southern Decoction

<!-- chunk -->

### Place 30 grams of herb and 2 liters of water (1 ounce per 2 quarts) in a pot.

<!-- chunk -->

### Bring to a low simmer for 4 or more hours, until there is about 500 milliliters

<!-- chunk -->

### (about 1 pint) of fluid remaining.

<!-- chunk -->

### This process allows most volatile oils to escape while extracting more mineral

<!-- chunk -->

### salts, creating a tea with a different medicinal action than standard teas.

# SYRUPS (ELECTUARIES)

<!-- chunk -->

### A syrup is a water extract that is preserved with a sweetener, typically honey or

<!-- chunk -->

### raw sugar. Most syrups are made by simmering the herbs in a mixture of 50%

<!-- chunk -->

### water and 50% sweetener and straining the syrup for use.

<!-- chunk -->

### Syrups keep longer than water extracts, because the sugar preserves the

<!-- chunk -->

### extraction. The sweet taste covers the nasty flavor of many herbs, such as

<!-- chunk -->

### dandelion, white pine bark, poplar bark, or raw garlic. Try medicinal syrups for

<!-- chunk -->

### anyone who cannot or will not tolerate bitter or strong flavors.

<!-- chunk -->

## Sweeteners

<!-- chunk -->

## Honey is a natural sweetener with proven antibiotic and antiseptic properties.

<!-- chunk -->

### Honey is a combination of sugars produced from nectar by the enzyme invertase,

<!-- chunk -->

### which is found in the bodies of bees. Honey contains about 40% fructose, 31%

<!-- chunk -->

### glucose, 18% water, 9% other sugars, and 2% sucrose. The flavor varies

<!-- chunk -->

### depending on the flower source. Honey contains all the vitamins and enzymes

<!-- chunk -->

### necessary for the proper metabolism and digestion of glucose and other sugar

<!-- chunk -->

### molecules. Honey is about 50% sweeter than table sugar, which means that ¾

<!-- chunk -->

### cup honey adds the same sweetness to a formula as 1 cup sugar. Do not give

<!-- chunk -->

### syrups made with honey to children under the age of one.

<!-- chunk -->

## Maple syrup is a natural sweetener made by boiling the sap of maple trees. It

<!-- chunk -->

### contains primarily sucrose, the same type of sugar as raw sugar. Maple syrup

<!-- chunk -->

### contains significant amounts of manganese and moderate but notable amounts of

<!-- chunk -->

### zinc. Pure maple syrup is expensive, but makes an excellent medicinal syrup

<!-- chunk -->

### base.

<!-- chunk -->

## Raw sugar. Syrups can be made using natural sugars like raw sugar, yellow D

<!-- chunk -->

### sugar, or freeze-dried sugarcane juice (Sucanat). These sugars are not as sweet as

<!-- chunk -->

### honey and do not have the same medicinal benefits, but they add their own

<!-- chunk -->

### flavor to the syrup, which may improve the taste of some preparations. Refined

<!-- chunk -->

### sugar is the ultimate naked carbohydrate, stripped of all nutritional benefits, and

<!-- chunk -->

### should be avoided when making syrups if raw sugar, honey, or maple syrup is

<!-- chunk -->

### available.

<!-- chunk -->

## Making a Syrup

<!-- chunk -->

## Standard Method Mix sweetener and water in equal parts by heating the mixture

<!-- chunk -->

### slightly. Add the plant material at a ratio of 1:5, or 20% of the volume of liquid

<!-- chunk -->

### by weight. To make 1 pint syrup (16 fluid ounces), mix 1 cup water (8 fluid

<!-- chunk -->

### ounces) and 1 cup raw sugar or honey (8 fluid ounces). Add about 3.2 ounces

<!-- chunk -->

### (20% of 16 ounces) of herbs. Bring the mixture to a boil, then turn the heat down

<!-- chunk -->

### low and simmer, covered, for 20–30 minutes. Strain the herbs and bottle the

<!-- chunk -->

### syrup.

<!-- chunk -->

## Simple Method Make a strong infusion or decoction of the herbs. Measure the

<!-- chunk -->

### volume of the decoction or infusion and add an equal amount of honey or raw

<!-- chunk -->

### sugar.

# FRESH BERRY SYRUP

Heat fresh berries (such as elder berries, bilberries, hawthorn berries, or rose hips) in just enough water to soften the berries, then press the berries and water through a jelly bag to express the juice, as you would do for making jelly. Measure the amount of berry juice and add an equal amount of honey or raw sugar. This syrup can be bottled or canned like jelly.

<!-- chunk -->

## Using Syrups

<!-- chunk -->

### Syrups are great for soothing sore throats, coughs, and most digestive upsets.

<!-- chunk -->

### The high sugar content makes them unsuitable for treating chronic fatigue,

<!-- chunk -->

### nutritive imbalances, or deep-seated chronic disorders like diabetes. Avoid

<!-- chunk -->

### syrups with people who have severe intestinal dysbiosis (an imbalance of gut

<!-- chunk -->

### bacteria that causes gas, bloating, and digestive upset), and use cautiously with

<!-- chunk -->

### hypoglycemia.

<!-- chunk -->

### Doses for syrups are relatively large (from 1 teaspoon to 2 tablespoons). Syrup

<!-- chunk -->

### stores in the refrigerator for up to a month. If the syrup develops an off taste, or

<!-- chunk -->

### if mold appears on the top of the syrup, discard it. To preserve the syrup for a

<!-- chunk -->

### longer period, add 20% of an 80-proof alcohol like brandy or rum. Syrups with

<!-- chunk -->

### alcohol added will last for a year or more when refrigerated or canned.

<!-- chunk -->

### There are several formulas for syrups in Chapter Twelve.

# ALCOHOL EXTRACTIONS (TINCTURES)

<!-- chunk -->

### Water preparations have limited shelf life. To make liquid preparations with a

<!-- chunk -->

### longer shelf life, you need to use a menstruum that has preservative actions.

<!-- chunk -->

### Alcohol has been used to make herbal extracts for thousands of years. The

<!-- chunk -->

### chemical analysis of residue found in clay pots in China confirms that a

<!-- chunk -->

### fermented drink made of honey, hawthorn berry, and grape was being produced

<!-- chunk -->

### between 7000 and 5600 BCE. At around the same time, barley beer and grape

<!-- chunk -->

### wine were being made in the Middle East. There is evidence of alcoholic

<!-- chunk -->

### beverages being consumed as far back as 3000 BCE in Egypt and 2000 BCE in

<!-- chunk -->

### the Americas. Research dating back to ancient Mesopotamia suggests that beer

<!-- chunk -->

### preceded bread as a caloric staple and as a way to preserve grains throughout the

<!-- chunk -->

### winter. There is even literature from the Babylonians and Egyptians warning

<!-- chunk -->

### about the ill effects of the overconsumption of alcohol. Ayurvedic texts from

<!-- chunk -->

### India describe the medicinal effects of alcohol and warn of alcohol-induced

<!-- chunk -->

### disease and intoxication.

<!-- chunk -->

### The medicinal effects of alcohol are cataloged in many of the oldest written

<!-- chunk -->

### works. The Bible talked about the medicinal use of wine. The Catholic Church

<!-- chunk -->

### and early Protestant leaders taught that alcohol was a gift from God to be used in

<!-- chunk -->

### moderation for enjoyment and health.

<!-- chunk -->

### In medieval Europe small beer was made and consumed by most households.

<!-- chunk -->

### Water and barley, boiled and then fermented, made a much safer drink than plain

<!-- chunk -->

### water. There are reports of people drinking more than a gallon of small beer

<!-- chunk -->

### daily, which clocks in at around 1% alcohol by volume.

<!-- chunk -->

### The use of wine as medicine was mentioned in Sima Qian’s biography of Bian

<!-- chunk -->

### Que, a Chinese doctor in the fifth century BCE. It is not known if Bian Que used

<!-- chunk -->

### herbs in the making of wine, or if he assigned energetics to different varieties of

<!-- chunk -->

### wine in a manner similar to that of traditional Chinese food theory. According to

<!-- chunk -->

### the Yellow Emperor’s Classic, written between 475 and 221 BCE, medicinal

<!-- chunk -->

### wines were made by fermenting herbs along with the fruit. It wasn’t until the

<!-- chunk -->

### Qing dynasty in the 1600s that tincturing and infusing wines with medicinal

<!-- chunk -->

### herbs became commonplace.

# HERBAL BEERS AND WINES

<!-- chunk -->

### Historically, bittering agents and flavorings (gruit) used in beers varied

<!-- chunk -->

### throughout the world. Households and geographic areas had favorite

<!-- chunk -->

### combinations of herbs that provided taste and helped with the preservation of the

<!-- chunk -->

### drink. Gruit typically consisted of a number of medicinal herbs, including sweet

<!-- chunk -->

### gale, yarrow, mugwort, ground ivy, and spruce tips.

<!-- chunk -->

### Herb-infused wines have been used in the Western world at least since

<!-- chunk -->

### Hippocrates. They played a big role in materia medicas prior to the seventeenth

<!-- chunk -->

### century, when distillation became more common. In our opinion, medicinal

<!-- chunk -->

### wines should play a bigger role in modern herbalism than they currently do.

<!-- chunk -->

### There is a wine formula under wild cherry in Chapter Thirteen.

# DISTILLATION

<!-- chunk -->

### Distillation vessels have been found dating back to the first century CE, but most

<!-- chunk -->

### experts think that they were used in the distillation experiments of alchemy

<!-- chunk -->

### rather than to distill alcohol or volatile oils. The discovery of alcohol distillation

<!-- chunk -->

### is a topic of contention. There is some evidence that the Chinese invented it, and

<!-- chunk -->

### other evidence that it was the Italians or Greeks. Most experts believe that the

<!-- chunk -->

### Arabians invented alcohol distillation. The Persian alchemist Jābir ibn Hayyān is

<!-- chunk -->

### credited with inventing the alembic, the precursor to the pot still; and the great

<!-- chunk -->

### physician Ibn Sīnā (Avicenna) refined distillation by adding the cooling coil. It

<!-- chunk -->

### was the German Albertus Magnus (1193–1280) who first clearly described the

<!-- chunk -->

### process for manufacturing distilled spirits.

<!-- chunk -->

### Distillation and the concentrated alcohol it produced spread slowly in

<!-- chunk -->

### medicine. Herbalists administered herbs as decoctions and wine infusions until

<!-- chunk -->

### the thirteenth century, when Spanish alchemists Arnold of Villanova and

<!-- chunk -->

### Raymond Lully introduced aqua vitae (water of life, otherwise known as brandy)

<!-- chunk -->

### as a solvent in European medicine. In the sixteenth century, Paracelsus

<!-- chunk -->

### popularized the use of distilled alcohol as a solvent to prepare tinctures. At this

<!-- chunk -->

### point brandy contained at most 70% alcohol. In the early nineteenth century,

<!-- chunk -->

### Aeneas Coffey invented the continuous column still, which led to the production

<!-- chunk -->

### of 94–96% ethanol.

# DRIED HERB TINCTURES (MACERATIONS)

<!-- chunk -->

### The most common way of making a tincture is by soaking an herb in a solution

<!-- chunk -->

### of alcohol and water for 14 days or more. This process is called maceration.

<!-- chunk -->

### Most maceration extracts are done as a cold process, meaning they do not

<!-- chunk -->

### require heat.

<!-- chunk -->

### Standard tinctures are made using a weight-to-volume ratio of 1:5 with dried

<!-- chunk -->

### herbs. A quart jar will hold about 150 grams of plant material with 750 milliliters

<!-- chunk -->

### of menstruum. The percentage of alcohol used depends on the plant and the

<!-- chunk -->

### constituents you are trying to extract. For most dry plants an alcohol content of

<!-- chunk -->

### 40–60% is best. The two most common choices for home medicine making are

<!-- chunk -->

### 40% alcohol vodka and brandy and 50% alcohol vodka. Note that the proof is

<!-- chunk -->

### double the percentage of alcohol, so 40% alcohol is 80 proof. In many cases it is

<!-- chunk -->

### more cost effective to buy 190-proof alcohol (95% EtOH) such as Everclear and

<!-- chunk -->

### dilute it with water to the appropriate percentage, as described in Chapter Five.

<!-- chunk -->

### Some herbs require a higher alcohol percentage than others. Resins and gums

<!-- chunk -->

### should be macerated in 90% alcohol to extract constituents, whereas glycoside-

<!-- chunk -->

### containing herbs like soapwort, mullein, or licorice, or a tannin-containing herb

<!-- chunk -->

### such as witch hazel, might require only 25% alcohol.

<!-- chunk -->

### Keep the maceration in a dark place. Shake the jar daily for 2 weeks or more.

<!-- chunk -->

### Pour the liquid out through a muslin cloth and squeeze the marc (herbs) until you

<!-- chunk -->

### have removed as much of the extract as possible. Put the finished tincture in an

<!-- chunk -->

### amber bottle or other dark container to protect it from degradation by light.

# FRESH PLANT TINCTURES (MACERATIONS)

<!-- chunk -->

### Fresh plants usually contain significant water content, so most fresh plant

<!-- chunk -->

### tinctures are made with 95% alcohol. High-proof alcohol ruptures the cell walls

<!-- chunk -->

### of plants, bringing the constituents into solution with the alcohol. It is easiest to

<!-- chunk -->

### make a fresh plant tincture by blending the plant material with alcohol in a

<!-- chunk -->

### blender, or you can chop the herb by hand and mix it with alcohol in a jar. Allow

<!-- chunk -->

### the maceration to sit for at least 14 days, then strain and bottle. We have found

<!-- chunk -->

### jars of fresh plant macerations, forgotten in the back of a cabinet for more than

<!-- chunk -->

### years, to be effective medicine. So while 14 days is the minimum time necessary

<!-- chunk -->

### to let a fresh plant maceration sit, the maximum time is whenever you remember

<!-- chunk -->

### to strain the tincture.

<!-- chunk -->

### If you want to make a fresh plant tincture using a lower percentage of alcohol,

<!-- chunk -->

### you will need to figure out the moisture content of the fresh plant material. This

<!-- chunk -->

### is not necessary for home herbalists unless they are making products for

<!-- chunk -->

### commercial sale.

<!-- chunk -->

### To determine the percentage of water in a plant, dry 100 grams of the fresh

<!-- chunk -->

### plant in a dehydrator or oven. Weigh the dried plant material. The difference

<!-- chunk -->

### between the fresh weight and dried weight is the percentage of water content: If

<!-- chunk -->

### the herb weighs 70 grams after drying, the plant material was 30% water.

# USING ALCOHOL WITH OTHER SOLVENTS

<!-- chunk -->

### To get a better extract, you can blend alcohol with other solvents. Adding 10%

<!-- chunk -->

### glycerin to an alcohol tincture helps extract volatile oils and stabilizes tannins. It

<!-- chunk -->

### gives the tincture a more pleasant taste, especially when using high-proof

<!-- chunk -->

### alcohol. In plants where the dominant constituents are alkaloids or minerals, add

<!-- chunk -->

### 5% vinegar to extract the constituents more effectively. Add 10% raw sugar or

<!-- chunk -->

### honey to a finished alcohol extract to create an elixir.

# GLYCERIN EXTRACTS (GLYCERITES)

<!-- chunk -->

### Glycerin is an underused menstruum in the herbal world. The sweet taste of

<!-- chunk -->

### glycerin helps to mask bitterness and other disagreeable flavors. Glycerin is a tri-

<!-- chunk -->

### atomic alcohol, not a sugar. This means glycerin does not affect blood sugar

<!-- chunk -->

### levels in healthy people, nor does it cause problems with yeast.

<!-- chunk -->

### Glycerin is a natural component of triglycerides. Fats and oils are composed

<!-- chunk -->

### of three (tri-) fatty acids attached to a molecule of glycerin (-glyceride). The

<!-- chunk -->

### body breaks down fats and oils to fatty acids and glycerin. It reassembles them

<!-- chunk -->

### to make the body’s own fats, or it can metabolize the glycerin and fatty acids for

<!-- chunk -->

### fuel. The conversion of glycerin to glucose produces only carbon dioxide and

<!-- chunk -->

### water.

<!-- chunk -->

### Glycerin is moisturizing, and it is frequently used in soaps, lotions, and

<!-- chunk -->

### creams. It is also a preservative, inhibiting the growth of bacteria and fungi.

<!-- chunk -->

### Because it is sweet, nourishing, and nontoxic, glycerin is the best menstruum to

<!-- chunk -->

### use in herbal extracts for children or adults who cannot use alcohol.

<!-- chunk -->

### Glycerin is a polar solvent, but not as polar as water. It does not extract

<!-- chunk -->

### constituents very well unless heat is used during the extraction process, so it is

<!-- chunk -->

### not a good menstruum to use for heat-sensitive herbs.

<!-- chunk -->

### Glycerin is sticky, so it’s not as easy to work with as alcohol. When using it as

<!-- chunk -->

### a menstruum, purchase pure vegetable glycerin that is intended for food use.

<!-- chunk -->

### Glycerin is available from a number of companies that sell bulk herbs and can

<!-- chunk -->

### easily be found on the internet.

# HOW GLYCERITES ARE MADE

<!-- chunk -->

### There are a number of ways to make an herbal glycerite. Here are four methods:

<!-- chunk -->

## Traditional Method

<!-- chunk -->

### Edward Shook, author of Advanced Treatise in Herbology, was a big advocate of

<!-- chunk -->

### glycerites. He made a strong decoction or infusion and added an equal amount of

<!-- chunk -->

### glycerin as a preservative. The resulting mixture is 50% water and 50% glycerin.

<!-- chunk -->

### In our experience, 10% of extracts made this way develop mold on the top.

<!-- chunk -->

### Increasing the glycerin content to 60% prevents mold growth. You can also add

<!-- chunk -->

### 20% of an 80-proof alcohol to a finished glycerite to improve shelf life of a 50%

<!-- chunk -->

### water/50% glycerin preparation.

<!-- chunk -->

## Standard Method

<!-- chunk -->

### Another method for making glycerites is to use the standard method for making

<!-- chunk -->

### syrups, as described earlier in this chapter. Some of the water will simmer off in

<!-- chunk -->

### this process, so start with 55% glycerin and 45% water. Add the herbs to the

<!-- chunk -->

### glycerin–water mixture at a 1:5 ratio. Simmer the herbs for 20–30 minutes, then

<!-- chunk -->

### strain and bottle.

<!-- chunk -->

## Sealed Simmer Glycerites

<!-- chunk -->

### Simmering herbs in a pot, even with the lid on, allows many of the volatile oils

<!-- chunk -->

### in the herbs to escape. To make a sealed simmer glycerite, start with a mixture of

<!-- chunk -->

### 60% glycerin and 40% water. Put the herbs and menstruum into canning jars at

<!-- chunk -->

### the standard 1:5 ratio. For pint jars add 3.33 ounces of herbs, and for quart jars

<!-- chunk -->

### add 6.66 ounces of herbs. If the plant material is dense, round these figures up to

<!-- chunk -->

### 4 ounces of herb for a pint jar and 8 ounces for a quart jar. If the plant material is

<!-- chunk -->

### extremely light and you can’t get the full amount of herb into the jar, pack it no

<!-- chunk -->

### more than three-quarters full.

<!-- chunk -->

### Cover the plant material with the glycerin–water mixture, leaving at least ¼

<!-- chunk -->

### inch of headspace in the jar. Put the canning lid on the jar and put it into a

<!-- chunk -->

### boiling water bath or steam canner. When the water starts to boil (or the steam

<!-- chunk -->

### starts to stream out of the steam canner), start timing the extract. Extract the

<!-- chunk -->

### herbs for a minimum of 15 minutes for leaves and flowers and 30 minutes for

<!-- chunk -->

### denser plant materials like barks and roots. Let the jars cool until they can be

<!-- chunk -->

### safely handled, then strain the contents and bottle them. To use a pressure canner

<!-- chunk -->

### to make glycerites, process the jars at 5 pounds of pressure for 10 minutes for

<!-- chunk -->

### leaves and flowers, and for 15 minutes for barks and roots.

<!-- chunk -->

## Fresh Plant Glycerites

<!-- chunk -->

### Glycerin will extract essential oils in fresh aromatic herbs such as peppermint

<!-- chunk -->

### and lemon balm. Stuff a canning jar completely full of the fresh plant material,

<!-- chunk -->

### leaving about 1 inch of headspace. Fill the jar with a mixture of 70% glycerin

<!-- chunk -->

### and 30% water. Put the canning lid on the jar and seal tightly to prevent the

<!-- chunk -->

### essential oils from escaping. Put the jar in the canner or a boiling-water bath and

<!-- chunk -->

### process in boiling water for about 15 minutes. Allow the jar to become cool to

<!-- chunk -->

### the touch before opening and straining out the herbs. The fragrance and aroma of

<!-- chunk -->

### such extracts are absolutely incredible.

# NOTES ABOUT COMMERCIAL GLYCERITES

<!-- chunk -->

### Alcohol can be used as an intermediate if the active constituents are not water

<!-- chunk -->

### soluble. Some glycerites are made commercially by making standard alcohol

<!-- chunk -->

### tinctures, then drawing off the alcohol using a vacuum and gentle heat. Glycerin

<!-- chunk -->

### is added to make a very strong glycerite preparation. Most of the aromatic

<!-- chunk -->

### qualities of the extract are lost during this process.

# USES AND APPLICATIONS OF GLYCERITES

<!-- chunk -->

### Glycerites are great for children, alcoholics, people whose religious beliefs

<!-- chunk -->

### prohibit the use of alcohol, and people who dislike the taste of alcohol tinctures.

<!-- chunk -->

### We have never seen a glycerite go bad as long as the glycerin content was well

<!-- chunk -->

### above 50% and the product was stored in a cool, dark place. Done properly,

<!-- chunk -->

### glycerites have a shelf life of at least 3 years. Some people refrigerate glycerites

<!-- chunk -->

### just to be safe.

<!-- chunk -->

### Glycerin isn’t as strong of a solvent as alcohol. Whereas the dose of tinctures

<!-- chunk -->

### is usually measured in drops, glycerites are taken in teaspoons. Glycerin is more

<!-- chunk -->

### expensive than alcohol, and the large dose makes glycerites more expensive per

<!-- chunk -->

### dose than alcohol tinctures.

# HERBAL VINEGARS

<!-- chunk -->

### Vinegars have been used for five thousand years as flavor enhancers and food

<!-- chunk -->

### preservers. Vinegars help digest heavy foods and high-protein meals and can be

<!-- chunk -->

### used in condiments, relishes, or dressings. The most nutritious vinegars still

<!-- chunk -->

### contain the mother (a mixture of beneficial bacteria and enzymes) in the bottle;

<!-- chunk -->

### they look slightly cloudy.

# HOW VINEGARS ARE MADE

<!-- chunk -->

### Herbal vinegars are made with apple cider or wine vinegar as a base. To make

<!-- chunk -->

### culinary vinegars, bruise freshly picked herbs and pack loosely in a jar. Cover

<!-- chunk -->

### with vinegar and cap with an acid-proof lid, or place parchment paper between

<!-- chunk -->

### the mouth of the jar and the metal lid. Set the vinegar in a sunny window and

<!-- chunk -->

### shake daily for 2 weeks. Test for flavor. If it is not strong enough, strain the

<!-- chunk -->

### vinegar and repeat the process with fresh herbs. Store as is or strain through

<!-- chunk -->

### cheesecloth and rebottle. (Add a fresh sprig of the herb to the bottle for

<!-- chunk -->

### identification and visual appeal.) Use culinary vinegars in salad dressings,

<!-- chunk -->

### marinades, gravies, and sauces.

<!-- chunk -->

### You can also make vinegars with fresh berries or flowers. To make a raspberry

<!-- chunk -->

### vinegar, steep 500 grams of raspberries in 1 liter of wine vinegar for 2 weeks,

<!-- chunk -->

### then strain. Berry vinegar can be added to cough remedies or used in gargles for

<!-- chunk -->

### sore throats, and the delightful flavor masks the taste of bitter herbal

<!-- chunk -->

### expectorants. To make floral vinegars, remove the stems and any green or white

<!-- chunk -->

### heels from the petals before steeping. Carnations, clover, elder flowers, lavender,

<!-- chunk -->

### and sweet violets make good vinegars.

<!-- chunk -->

### Medicinal vinegars are made with high-mineral herbs like horsetail, oat straw,

<!-- chunk -->

### alfalfa, nettles, and even eggshells. Vinegar leaches the minerals out of herbs and

<!-- chunk -->

### eggshells, and can be a great addition to a program for bone building.

<!-- chunk -->

### Fire Cider, a traditional remedy for cold and flu, is made using vinegar. See

<!-- chunk -->

### Chapter Twelve for a formula.

# USES AND APPLICATIONS OF VINEGARS

<!-- chunk -->

### Herbal vinegars are good for bone building and for use in salads and cooking

<!-- chunk -->

### applications. They help to regulate gut flora and improve function in the

<!-- chunk -->

### digestive tract. Vinegar extracts are generally not as strong as alcohol or glycerin

<!-- chunk -->

### preparations, so they require larger doses. However, they are inexpensive to

<!-- chunk -->

### make and are a great way to incorporate herbs into the diet.

<!-- chunk -->

### Vinegar is used as an addition to some tinctures to acidify the solution, and is

<!-- chunk -->

### particularly useful if you use hard water or if you are trying to extract alkaloids

<!-- chunk -->

### from the herb.

<!-- chunk -->

### Because herbal oils are nearly always intended for topical use, we explain

<!-- chunk -->

### how to extract herbs in fat and oil in Chapter Eight, “Topical Preparations.”


<!-- chunk -->

## CHAPTER SEVEN

# Advanced Extraction Techniques

<!-- chunk -->

## PERCOLATION EXTRACTS, FLUID EXTRACTS, AND

<!-- chunk -->

## SOXHLET EXTRACTS

<!-- chunk -->

### he techniques described in Chapter Six produce reasonably good herbal

<!-- chunk -->

### extracts. In this chapter we’ll cover making more potent extracts, including

<!-- chunk -->

### percolation extracts, fluid extracts, and Soxhlet extracts. All of these techniques

<!-- chunk -->

### require more equipment and setup than macerations and teas, but they are not so

<!-- chunk -->

### technical that you can’t do them at home.

# PERCOLATION EXTRACTS

<!-- chunk -->

### Percolation is the process of passing a solvent (such as alcohol) through plant

<!-- chunk -->

### material. As the solvent drips through coarsely powdered plant material, active

<!-- chunk -->

### constituents of the herb are extracted while inert components of the plant are left

<!-- chunk -->

### behind. Drip coffeemakers are a great example of a percolation extraction. The

<!-- chunk -->

### coffeemaker slowly drips hot water (the solvent) through coffee grounds to

<!-- chunk -->

### produce coffee. Making herbal percolation extracts is a very similar process,

<!-- chunk -->

### usually using a combination of water and alcohol as a solvent.

<!-- chunk -->

### Percolation extracts became popular in the mid-nineteenth century, and

<!-- chunk -->

### directions for making them can be found in older pharmaceutical texts.

<!-- chunk -->

### Herbalists favor percolation extracts over regular tinctures for a number of

<!-- chunk -->

### reasons:

<!-- chunk -->

### ▪ Experience suggests that for many herbs percolation results in a more complete

<!-- chunk -->

### and potent extract.

<!-- chunk -->

### ▪ Percolation extracts are usually ready in 2 days. Macerations take a minimum

<!-- chunk -->

### of 2 weeks.

<!-- chunk -->

### ▪ Percolations don’t require pressing or straining to extract the liquid.

<!-- chunk -->

### ▪ Percolations allow greater flexibility in deciding the strength of the extract. If

<!-- chunk -->

### you make a 1:3 percolation and decide that you want more tincture, you can

<!-- chunk -->

### pour fresh menstruum through the herb and turn it into a 1:4 or 1:5 extract.

<!-- chunk -->

### There are some drawbacks to making percolation extracts. You can’t use this

<!-- chunk -->

### technique to extract gums, resins, or herbs that contain a lot of mucilage; nor can

<!-- chunk -->

### you percolate fresh plant material. Making a percolation extract requires more

<!-- chunk -->

### hands-on time than a regular maceration or tincture.

<!-- chunk -->

### It is difficult to describe the process of percolation in words. In addition to the

<!-- chunk -->

### instructions below, you can watch a video demonstration at our website,

<!-- chunk -->

### modernherbalmedicine.com.

# EQUIPMENT NEEDED

<!-- chunk -->

### To make a percolation extract you will need the following equipment:

<!-- chunk -->

## Percolation cone. Search for “percolation cone” online to find retailers who sell

<!-- chunk -->

### them, or you can make your own. To make a percolation cone, use a glass cutter

<!-- chunk -->

### to cut off the bottom of a thick glass water bottle such as Perrier or San

<!-- chunk -->

### Pelligrino. Keep the screw-top lid so you can control the drip rate of the

<!-- chunk -->

### percolation. You can also use a wine bottle with a silicon stopper and a brass

<!-- chunk -->

### needle valve to control drip rate (instead of the bottle cap).

<!-- chunk -->

## Wide-mouth, half-gallon mason jar. This jar is used to hold the percolation

<!-- chunk -->

### cone and to collect the finished extract.

<!-- chunk -->

## Packing rod. You will need a long instrument with a broad end to tamp down

<!-- chunk -->

### the powdered herbs inside the percolation cone. Wooden dowels (muddlers) and

<!-- chunk -->

### large granite pestles work well.

<!-- chunk -->

## Filter. Use an unbleached coffee filter or cotton balls. These are used on the

<!-- chunk -->

### bottom of the percolation cone to hold the powder inside the cone, and coffee

<!-- chunk -->

### filters are also placed on top of the powdered herbs. Don’t use funnel-style

<!-- chunk -->

### coffee filters, which are glued together at the bottom. If you do, the alcohol will

<!-- chunk -->

### dissolve some of the glue and you’ll wind up with glue in your extract. Cotton

<!-- chunk -->

### balls can be inserted into the neck of the bottle and are the easiest filters to use.

<!-- chunk -->

## Weight. A small weight is needed to hold down the top filter. Marbles work very

<!-- chunk -->

### well for this purpose. Early texts called for washed sand, but we don’t

<!-- chunk -->

### recommend that. Whatever you use for a weight needs to be clean and

<!-- chunk -->

### nonporous.

<!-- chunk -->

## Lid. You will need a lid to cover the top of the percolation cone to prevent

<!-- chunk -->

### evaporation and contamination. A wide-mouth mason jar lid will fit most glass

<!-- chunk -->

### bottles, or you can use a plastic bag secured with rubber bands.

<!-- chunk -->

### Once you have all of these items, assemble the percolation cone as shown.

<!-- chunk -->

### Now it’s time to percolate.

# PREPARING THE PLANT MATERIAL

<!-- chunk -->

### You will need coarsely ground (powdered) dried herb for your percolation. A

<!-- chunk -->

### uniform powder allows the solvent to flow through evenly. If you grind the plant

<!-- chunk -->

### material yourself, pass it through a sieve with at least 30 holes per inch (#30

<!-- chunk -->

### sieve) to get an even powder.

<!-- chunk -->

### In any tincture or extract using dried herbs, some of the solvent will remain in

<!-- chunk -->

### the plant material when you are finished. If you put 1,000 milliliters of solvent

<!-- chunk -->

### into a percolation cone without premoistening, less than 900 milliliters will come

<!-- chunk -->

### out. This throws the weight-to-volume ratio off considerably. Before starting the

<!-- chunk -->

### percolation, you need to moisten the powdered herb with a small amount of

<!-- chunk -->

### menstruum.

<!-- chunk -->

### There are two ways to calculate the amount of solvent needed to moisten the

<!-- chunk -->

### plant material. The first method is to weigh the plant material and add 75% of

<!-- chunk -->

### that weight in solvent. For example, 100 grams of plant material requires

<!-- chunk -->

### milliliters of solvent to moisten it, and 100 ounces of plant material requires

<!-- chunk -->

### fluid ounces of solvent.

<!-- chunk -->

### The second calculation method is more accurate. Pack the powdered plant

<!-- chunk -->

### material into a liquid measuring container to determine its volume, adding a little

<!-- chunk -->

### bit of the herb at a time and tamping it down to ensure it is evenly packed. For

<!-- chunk -->

### instance, 100 grams (or ounces) by weight of plant material might take up

<!-- chunk -->

### milliliters (or fluid ounces) of volume. Add the amount of solvent the herb

<!-- chunk -->

### displaces by volume: In this example, add 120 milliliters or 120 fluid ounces of

<!-- chunk -->

### solvent.

<!-- chunk -->

### Mix all of the menstruum at once. This includes the desired final volume of

<!-- chunk -->

### the tincture plus the amount you calculated to moisten the herb. If you are

<!-- chunk -->

### preparing a 1:5 percolation with 100 ounces of herb, and the herb takes up

<!-- chunk -->

### ounces by volume, you need 500 ounce s plus 120 ounces of total menstruum.

<!-- chunk -->

### Slowly add menstruum to the plant material to moisten it. When the powder is

<!-- chunk -->

### properly moistened, it should be damp enough that it sticks together when you

<!-- chunk -->

### pinch it between your fingers, like wet sand. You can always add more liquid,

<!-- chunk -->

### but if you add too much you’ll have to add more powdered herbs, which will

<!-- chunk -->

### really throw off your calculations. You may end up with menstruum left over

<!-- chunk -->

### from the amount that you calculated; add this to the rest of the menstruum. If

<!-- chunk -->

### you calculated 120 ounces to moisten the herb and only used 100 ounces, add

<!-- chunk -->

### the remaining 20 ounces to the 500 ounces of menstruum that you are using for

<!-- chunk -->

### the percolation. Once the powdered herb is properly moistened, set it aside in an

<!-- chunk -->

### airtight container for 1–12 hours.

# PACKING THE PERCOLATION CONE

<!-- chunk -->

### Once the powdered herb has had time to properly absorb the menstruum, it’s

<!-- chunk -->

### time to prepare the percolation cone.

<!-- chunk -->

### Learning to pack a percolation cone requires practice. Use the cheapest herbs

<!-- chunk -->

### you can find for your first few tries. Licorice root is a good herb to practice with;

<!-- chunk -->

### not only is it inexpensive, but it packs well, and it’s always nice to have on hand.

<!-- chunk -->

## Step one: Put a filter at the bottom of the percolation cone. Many people use a

<!-- chunk -->

### coffee filter cut to size. Putting two organic cotton balls in the neck of the bottle

<!-- chunk -->

### works just as well and it’s a lot easier. After you put the cotton balls in, put your

<!-- chunk -->

### cap on loosely or put your stopper in, leaving your valve open.

<!-- chunk -->

## Step two: Fill the cone with the moistened herb powder. Divide the

<!-- chunk -->

### premoistened plant material into three equal parts. Put the first third of the

<!-- chunk -->

### powder in the cone and tamp it down evenly with the packing rod. Add the

<!-- chunk -->

### second third of the plant material and pack it down, and then add the remaining

<!-- chunk -->

### powder and tamp it down too. This is another skill that you will get better at with

<!-- chunk -->

### practice and experience. Don’t pack the plant material too tight or too loose: too

<!-- chunk -->

### tight and the menstruum won’t be able to get through; too loose and the

<!-- chunk -->

### menstruum won’t flow evenly through the powder.

<!-- chunk -->

## Step three: Cut a coffee filter so that it is slightly larger than the opening to the

<!-- chunk -->

### percolation cone. Set the coffee filter on top of the herb powder to prevent the

<!-- chunk -->

### packed plant material from being disturbed as you pour your menstruum on it.

<!-- chunk -->

### Put the washed weight on top of the filter to hold it in place.

<!-- chunk -->

## Step four: Set the percolation cone in the mason jar or in the stand, and add the

<!-- chunk -->

### menstruum to the cone. Cover the top of the percolation cone with a lid. Allow

<!-- chunk -->

### the menstruum to seep through the plant material and cotton balls so that it drips

<!-- chunk -->

### a few times. Then tighten your cap or close your valve. Letting the menstruum

<!-- chunk -->

### flow all the way through forces out air, and prevents an air bubble from burping

<!-- chunk -->

### up through your packed herb powders. Let this sit (macerate/digest) for 24 hours.

<!-- chunk -->

## Step five: After 24 hours, slightly loosen the cap of the percolation cone to

<!-- chunk -->

### allow a slow, steady drip of menstruum. About 1 drip every 3–5 seconds is ideal.

<!-- chunk -->

### Let the menstruum percolate through the marc until the menstruum stops

<!-- chunk -->

### flowing out of the percolation cone.

<!-- chunk -->

## Step six: Let the extracted liquid sit for 24 hours to allow precipitates to settle to

<!-- chunk -->

### the bottom. Decant the liquid, leaving the precipitates behind, and bottle the

<!-- chunk -->

### finished tincture.

# RE-PERCOLATION AND DUAL PERCOLATIONS

<!-- chunk -->

### Some of the old pharmacy texts recommend running the finished tincture back

<!-- chunk -->

### through the plant material a second time. This dual extract throws the weight-to-

<!-- chunk -->

### volume ratio off slightly and there isn’t a good formula to account for the

<!-- chunk -->

### difference. Dual extraction seems to increase the potency of the extract for many

<!-- chunk -->

### herbs, but doesn’t make a difference with others. Experiment and decide for

<!-- chunk -->

### yourself if these extra steps are worth it.

<!-- chunk -->

## Step seven: Once all of the menstruum has run through the percolation cone, set

<!-- chunk -->

### the extracted liquid aside in a covered container and put the percolation cone

<!-- chunk -->

### into a fresh mason jar. Remove the cap at the bottom of the cone and slowly pour

<!-- chunk -->

### boiled water through the cone. When the liquid dripping out of the cone is clear

<!-- chunk -->

### and tasteless, remove the cone and set it aside.

<!-- chunk -->

## Step eight: Put the water extract that percolated through the cone into a stainless

<!-- chunk -->

### steel or enamel pot. Simmer over low heat until the liquid is reduced by 90% of

<!-- chunk -->

### its original volume (1,000 milliliters of water extract would be reduced to

<!-- chunk -->

### milliliters, or 100 fluid ounces to 10 fluid ounces).

<!-- chunk -->

## Step nine: Add the concentrated water extract back to the original percolation

<!-- chunk -->

### extract and mix them together. Let the mixture sit for 24 hours, then decant the

<!-- chunk -->

### liquid, leaving the precipitates behind. Bottle the finished tincture.

# FLUID EXTRACTS

<!-- chunk -->

### Fluid extracts are tinctures that are concentrated to a ratio of 1:1. They are made

<!-- chunk -->

### by percolation, with some added steps.

<!-- chunk -->

### To make a fluid extract, use the standard 1:5 weight-to-volume ratio but

<!-- chunk -->

### increase the volume of alcohol by 20%. If the herb is normally extracted using

<!-- chunk -->

### 50% alcohol, this means increasing the percentage of alcohol to 70%. The added

<!-- chunk -->

### alcohol makes up for the water that will be added later during the process.

<!-- chunk -->

### Set up the percolation and allow the equivalent of 75% of the weight of the

<!-- chunk -->

### herb to percolate through. If you used 100 grams of herb, you would allow

<!-- chunk -->

### milliliters to percolate through; if you used 10 ounces of herb, that would be 7.5

<!-- chunk -->

### fluid ounces. Set this liquid aside.

<!-- chunk -->

### Continue percolating the remaining menstruum, then loosen the cap on the

<!-- chunk -->

### percolation cone and flush the marc with boiling hot water. Transfer the

<!-- chunk -->

### remaining percolated menstruum and the water to a shallow pan and evaporate in

<!-- chunk -->

### a food dehydrator or in an oven on low heat until the liquid is reduced to 25% of

<!-- chunk -->

### the volume of herb. Using the above examples, that’s 25 milliliters or 2.5 fluid

<!-- chunk -->

### ounces. Combine this 25% reduction with the 75% percolated menstruum that

<!-- chunk -->

### you set aside earlier. You now have a 1:1 fluid extract.

# FLUID EXTRACTS STEP-BY-STEP

<!-- chunk -->

### Here’s a step-by-step guide to making a 1:1 fluid extract of calendula.

<!-- chunk -->

## Step one: Calendula is normally tinctured using 70% alcohol. Add 20 to 70% to

<!-- chunk -->

### get 90%. Prepare a menstruum that is 90% alcohol and 10% water.

<!-- chunk -->

## Step two: Calculate the amount of menstruum based on a 1:5 ratio. For example,

<!-- chunk -->

### 5 ounces of calendula would need 25 fluid ounces of menstruum. (The final

<!-- chunk -->

### product will yield 5 fluid ounces of a 1:1 fluid extract of calendula.)

<!-- chunk -->

## Step three: Calculate 75% (by volume) of the weight of the herb. For instance,

<!-- chunk -->

### 75% of 5 fluid ounces is 3.75 ounces. Measure out 3.75 ounces of water and

<!-- chunk -->

### pour it into the jar you will be using to catch the percolated menstruum. Use a

<!-- chunk -->

### permanent marker to mark the water level on the outside of the jar, then empty

<!-- chunk -->

### the water from the jar. (We’ll call the marked jar Container A.)

<!-- chunk -->

## Step four: Set up the percolation cone and start the percolation process, using

<!-- chunk -->

### Container A to catch the percolated liquid. When the liquid in Container A

<!-- chunk -->

### reaches the line that you marked in step three, tighten the cap on the percolation

<!-- chunk -->

### cone to stop the flow. Transfer the 3.75 ounces of liquid into another container

<!-- chunk -->

### (Container B) and set aside.

<!-- chunk -->

## Step five: Return Container A to its place under the percolation cone and

<!-- chunk -->

### continue the percolation. When the menstruum has finished dripping through,

<!-- chunk -->

### loosen the cap on the cone. Flush water-soluble constituents out of the marc by

<!-- chunk -->

### pouring boiling hot water through the percolation cone until the water runs clear.

<!-- chunk -->

## Step six: Calculate 25% of the desired final volume of the fluid extract. In our

<!-- chunk -->

### example, 25% of 5 ounces is 1.25 ounces. Follow the instructions in step three to

<!-- chunk -->

### mark this volume on a shallow container (Container C).

<!-- chunk -->

## Step seven: Transfer the liquid from Container A to Container C. Put Container

<!-- chunk -->

### C in a food dehydrator or an oven set on the lowest temperature. Check the

<!-- chunk -->

### liquid frequently until it has evaporated to the mark you made, leaving you with

<!-- chunk -->

### 1.25 fluid ounces of extract.

<!-- chunk -->

## Step eight: Combine the liquids from Container B (3.75 fluid ounces) and

<!-- chunk -->

### Container C (1.25 fluid ounces). You now have 5 fluid ounces of an extract made

<!-- chunk -->

### from 5 ounces of calendula, which means you have a 1:1 extract.

<!-- chunk -->

### Congratulations! You have made a fluid extract.

<!-- chunk -->

### Only a few herbs respond well to concentration without changing the relative

<!-- chunk -->

### ratio of constituents, and most herbs are not given in such a big dose that

<!-- chunk -->

### concentration is necessary. Some herbs that do work well as fluid extracts

<!-- chunk -->

### include Corydalis yanhusuo, calendula, turmeric, Echinacea angustifolia root,

<!-- chunk -->

### eleuthero, licorice, black walnut, passionflower, Jamaican dogwood, willow

<!-- chunk -->

### bark, prickly ash, kava kava, dandelion, and barberry.

# SOXHLET EXTRACTION

<!-- chunk -->

### The Soxhlet extractor is a piece of lab equipment that was developed in 1879 to

<!-- chunk -->

### facilitate the continual extraction of plant material and create very concentrated

<!-- chunk -->

### extracts. In a Soxhlet extractor, the menstruum is boiled, evaporated, and

<!-- chunk -->

### allowed to condense. As it condenses, it drips through the plant material to

<!-- chunk -->

### extract constituents. This extract returns to the heated container, where it is

<!-- chunk -->

### boiled, evaporated, and condensed again to drip through the plant material and

<!-- chunk -->

### extract more constituents.

<!-- chunk -->

### A Soxhlet extractor with a 500 milliliter boiling flask will hold about

<!-- chunk -->

### grams of plant material. One run through the extractor will produce a standard

<!-- chunk -->

### 1:5 tincture. If you replace the exhausted marc with 100 grams of fresh plant

<!-- chunk -->

### material and extract that using the original menstruum, you get a 1:2.5 extract.

<!-- chunk -->

### Repeat the process a third time to get a 1:1.25 extract.

<!-- chunk -->

### It takes a day or two for a Soxhlet extractor to make such a concentrated

<!-- chunk -->

### extract, but once you’ve set it up it requires only occasional monitoring.

# MATERIALS FOR MAKING SOXHLET EXTRACTS

<!-- chunk -->

### A high-quality, American-made Soxhlet extractor can set you back $500. You

<!-- chunk -->

### can sometimes find cheaper, Chinese-made Soxhlet extractors on eBay for less

<!-- chunk -->

### than $100. Cheaper glassware means thinner glass, which means you’ll have to

<!-- chunk -->

### be more careful when handling it. With the less-expensive glassware, you can set

<!-- chunk -->

### up a complete Soxhlet extractor for around $200. You should be able to find all

<!-- chunk -->

### of the components on the internet. Here’s what you’ll need.

<!-- chunk -->

## ▪ A lab stand to hold the Soxhlet extractor, with a heavy base for stability. Look

<!-- chunk -->

### for a cast iron support ring stand.

<!-- chunk -->

## ▪ Lab clamps to secure the Soxhlet extractor to the lab stand. Get the kind with

<!-- chunk -->

### three prongs.

<!-- chunk -->

## ▪ A submersible recirculating pump is used to pump ice water into the

<!-- chunk -->

### condenser at the top of the Soxhlet extractor. A submersible pump like a fish

<!-- chunk -->

### tank pump that can pump 3.8 liters is sufficient.

<!-- chunk -->

## ▪ A DC power supply to run the pump (unless you get a 110-volt pump).

<!-- chunk -->

## ▪ Plastic tubing to connect the pump to the condenser on the Soxhlet extractor.

<!-- chunk -->

## You can get tubing from any hardware store. Make sure to get hose clamps as

<!-- chunk -->

### well.

<!-- chunk -->

## ▪ An insul ated container to fill with ice water and connect to the tubing.

<!-- chunk -->

## ▪ A heat source to put under the Soxhlet extractor to boil the menstruum. The

<!-- chunk -->

### safest (and most expensive) option is a heating mantle with a magnetic stirrer.

<!-- chunk -->

### Prices start at $200. A $20 fondue cooker filled with vegetable oil will hold the

<!-- chunk -->

### temperature within a few degrees of where you want it.

<!-- chunk -->

## ▪ Boiling stones ensure an even boil of the menstruum. They go in the boiling

<!-- chunk -->

### flask that holds the menstruum. Look for stones that are 4–6 millimeters in

<!-- chunk -->

### diameter.

<!-- chunk -->

## ▪ A digital or laser thermometer to monitor the temperature of the oil bath in the

<!-- chunk -->

### fondue cooker.

# SOXHLET EXTRACTION STEP-BY-STEP

<!-- chunk -->

## Step one: Prepare the heat source. If you are using a fondue cooker, fill it with

<!-- chunk -->

### vegetable oil. (Oil doesn’t evaporate, so it requires less watching than a water

<!-- chunk -->

### bath.) The oil can be reused to make multiple batches of extracts. It will start to

<!-- chunk -->

### smell rancid after a few uses, but you can add 1 drop rosemary essential oil to

<!-- chunk -->

### mask the smell.

<!-- chunk -->

## Step two: Moisten the plant material using the same method described for

<!-- chunk -->

### percolations earlier in this chapter. Allow the moistened powder to sit for at least

<!-- chunk -->

### 4 hours before beginning the extraction.

<!-- chunk -->

## Step three: The plant material goes in the middle section of the Soxhlet

<!-- chunk -->

### extractor. Put a filter at the bottom of this middle piece. Try different types of

<!-- chunk -->

### filters: Some options include lab filter paper, organic unbleached cotton balls,

<!-- chunk -->

### and organic unbleached facial cleansing pads.

<!-- chunk -->

## Step four: Spoon the moistened plant material on top of the filter in the middle

<!-- chunk -->

### piece of the Soxhlet extractor. Clamp the middle piece of the Soxhlet extractor to

<!-- chunk -->

### the lab stand.

<!-- chunk -->

## Step five: Fill the flat-bottomed boiling flask 80% full with the menstruum. This

<!-- chunk -->

### can be straight alcohol or a mixture of alcohol and water. Add a teaspoon of

<!-- chunk -->

### boiling stones to the flask.

<!-- chunk -->

## Step six: Grease all of the joints on the Soxhlet extractor with a thin layer of

<!-- chunk -->

### shea butter, cocoa butter, or a natural oil. Lubricate both of the pieces that are

<!-- chunk -->

### joined together to prevent the pieces of glass from getting irreversibly stuck

<!-- chunk -->

### together.

<!-- chunk -->

## Step seven: Slide the boiling flask into place below the Soxhlet extractor, and fit

<!-- chunk -->

### the joints together. Lower the Soxhlet extractor into the oil bath or heating

<!-- chunk -->

### mantle. Attach the condenser to the top of the extraction chamber. (Make sure

<!-- chunk -->

### the joints are greased!)

<!-- chunk -->

## Step eight: Connect the plastic tubing to the condenser. The input line attaches

<!-- chunk -->

### at the bottom of the condenser, and the output line attaches at the top. Use hose

<!-- chunk -->

### clamps to secure the tubing connections.

<!-- chunk -->

## Step nine: Put the loose end of the output line from the condenser in an

<!-- chunk -->

### insulated container filled with ice water. Run a line from the insulated container

<!-- chunk -->

### to the input line of the water pump. Connect the input line from the condenser to

<!-- chunk -->

### the output line of the pump. Turn the pump on to start circulating ice water

<!-- chunk -->

### through the condenser.

<!-- chunk -->

## Step ten: Turn the heat source on. If you are extracting alcohol-soluble

<!-- chunk -->

### constituents, set the temperature at 195°. To extract both alcohol- and water-

<!-- chunk -->

### soluble constituents, set the temperature at 220°. Check the temperature

<!-- chunk -->

### occasionally while the Soxhlet extractor is running, adjusting as needed to keep

<!-- chunk -->

### it within 5° on either side of the optimal temperature.

<!-- chunk -->

## Step eleven: Check the ice water bath every few hours, adding ice packs as

<!-- chunk -->

### needed to keep the temperature below 45°.

<!-- chunk -->

## Step twelve: Once the menstruum runs clear through the reflux tube, turn the

<!-- chunk -->

### heat source off and let everything cool down. Disassemble the Soxhlet extractor

<!-- chunk -->

### and replace the exhausted plant material with fresh material. Set the exhausted

<!-- chunk -->

### marc aside in a separate container and keep it covered in the refrigerator until the

<!-- chunk -->

### extraction is complete.

<!-- chunk -->

## Step thirteen: Put the Soxhlet back together and start the oil bath again. Repeat

<!-- chunk -->

### the process. You can do this as many times as you need to until you reach the

<!-- chunk -->

### desired concentration.

<!-- chunk -->

## Step fourteen: To extract water-soluble constituents, put all of the exhausted

<!-- chunk -->

### marc into a pot and decoct it. Strain the decoction and then simmer it to

<!-- chunk -->

### concentrate it down to about 2 ounces. Add this back to the alcohol extract to

<!-- chunk -->

### produce the finished Soxhlet extract.

<!-- chunk -->

### Soxhlet extraction in some cases doesn’t produce better tinctures than

<!-- chunk -->

### percolation or maceration. It seems to work best when a plant has difficult-to-

<!-- chunk -->

### extract alcohol-soluble compounds and lipids. Calamus, angelica, lemon balm,

<!-- chunk -->

### spearmint, and blue vervain all make exceptional Soxhlet extracts.

<!-- chunk -->

### Making percolation extracts, fluid extracts, and Soxhlet extracts is more

<!-- chunk -->

### complicated than making a standard alcohol tincture, but these techniques

<!-- chunk -->

### produce more potent extracts, so you get more value from the same amount of

<!-- chunk -->

### plant material and you can use smaller doses. Don’t be afraid to experiment.


<!-- chunk -->

## CHAPTER EIGHT

# Topical Preparations

<!-- chunk -->

## OIL-BASED EXTRACTIONS, TOPICAL APPLICATIONS, AND

<!-- chunk -->

## LOCAL APPLICATIONS

<!-- chunk -->

### here are many topical (local) applications of herbs. In this chapter we

<!-- chunk -->

### discuss how to make oil- or fat-based extractions and turn them into salves,

<!-- chunk -->

### ointments, lotions, and other topical preparations. In the second half of the

<!-- chunk -->

### chapter, we discuss techniques for applying bulk herbs and various extracts

<!-- chunk -->

### topically.

# OIL-BASED HERBAL PREPARATIONS

<!-- chunk -->

### Oil-based preparations of herbs are suited for minor skin problems like

<!-- chunk -->

### abrasions, burns, rashes, and dry skin. Be careful with new cuts or wounds,

<!-- chunk -->

### especially if the wound is deep; oil can trap bacteria and help them spread. For

<!-- chunk -->

### active infections, direct application of herbs in a poultice or indirect applications

<!-- chunk -->

### like compresses and soaks work best.

<!-- chunk -->

### Compresses are made by soaking a cloth in an herbal tea or decoction and

<!-- chunk -->

### applying the cloth to the affected area; soaks are done by submersing an area in

<!-- chunk -->

### an herbal tea or decoction (compresses are covered later in this chapter, and

<!-- chunk -->

### soaks are discussed in Appendix One). After a couple of days of healing, if the

<!-- chunk -->

### wound shows no sign of infection, salves are more convenient than poultices and

<!-- chunk -->

### compresses. Salves work really well for the proliferative and remodeling phases

<!-- chunk -->

### of tissue healing.

# EXTRACTING HERBS IN OIL OR FAT

<!-- chunk -->

### In an oil extract (olea medicata in Latin), herbs are macerated in a fixed oil to

<!-- chunk -->

### extract constituents that are soluble in fats. Olive oil, grape seed oil, almond oil,

<!-- chunk -->

### peanut oil, and apricot oil are commonly used to make herbal oils. In the past,

<!-- chunk -->

### herbs were frequently extracted in saturated fats such as lard or butter. Oil isn’t a

<!-- chunk -->

### great solvent on its own. Using gentle heat or an intermediate solvent like

<!-- chunk -->

### alcohol produces a better oil. Making an herbal oil is the first step to making an

<!-- chunk -->

### oil-based salve, as described later in this chapter.

<!-- chunk -->

## Herbal Oils (Oil Extractions)

<!-- chunk -->

### There are three primary methods for creating an herbal oil. The first is similar to

<!-- chunk -->

### making a tincture, the second is like making a low-temperature decoction, and

<!-- chunk -->

### the final method uses intermediate solvents to produce the oil.

<!-- chunk -->

## Cold Extraction Pack herbs loosely in a glass jar. Add enough oil to barely

<!-- chunk -->

### cover the plant material. Put the jar in a warm, dark area for 14 days, shaking or

<!-- chunk -->

### stirring daily, then strain and bottle the oil.

<!-- chunk -->

### Most oils made with fresh plants will grow mold due to the water content of

<!-- chunk -->

### the plants. Two herbs that can be used fresh to make herbal oils are mullein

<!-- chunk -->

### flowers and St. John’s wort flowers. Gather the flowers and place them loosely

<!-- chunk -->

### into a jar. Cover them with oil and macerate for 14 days as described above.

<!-- chunk -->

### Some herbs commonly infused in oil after drying include garlic, lobelia seeds,

<!-- chunk -->

### and poke root. Other plant materials should be dried for about 48 hours and

<!-- chunk -->

### extracted using the hot extraction method.

<!-- chunk -->

## Hot Extraction The 1898 King’s American Dispensatory recommends

<!-- chunk -->

### maintaining heat between 122° and 140° to produce medicinal oils. You can use

<!-- chunk -->

### the lowest setting on a slow cooker or oven, which will hold the temperature at

<!-- chunk -->

### about 150°. Extracting at 150° rather than between 122° and 140° won’t damage

<!-- chunk -->

### the plant compounds, but oils that are heated above 150° tend to go rancid faster

<!-- chunk -->

### than otherwise. To keep the temperature within the range given by King’s

<!-- chunk -->

### American Dispensatory, prop a wooden spoon against the door of the oven to

<!-- chunk -->

### keep it slightly ajar. If you are using a slow cooker, you can have an electrician

<!-- chunk -->

### wire a light dimmer into the power cord to control the temperature more

<!-- chunk -->

### precisely. Use a thermometer to monitor the temperature.

<!-- chunk -->

### To extract herbs in a slow cooker, put them in the crock and add enough oil to

<!-- chunk -->

### barely cover the herbs (approximately 1 gallon of oil to 1 pound of herbs). Turn

<!-- chunk -->

### the slow cooker on low and let the herbs steep overnight, or for 8–12 hours. Let

<!-- chunk -->

### the oil cool, then strain and bottle it.

<!-- chunk -->

### If you are using an oven, use a large enamel pan to hold the herbs. Cover them

<!-- chunk -->

### in oil. Set the oven to the lowest temperature and prop the oven door open with a

<!-- chunk -->

### wooden spoon for ventilation. Keep the herbs in the oven for 8–12 hours, allow

<!-- chunk -->

### the oil to cool, then strain and bottle.

<!-- chunk -->

### To make a hot extract using fresh plants, keep the plant material in the oven or

<!-- chunk -->

### crock until all the liquid is cooked out of the fresh plant material and the herbs

<!-- chunk -->

### are crispy.

<!-- chunk -->

## Using Intermediate Solvents To make a more potent oil, you can use high-proof

<!-- chunk -->

### alcohol as an intermediate solvent. Measure the amount of plant material by

<!-- chunk -->

### weight and add 75% of that weight in 95% alcohol to the plant material. For

<!-- chunk -->

### grams of plant material, use 150 milliliters of 95% alcohol, and for 16 ounces of

<!-- chunk -->

### herb add 12 fluid ounces of 95% alcohol. Let this sit for 24 hours, then add the

<!-- chunk -->

### oil at a 1:5 ratio and apply gentle heat for 8–12 hours as described above. All of

<!-- chunk -->

### the alcohol will evaporate. For a 1:5 oil extraction of 200 grams of plant

<!-- chunk -->

### material, you need 1,000 grams by weight of oil, or 5 pounds of oil to 1 pound of

<!-- chunk -->

### herbs. This kind of precise measurement is not necessary for making herbal

<!-- chunk -->

### products for use by family and friends.

# SALVES, OINTMENTS, AND BALMS

<!-- chunk -->

### Salves are semi-solid medicinal preparations made of an herbal oil base that is

<!-- chunk -->

### solidified with beeswax or candelilla wax (a vegan alternative). Salves can also

<!-- chunk -->

### be called ointments. Herbal salves are used for scrapes, burns, skin irritations,

<!-- chunk -->

### and other skin conditions. They are used to carry herbs directly to the tissues that

<!-- chunk -->

### need them, or to deliver compounds like volatile oils to a referred site (like

<!-- chunk -->

### massaging a salve into the upper back and chest to affect the lungs). Some salves

<!-- chunk -->

### act as drawing agents to pull splinters and glass out of skin.

<!-- chunk -->

### To make a salve, make an herbal oil as described above. Use a 6:1–8:1 ratio of

<!-- chunk -->

### oil to beeswax, depending on how hard you want your salve. For softer salves

<!-- chunk -->

### use less wax, and for harder salves use more. That’s around 30 grams (1 ounce)

<!-- chunk -->

### of wax by weight for every 170–220 milliliters (6–8 ounces) by volume of oil.

<!-- chunk -->

### Wax pellets and grated wax are easier to melt than chunks of wax. (Use hot

<!-- chunk -->

### water to melt the wax off the grater.) Melt the beeswax in a jar or can that is

<!-- chunk -->

### dedicated to melting wax so you don’t have to deal with getting wax off the sides

<!-- chunk -->

### of a pot. Fill a large pot halfway with water and set a few canning jar rings on

<!-- chunk -->

### the bottom of the pot. Set the container full of beeswax on the canning rings so

<!-- chunk -->

### that it does not touch the bottom of the pot. Simmer the water over medium-high

<!-- chunk -->

### heat to melt the wax. While the beeswax is melting, warm up the oil in a

<!-- chunk -->

### nonreactive stainless steel or enamel pot. When the wax is completely melted,

<!-- chunk -->

### pour the hot wax into the warm oil.

<!-- chunk -->

### Another way to make salves is to pour beeswax pellets or grated beeswax

<!-- chunk -->

### directly into the hot oil. Stir regularly for about 20 minutes until the wax is

<!-- chunk -->

### completely melted. Keep an eye on the wax to keep it from bursting into flames.

<!-- chunk -->

### Once the oil and wax are blended together, remove them from the heat. Stir

<!-- chunk -->

### the liquid salve as it cools to make sure that the wax and oil do not separate. As

<!-- chunk -->

### the mixture cools, it will begin to turn translucent, at which point it is ready to

<!-- chunk -->

### put into jars. Add heat-sensitive ingredients like essential oils, vitamin E, and

<!-- chunk -->

### lanolin at this point, if desired.

<!-- chunk -->

### Cleaning up equipment that was used to make salve is tedious. Wipe down the

<!-- chunk -->

### equipment with paper towels while the salve is still liquid, and be prepared to

<!-- chunk -->

### wash everything in very hot soapy water a few times to get the equipment

<!-- chunk -->

### completely clean.

<!-- chunk -->

### There is a basic salve formula in Chapter Twelve to get you started.

<!-- chunk -->

### Balms are made with more beeswax than salves are: 30 grams (1 ounce) by

<!-- chunk -->

### weight to 80–110 milliliters (3–4 ounces) by volume of oil. The beeswax creates

<!-- chunk -->

### a protective barrier on the skin.

# LOTIONS

<!-- chunk -->

### Lotions are mixtures of oil and water that are used to moisturize dry skin. Water

<!-- chunk -->

### and oil do not naturally mix and must be emulsified. Emulsifiers are also known

<!-- chunk -->

### as surface active agents or surfactants, which will cause oil and water to stay

<!-- chunk -->

### mixed. For example, when blended with oil, the lecithin in egg yolk creates the

<!-- chunk -->

### stabilized emulsion known as mayonnaise.

<!-- chunk -->

### Commercial lotions usually contain highly processed chemical emulsifiers.

<!-- chunk -->

### Emulsifying wax is a reasonably safe chemical emulsifier, or you can make an

<!-- chunk -->

### emulsifier.

<!-- chunk -->

### Lotions need to include a preservative to prevent mold and bacteria growth.

<!-- chunk -->

### Rosemary essential oil and vitamin E oil are both antibacterial, and can be added

<!-- chunk -->

### to extend the shelf life of your lotions to well beyond 6 months.

<!-- chunk -->

### To make a lotion, melt the following ingredients together in a pan:

<!-- chunk -->

### 2 parts of a liquid fixed oil (grape seed oil, olive oil, or almond oil)

<!-- chunk -->

### 1 part of a solid fixed oil (cocoa butter, coconut oil, mango butter, or shea

<!-- chunk -->

### butter)

<!-- chunk -->

### 1 part emulsifier

<!-- chunk -->

### Stir in 4–6 parts of one of the following: an herbal infusion, aloe vera juice, or

<!-- chunk -->

### a hydrosol like rose water. Add 1 drop rosemary essential oil for every

<!-- chunk -->

### milliliters (4 ounces) of liquid, and add 1 milliliter of vitamin E oil per

<!-- chunk -->

### milliliters (8 ounces) of liquid. You can blend the mixture by hand with a whisk

<!-- chunk -->

### if you happen to be an Olympic level athlete. For us mere mortals, a stick

<!-- chunk -->

### blender or a strong countertop blender works a lot better.

# HOMEMADE EMULSIFIER

80% beeswax 10% borax 10% liquid lecithin This emulsifier will not keep for long and needs to be made fresh with each batch of lotion.

# BUTTERS

<!-- chunk -->

### Body butters are a whipped blend of saturated oils (solid at room temperature)

<!-- chunk -->

### and monounsaturated or polyunsaturated oils (liquid at room temperature). Body

<!-- chunk -->

### butters aren’t usually made with water (although they can be), so they last longer

<!-- chunk -->

### than lotions and have a creamy-smooth feel without the hassle of adding an

<!-- chunk -->

### emulsifier. (If you use a hydrosol or aloe vera gel to make body butter, you will

<!-- chunk -->

### need to add an emulsifier.)

<!-- chunk -->

### To make a butter, use:

<!-- chunk -->

### 7 parts by weight of a solid fixed oil

<!-- chunk -->

### 3 parts by weight of a liquid fixed oil

<!-- chunk -->

### Slowly melt the oils together in a double boiler. Remove the oil from the heat,

<!-- chunk -->

### add essential oils, and let the oil cool to room temperature. Whip the mixture

<!-- chunk -->

### into a buttery consistency with a hand or stand mixer. Refrigerate for 30 minutes

<!-- chunk -->

### and whip again. See Chapter Twelve for a sample formula.

# MASSAGE OILS

<!-- chunk -->

### Massage oils are made with carrier oils with essential oils added for fragrance

<!-- chunk -->

### and therapeutic action. Depending on the person and the oils used, massage oil

<!-- chunk -->

### can have a relaxing or stimulating effect on the mind and spirit. Massage oils

<!-- chunk -->

### help to relieve stress and tension by their effect on nerve endings, the mind, and

<!-- chunk -->

### muscles. Oils and massage can improve skin cell growth and accelerate the

<!-- chunk -->

### elimination of wastes through the body’s lymphatic system. See Chapter Ten for

<!-- chunk -->

### more information on using essential oils.

# OTHER TOPICAL APPLICATIONS

<!-- chunk -->

### Alcohol tinctures, glycerites, teas, and decoctions can be used topically.

# LINIMENTS

<!-- chunk -->

### Liniments are made in the same way as macerated tinctures, but with rubbing

<!-- chunk -->

### alcohol instead of drinkable ethanol. This cuts down on cost. It also makes the

<!-- chunk -->

### liniment toxic for internal use; many use regular alcohol tinctures topically as

<!-- chunk -->

### liniments to avoid the risk of poisoning. Label liniments that are made with

<!-- chunk -->

### rubbing alcohol to prevent accidental ingestion. Menthol crystals or wintergreen

<!-- chunk -->

### add a nice smell, increase the anti-inflammatory properties of the liniment, and

<!-- chunk -->

### help prevent accidental ingestion. The Antispasmodic Tincture in Chapter

<!-- chunk -->

### Twelve makes a good liniment.

# COMPRESSES AND FOMENTATIONS

<!-- chunk -->

### A compress is a cloth pad soaked in an herbal extract and applied to the skin.

<!-- chunk -->

### Most compresses are applied warm, but cold compresses have their applications.

<!-- chunk -->

### Compresses are used to ease pain or accelerate the healing of wounds or muscle

<!-- chunk -->

### injuries. Cold compresses are used for headaches, burns, bites, and stings.

<!-- chunk -->

### Infusions, decoctions, and tinctures or glycerites diluted with water may be used

<!-- chunk -->

### for a compress. The pad may be soft cotton or linen, a cotton ball, or woven

<!-- chunk -->

### gauze.

<!-- chunk -->

### To prepare a compress:

<!-- chunk -->

### ▪ Submerge a clean piece of soft cloth in a warm infusion or other herbal extract.

<!-- chunk -->

### ▪ Squeeze out the excess liquid.

<!-- chunk -->

### ▪ Place the pad against the affected area.

<!-- chunk -->

### ▪ When the pad cools or dries, repeat the process.

<!-- chunk -->

### Fomentations are compresses that cover a larger area of the body. Soak a cloth

<!-- chunk -->

### in hot tea and apply it to the affected area. Herbs that make good compresses and

<!-- chunk -->

### fomentations include chamomile, elder flowers and leaves, yarrow, and

<!-- chunk -->

### calendula.

# POULTICES AND PLASTERS

<!-- chunk -->

### A plaster or poultice (also known as a cataplasm) is a mixture of dried or fresh

<!-- chunk -->

### herbs moistened with water or oil and applied externally. It is similar to a

<!-- chunk -->

### compress, but plant parts are used rather than a liquid extraction. Generally a

<!-- chunk -->

### poultice implies a hot treatment, whereas a plaster is applied at room

<!-- chunk -->

### temperature. Smash or chop fresh herbs and apply them directly to the affected

<!-- chunk -->

### area. If you’re using dried herbs, boil them for up to 5 minutes or mix them with

<!-- chunk -->

### a small amount of boiling water. Apply the poultice or plaster in between layers

<!-- chunk -->

### of gauze to prevent messes.

<!-- chunk -->

### Poultices should be applied as hot as can be tolerated. Use a gauze bandage

<!-- chunk -->

### covered with a warm cloth over the poultice.

<!-- chunk -->

### Good herbs for making plasters and poultices include slippery elm, comfrey

<!-- chunk -->

### leaf and root, marshmallow, flaxseed, white oak bark, psyllium seeds, plantain,

<!-- chunk -->

### lily of the valley, pine gum, lobelia, calendula, yarrow, goldenseal, and aloe vera.

<!-- chunk -->

### In addition to herbs, you can use fine clay and activated charcoal in plasters and

<!-- chunk -->

### poultices. There is a basic poultice formula in Chapter Twelve.

<!-- chunk -->

### Prickly pear cactus (nopal, Opuntia ficus-indica) is a great herb to use as a

<!-- chunk -->

### poultice. Its actions are similar to those of aloe vera gel. Most ethnic grocery

<!-- chunk -->

### stores sell nopal pads with the spines removed; if you harvest a fresh prickly

<!-- chunk -->

### pear pad, you will have to remove the spines yourself. Prickly pear cacti have

<!-- chunk -->

### two types of spines: large fixed spines and small, hair-like glochids that can

<!-- chunk -->

### work their way into the skin, causing general irritation and misery. Remove the

<!-- chunk -->

### glochids from the pads by scraping them off with a knife; if you roast the pads

<!-- chunk -->

### over an open flame first, it becomes much easier. Cut the pads in half vertically

<!-- chunk -->

### to expose the gooey inside, warm the pads, and apply them directly over wounds

<!-- chunk -->

### and burns. The goo can be scraped out, dehydrated on a fruit leather tray, then

<!-- chunk -->

### powdered and reconstituted as needed.

# LOCAL APPLICATIONS

<!-- chunk -->

### These are applications made to specific parts of the body, such as the rectum,

<!-- chunk -->

### vagina, mouth, nose, eyes, and ears.

# SUPPOSITORY, PESSARY, OR BOLUS

<!-- chunk -->

### A suppository is used to insert herbs rectally or vaginally. A pessary is another

<!-- chunk -->

### word for a vaginal suppository. A bolus is administered orally.

<!-- chunk -->

### To prepare a suppository or pessary, melt cocoa butter on low heat, preferably

<!-- chunk -->

### in a double boiler, and stir in finely powdered herbs. The last thing you want to

<!-- chunk -->

### do is insert rough, scratchy plant material in a sensitive area, so make sure the

<!-- chunk -->

### herbs are very finely powdered.

<!-- chunk -->

### Add essential oils, if desired, and pour the herb-infused cocoa butter into a

<!-- chunk -->

### suppository mold and let it cool. Wrap each suppository individually with waxed

<!-- chunk -->

### paper and store it in the refrigerator. Insert rectally or vaginally depending on

<!-- chunk -->

### need. Use suppositories within 7–10 days, or keep in the freezer for up to

<!-- chunk -->

### months.

<!-- chunk -->

### To make a bolus, mix a binding herb like marshmallow or comfrey root with

<!-- chunk -->

### other herbs to form a stiff paste. Shape the paste into pieces the size of large pills

<!-- chunk -->

### and wrap with waxed paper. Boluses must be used immediately.

<!-- chunk -->

### Herbs for suppositories and boluses include comfrey root, marshmallow,

<!-- chunk -->

### Oregon grape, and cranesbill (geranium).

<!-- chunk -->

## Making a Suppository Mold

<!-- chunk -->

### To make a suppository mold, wrap a pencil, a slim marker, or the handle of a

<!-- chunk -->

### wooden spoon in several layers of aluminum foil. Slide the foil off and seal one

<!-- chunk -->

### end of the foil mold. Make several of these and stand them together in a can or

<!-- chunk -->

### jar. When the suppository mixture is ready, fill each tube and freeze for

<!-- chunk -->

### minutes. Remove the foil and slice the suppositories into pieces 2 centimeters (¾

<!-- chunk -->

### inch) long.

# GARGLES AND MOUTHWASHES

<!-- chunk -->

### Herbal gargles and mouthwashes are used for sore throats, laryngitis, tonsillitis,

<!-- chunk -->

### irritated throats, dry coughs, and bad breath. Make a decoction or mix tinctures

<!-- chunk -->

### or glycerites with water.

<!-- chunk -->

## Suggested Ingredients for Herbal Mouthwashes and Gargles

<!-- chunk -->

## Antiseptics and disinfectants: Myrrh, barberry, thyme, clove

<!-- chunk -->

## Astringents: Bayberry, white oak bark, sage, calendula

<!-- chunk -->

## Demulcents (for dry, irritated throats): Licorice, slippery elm, marshmallow

<!-- chunk -->

## Flavoring agents: Spearmint, peppermint, anise, fennel, licorice, clove,

<!-- chunk -->

### cinnamon

<!-- chunk -->

## Stimulants: Capsicum (used in very small amounts), ginger, thyme

# HERBAL SNUFF

<!-- chunk -->

### A snuff is used to dry up excess mucus in the sinuses or to shrink nasal polyps.

<!-- chunk -->

### Take a pinch of powdered herb in the palm of your hand. Use your other hand to

<!-- chunk -->

### hold one nostril closed while you inhale the powder through the other nostril.

<!-- chunk -->

### Repeat with the other side. Be prepared for a few sneezes and copious drainage.

<!-- chunk -->

### Herbs that are used for sinus snuffs include bayberry bark, goldenseal, and

<!-- chunk -->

### yarrow.

# TOOTH POWDER

<!-- chunk -->

### Tooth powders can tone bleeding gums and strengthen tooth enamel. Use tooth

<!-- chunk -->

### powders with a toothbrush or with short sticks made from rosemary stems.

<!-- chunk -->

## Herbs for Tooth Powder

<!-- chunk -->

### Horsetail (strengthens tooth enamel)

<!-- chunk -->

### White oak bark (tones bleeding gums)

<!-- chunk -->

### Black walnut (tones bleeding gums)

<!-- chunk -->

### Calendula (tones bleeding gums)

<!-- chunk -->

### Myrrh gum (antiseptic)

<!-- chunk -->

### Elecampane (antiseptic)

# EYEWASH

<!-- chunk -->

### Herbal infusions can be applied as drops, as eyewashes, in eyecups, or in a

<!-- chunk -->

### compress for sore, red, tired eyes or for eye infections. Use only freshly made

<!-- chunk -->

### teas to avoid the risk of infection. Herbs for eyewashes include agrimony,

<!-- chunk -->

### chamomile, eyebright, and red raspberry.

# EAR DROPS

<!-- chunk -->

### Earaches and ear infections can be very painful. Herbal ear drops reduce

<!-- chunk -->

### inflammation, fight infection, and reduce pain in the ear. Make sure the eardrum

<!-- chunk -->

### is intact before putting anything into the ear.

<!-- chunk -->

### Warm a bottle of oil or tincture in a pan of hot water until the oil or tincture

<!-- chunk -->

### feels warm, but not hot, on the inside of your wrist. Drop 5–10 drops in the ear

<!-- chunk -->

### and use a cotton ball to prevent the oil or tincture from leaking out. Herbs that

<!-- chunk -->

### are used for ear drops include garlic, mullein, and St. John’s wort. Tea tree,

<!-- chunk -->

### lavender, or lemon essential oils can be diluted 1:20 in olive oil and applied as

<!-- chunk -->

### above.
