<!-- chunk -->

## CHAPTER NINE

# Other Preparations

<!-- chunk -->

## CONCENTRATES, LOZENGES, AND TRADITIONAL CHINESE

<!-- chunk -->

## METHODS

<!-- chunk -->

### his chapter covers some interesting techniques for preparing herbal

<!-- chunk -->

### medicines. These include powdered concentrates, lozenges, and hot alcohol

<!-- chunk -->

### extraction. We also discuss some of the Asian techniques used to prepare herbs.

<!-- chunk -->

### As you become more skilled at making your own herbal medicines, these are

<!-- chunk -->

### some techniques you can use to expand your skills.

# POWDERED CONCENTRATES

<!-- chunk -->

### These are techniques for taking herbs that have been extracted using water or

<!-- chunk -->

### alcohol and turning them back into powders. These powders are very potent and

<!-- chunk -->

### require much lower doses than bulk herb powders or regular capsules.

# TEA CONCENTRATES

<!-- chunk -->

### Tea powder concentrates are a great way to concentrate the water-soluble

<!-- chunk -->

### constituents from a plant extract. The idea, in a nutshell, is to make a strong

<!-- chunk -->

### decoction, reduce the decoction (concentrating it), then dry the decoction into a

<!-- chunk -->

### powder that can be easily administered in a beverage, as a capsule, or as a

<!-- chunk -->

### powder.

<!-- chunk -->

### Concentrated powders are great for children or adults who are unlikely to

<!-- chunk -->

### drink a cup of bad-tasting tea, and for those who can’t take tinctures. Any herb

<!-- chunk -->

### that can be decocted works well as a tea concentrate.

<!-- chunk -->

### Here’s the step-by-step way to make a tea powder concentrate.

<!-- chunk -->

## Step one: Slightly moisten ground herb powder (if dry) or freshly minced herb

<!-- chunk -->

### (if fresh) with 95% alcohol. If alkaloids are the primary constituent you are

<!-- chunk -->

### trying to extract, use a mixture of equal parts alcohol and apple cider vinegar to

<!-- chunk -->

### moisten the plant material. Let the marc soak for 1 hour.

<!-- chunk -->

## Step two: Add 1 part herb to 32 parts water. So, 1 ounce of herb would be added

<!-- chunk -->

### to 32 ounces (1 quart) of water. Simmer the herbs and water for 2–4 hours on

<!-- chunk -->

### very low heat.

<!-- chunk -->

## Step three: Strain the decoction to remove the marc (plant material). Then

<!-- chunk -->

### return the liquid to the pot.

<!-- chunk -->

## Step four: Simmer the remaining liquid down to about 20% of its original

<!-- chunk -->

### volume. So, 20 ounces would be reduced to 4 ounces.

<!-- chunk -->

## Step five: Remove the liquid from the heat and measure the amount of

<!-- chunk -->

### concentrated liquid. You are going to add a powder to this mixture before drying.

<!-- chunk -->

### Two possibilities are arrowroot starch or some of the herb powder. For example,

<!-- chunk -->

### if you just made a decoction of licorice root, you’d use some fresh licorice root

<!-- chunk -->

### powder.

<!-- chunk -->

### If you are using arrowroot starch, add 5 grams (⅙ ounce) for every

<!-- chunk -->

### milliliters (1 ounce) of liquid. If using herb powders, add 10 grams (⅓ ounce) of

<!-- chunk -->

### powdered herb for every 30 milliliters (1 ounce) of liquid. Blend the powder and

<!-- chunk -->

### liquid well.

<!-- chunk -->

## Step six: Thinly spread the mixture of the decoction and powder onto a fruit

<!-- chunk -->

### leather tray and place in a dehydrator. Set the temperature to 120°. Allow the

<!-- chunk -->

### mixture to dry until it is dry and brittle.

<!-- chunk -->

## Step seven: Remove the dried concentrate from the tray. Grind it into a powder

<!-- chunk -->

### using a coffee grinder or a mortar and pestle. Store the powder in an airtight

<!-- chunk -->

### container away from heat and light.

<!-- chunk -->

### You now have a concentrated herbal extract in powdered form. You can

<!-- chunk -->

### encapsulate the powder or mix it into water, juice, or honey to consume.

# TINCTURE POWDER CONCENTRATES

<!-- chunk -->

### Tincture powder concentrates are similar to tea concentrates, but are appropriate

<!-- chunk -->

### for herbs with alcohol-soluble constituents. They don’t work well for plants

<!-- chunk -->

### whose primary constituents are aromatic oils. Here are the steps for producing

<!-- chunk -->

### tincture powder concentrates.

<!-- chunk -->

## Step one: Add 5 grams (⅙ ounce) of arrowroot starch or 10 grams (⅓ ounce) of

<!-- chunk -->

### dried powdered herb for every 30 milliliters (1 ounce) of tincture and blend well.

<!-- chunk -->

## Step two: Spread the mixture thinly on a fruit leather tray. Place in a dehydrator

<!-- chunk -->

### set at 140°. Allow it to dry until it becomes dry and brittle.

<!-- chunk -->

## Step three: Remove the dried concentrate from the tray and grind using a coffee

<!-- chunk -->

### grinder or mortar and pestle. Store in an airtight container away from heat and

<!-- chunk -->

### light.

<!-- chunk -->

### Due to the alcohol content, dehydrate tincture powder concentrates in a well-

<!-- chunk -->

### ventilated area away from any sources of flame or spark.

# LOZENGES OR COUGH DROPS

<!-- chunk -->

### You can make lozenges or cough drops out of herbal extracts to ease sore throats

<!-- chunk -->

### and respiratory problems. This is actually a form of candy making and follows

<!-- chunk -->

### the same procedures for making hard candy. Although not completely necessary,

<!-- chunk -->

### candy molds are helpful for making uniform lozenges. It’s also helpful to have a

<!-- chunk -->

### good thermometer for checking the temperature. Here are the procedures to

<!-- chunk -->

### follow.

<!-- chunk -->

## Step one: Make a decoction of the herb or herbs you want to use in your cough

<!-- chunk -->

### drops. Good choices include horehound, wild cherry, white pine, coltsfoot, elder

<!-- chunk -->

### berry, or cinnamon.

<!-- chunk -->

## Step two: Measure 1 cup of the decoction and add either 2 cups organic raw

<!-- chunk -->

### sugar or freeze-dried sugarcane juice or 1½ cups honey and ⅛ teaspoon cream of

<!-- chunk -->

### tartar. The cream of tartar helps offset the excess moisture in the honey so the

<!-- chunk -->

### candy will harden.

<!-- chunk -->

## Step three: Heat the mixture in a saucepan on low heat until the syrup reaches

<!-- chunk -->

### the hard-crack stage (about 290°–300°). You can check this by dropping a small

<!-- chunk -->

### amount of the syrup into ice water. If it hardens properly, it is ready.

<!-- chunk -->

## Step four (optional): Just as the mixture reaches the proper temperature, you can

<!-- chunk -->

### add some essential oils to make the drops more potent. Good oils to consider are

<!-- chunk -->

### eucalyptus, thyme, rosemary, and peppermint. Add about 10–20 drops of

<!-- chunk -->

### essential oils.

<!-- chunk -->

## Step five: Being very careful not to get any of the syrup onto your skin (it will

<!-- chunk -->

### burn you very badly), pour the hot mixture into your greased candy molds. If

<!-- chunk -->

### you do not have candy molds, you can pour it onto a greased dish. If using the

<!-- chunk -->

### dish method, wait until the candy is halfway hardened, then score the mixture

<!-- chunk -->

### with a knife to create drop-sized pieces. You’ll be able to break the candy along

<!-- chunk -->

### the scores after it has hardened completely.

<!-- chunk -->

### Another option if you don’t have candy molds is to fill a pan with powdered

<!-- chunk -->

### sugar. (If you don’t want to use refined sugar, powder freeze-dried sugarcane

<!-- chunk -->

### juice in the blender.) Drop little bits of the candy mixture into the powdered

<!-- chunk -->

### sugar. They will form small drops. You can roll the drops in the sugar when they

<!-- chunk -->

### cool to coat them with sugar.

<!-- chunk -->

## Step six: When the lozenges have cooled, store them in a sealed container. You

<!-- chunk -->

### now have your own homemade lozenges.

# HOT ALCOHOL EXTRACT

<!-- chunk -->

### Certain herbs, such as usnea, can be extracted using alcohol and heat. This is a

<!-- chunk -->

### potentially dangerous method of extraction, because alcohol has a lower boiling

<!-- chunk -->

### point (173°) than water (212°). Alcohol is also very flammable, so keep the

<!-- chunk -->

### extract away from any open flame and keep the temperature well below the

<!-- chunk -->

### boiling point of alcohol (173°) during the extraction process.

<!-- chunk -->

### The safest way to do this is using a mason jar and a crock pot. Put the herbs

<!-- chunk -->

### along with the appropriate mixture of water and alcohol into the mason jar(s).

<!-- chunk -->

### Put lids on them, but do not tighten them completely. Allow a little space for

<!-- chunk -->

### ventilation so that pressure does not build up inside the jar and cause it to break

<!-- chunk -->

### or explode.

<!-- chunk -->

### Fill the crock pot with water and place the jar(s) into the water. Turn the crock

<!-- chunk -->

### pot on low for 8 hours. Wait until the jar has cooled before opening the jar and

<!-- chunk -->

### straining the extract.

# TRADITIONAL CHINESE METHODS

<!-- chunk -->

### Both Ayurvedic and traditional Chinese medicine developed methods for

<!-- chunk -->

### processing herbs that reduce toxicity, increase their effectiveness, alter their

<!-- chunk -->

### properties, or remove offensive odors. Western herbalism is not as sophisticated

<!-- chunk -->

### in its processing methods. Here are some of the methods used in traditional

<!-- chunk -->

### Chinese medicine.

<!-- chunk -->

## Steaming and drying: A glutinous and demulcent root such as Rehmannia

<!-- chunk -->

### glutinosa (which has a cooling energy) is repeatedly steamed with wine and sun-

<!-- chunk -->

### dried by the Chinese. Korean red ginseng is made by steaming the ginseng roots

<!-- chunk -->

### and then drying them. These processes make the herbs have a more warming

<!-- chunk -->

### energy. Western herbs such as marshmallow and comfrey may be prepared in

<!-- chunk -->

### this manner.

<!-- chunk -->

## Dry-roasting and stir-frying: Dry-roasting certain herbs will enhance their fire

<!-- chunk -->

### or yang characteristics. Herbs like bupleurum are prepared in this way. Western

<!-- chunk -->

### herbs that are dry-roasted for added flavor include dandelion, chicory, and

<!-- chunk -->

### comfrey roots. In the raw state these herbs have a cooling energy, but when

<!-- chunk -->

### roasted they develop a slightly warmer energy. When a little honey is added to

<!-- chunk -->

### the herb as it is stirred and dry-roasted in a wok, it adds a tonic effect through its

<!-- chunk -->

### sweetness. In traditional Chinese medicine, licorice and astragalus are often dry-

<!-- chunk -->

### roasted with honey.

<!-- chunk -->

## Soaking in spirits or wine: Herbs that are soaked in grain spirits or wine enter

<!-- chunk -->

### the blood with optimum efficiency and have an escalating energy. The alcohol

<!-- chunk -->

### acts as an usher as well as an energizer to blood circulation. Such preparations

<!-- chunk -->

### are used in traditional Chinese medicine as tonics to treat circulatory disorders

<!-- chunk -->

### such as arthritic and rheumatic complaints.

<!-- chunk -->

## Processing with salt: In traditional Chinese medicine, a pinch of salt is added to

<!-- chunk -->

### herbs intended to tonify the urinary tract. Salt acts as a carrier for the other herbs

<!-- chunk -->

### in the formula, bringing their action to the kidneys. It also adds a moisture-

<!-- chunk -->

### retaining effect. Salt enhances the descending energy of a formula.

<!-- chunk -->

## Processing with vinegar: The sour taste generally, and vinegar in particular, has

<!-- chunk -->

### a descending and astringent energy and targets the liver.

<!-- chunk -->

## Leaching with water: This is done mainly to remove tannic acid and certain

<!-- chunk -->

### toxic substances. Acorns were prepared in this manner by Native Americans.

<!-- chunk -->

## Sand heating: Certain herbs are roasted by mixing them in sand and heating

<!-- chunk -->

### them in a wok while stirring frequently. This allows a much higher temperature

<!-- chunk -->

### than stir-frying without sand, and the roots will heat more evenly.

<!-- chunk -->

## Baking: Herbs can be baked with honey, ginger, or other carriers to enhance

<!-- chunk -->

### certain effects. Honey makes herbs more tonic and moistening. Ginger makes

<!-- chunk -->

### them more warming and dispersing, promoting circulation and digestion.


<!-- chunk -->

## CHAPTER TEN

# Aromatherapy and Flower Essences

<!-- chunk -->

## TWO UNIQUE WAYS TO EXTRACT AND USE HERBS

<!-- chunk -->

### his chapter covers the use of specialized preparations made from plants—

<!-- chunk -->

### essential oils, homeopathic remedies, and flower essences. These

<!-- chunk -->

### preparations require additional study in order to be able to use them effectively,

<!-- chunk -->

### but we will introduce them here.

# AROMATHERAPY

<!-- chunk -->

### Aromatherapy is a specialized application of plant medicine that uses the volatile

<!-- chunk -->

### compounds from plants known as essential oils. Essential oils are what give

<!-- chunk -->

### flowers and herbs their distinctive fragrances. They are liquids that readily

<!-- chunk -->

### evaporate at room temperature. When we smell something, these volatile

<!-- chunk -->

### compounds are triggering the olfactory receptors in our nose.

<!-- chunk -->

### Essential oils are derived from the flowers, leaves, stems, seeds, or bark of

<!-- chunk -->

### different plants. Although they are called oils, essential oils are not made of the

<!-- chunk -->

### fatty acids found in fats and vegetable oils. They are complex mixtures of

<!-- chunk -->

### alcohols, terpenes, terpenoids, phenols, ketones, and oxides and are soluble in

<!-- chunk -->

### fats, oils, glycerin, and high-proof alcohol, but not in water.

<!-- chunk -->

### The French chemist who coined the term “aromatherapy,” Professor René-

<!-- chunk -->

### Maurice Gattefossé, conducted experiments with essential oils on wounded

<!-- chunk -->

### soldiers during World War I. At that time, the most commonly used antiseptic

<!-- chunk -->

### was phenol, which was good for cleaning hospital floors but not very effective

<!-- chunk -->

### for healing wounds. The soldiers Gattefossé treated had badly infected wounds

<!-- chunk -->

### that often resulted in serious poisoning as the body reabsorbed harmful

<!-- chunk -->

### substances produced by decaying tissue. His work proved that essential oils,

<!-- chunk -->

### particularly lavender, are superior to chemical antiseptics in their ability to

<!-- chunk -->

### detoxify and speed up the elimination of these substances.

<!-- chunk -->

### Aromatherapy is widely accepted in Europe by the medical profession. In

<!-- chunk -->

### England, for instance, the relaxing aroma of lavender is wafted through the

<!-- chunk -->

### wards to help raise patient morale, which helps patients to heal faster. Before

<!-- chunk -->

### bed, these same patients are given a choice of a tranquilizer drug or an

<!-- chunk -->

### aromatherapy oil massage. The use of essential oils is also gaining popularity in

<!-- chunk -->

### the United States, as more and more companies are selling the oils and

<!-- chunk -->

### promoting their use.

# HEALING PROPERTIES OF ESSENTIAL OILS

<!-- chunk -->

### There are many benefits of essential oils, one of them being that they do not

<!-- chunk -->

### appear to lose their effect with repeated applications. Generally speaking, most

<!-- chunk -->

### essential oils have effects on the nervous and glandular systems and help to

<!-- chunk -->

### destroy harmful microbes. Their effects on the nervous and glandular systems

<!-- chunk -->

### make them mood altering, and this effect takes place primarily through the sense

<!-- chunk -->

### of smell.

<!-- chunk -->

### The sense of smell is unique because it is wired directly into the brain. Nerve

<!-- chunk -->

### pathways connect the sinus cavity with the olfactory bulb, which is part of the

<!-- chunk -->

### limbic system of our brain. This is a survival mechanism, as smells can alert us

<!-- chunk -->

### to danger or suggest that something may be good to eat.

# ESSENTIAL OILS AND MOOD

<!-- chunk -->

### Odors directly affect the amygdala and the hippocampus, as well as the

<!-- chunk -->

### hypothalamus, the part of the brain that regulates the pituitary gland. This means

<!-- chunk -->

### that smells can directly alter hormone production and affect the autonomic

<!-- chunk -->

### nervous system, which regulates digestion, heart rate, blood pressure, and

<!-- chunk -->

### breathing. This is what gives them their mood-altering effects.

<!-- chunk -->

### Marketers were the first to capitalize on the ability of fragrances to alter

<!-- chunk -->

### mood. For instance, the Smell and Taste Treatment and Research Foundation

<!-- chunk -->

### tested the effects of various scents on shoppers in stores selling Nike® shoes.

<!-- chunk -->

### The results were surprising: 84% of customers preferred shoes in the scented

<!-- chunk -->

### showroom and were even willing to pay more for them! Soon thereafter,

<!-- chunk -->

### Japanese companies began applying this type of research by using lemon and

<!-- chunk -->

### other citrus scents in their office buildings to stimulate alertness and

<!-- chunk -->

### concentration, thereby reducing errors and boosting productivity.

<!-- chunk -->

### Because of their effect on mood, essential oils can even help set the mood for

<!-- chunk -->

### intimacy or aid meditation and spiritual awareness. They can also be used in

<!-- chunk -->

### emotional healing work and to aid in the treatment of mood disorders, such as

<!-- chunk -->

### anxiety, sadness, and depression. They can also improve mental alertness,

<!-- chunk -->

### concentration, memory, and cognition.

# PHYSICAL HEALING PROPERTIES

<!-- chunk -->

### Besides having a direct impact on our mind and mood, essential oils have

<!-- chunk -->

### beneficial physical healing properties. As already mentioned, essential oils tend

<!-- chunk -->

### to be infection fighters, possessing various degrees of antibacterial, antiviral,

<!-- chunk -->

### antifungal, and, in some cases, antiparasitic actions. They can stimulate various

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolic processes, which may result in the faster healing of wounds, increased

<!-- chunk -->

### white blood cell counts, improved digestion, enhanced energy production, and

<!-- chunk -->

### increased circulation.

<!-- chunk -->

### It is not necessary to ingest essential oils to obtain these benefits. Research has

<!-- chunk -->

### shown that essential oils are absorbed through the sinuses and skin and carried

<!-- chunk -->

### through the circulatory system to all the organs. They are then removed from the

<!-- chunk -->

### body via various eliminative organs such as the lungs or the urinary system.

<!-- chunk -->

### The process takes anywhere from minutes to several hours. You can

<!-- chunk -->

### experience this if you rub the sole of your foot with a slice of raw garlic. It will

<!-- chunk -->

### show up on your breath several hours later. The volatile compounds in garlic are

<!-- chunk -->

### absorbed through the skin, travel through the bloodstream, and are eliminated

<!-- chunk -->

### from the body via the lungs. The organs the body uses to eliminate an oil are

<!-- chunk -->

### going to receive the antimicrobial and stimulating actions of that herb. Because

<!-- chunk -->

### garlic is eliminated via the lungs, but not the kidneys, it helps respiratory

<!-- chunk -->

### infections but not urinary tract infections.

# UTILITARIAN USES

<!-- chunk -->

### Essential oils have utilitarian uses, too. They are often added to products for skin

<!-- chunk -->

### and hair care, such as lotions and shampoos, both for their therapeutic benefits

<!-- chunk -->

### and their emotional appeal. Their disinfectant properties make them useful in

<!-- chunk -->

### mouthwashes, natural deodorants, and disinfectant cleaners for the home.

<!-- chunk -->

### Essential oils can also be used as perfumes and natural air fresheners. Many will

<!-- chunk -->

### also help to repel insects and rodents.

<!-- chunk -->

### Aromatherapy involves using essential oils in many different ways. For

<!-- chunk -->

### example, they can be added to a fixed oil or massage lotion to be applied to the

<!-- chunk -->

### skin. They can be added to bath water or used in soaks, compresses,

<!-- chunk -->

### fomentations, gargles, and mouthwashes, or diffused into the air for inhalation

<!-- chunk -->

### via a vaporizer or diffuser. For health problems, they are usually inhaled and

<!-- chunk -->

### applied topically, although some may be used internally by professionals if they

<!-- chunk -->

### are highly diluted.

<!-- chunk -->

### The chaos of modern life and the continual rise of medical costs have inspired

<!-- chunk -->

### many individuals to rediscover aromatherapy as a natural self-help technique.

# ESSENTIAL OIL SAFETY

<!-- chunk -->

### Because the oils used in aromatherapy are available over the counter, some

<!-- chunk -->

### people assume that they are completely safe. They are safe when used properly,

<!-- chunk -->

### but can cause problems ranging from irritation of the skin and mucous

<!-- chunk -->

### membranes to more severe reactions such as liver damage, uterine hemorrhage,

<!-- chunk -->

### and abortion. Consult with a qualified aromatherapist before using undiluted

<!-- chunk -->

### oils, and talk to a health care professional before using aromatherapy if you are

<!-- chunk -->

### pregnant, have high or low blood pressure, or have allergies. The National

<!-- chunk -->

### Association for Holistic Aromatherapy is a good place for finding information

<!-- chunk -->

### on practitioners and education in aromatherapy.

<!-- chunk -->

### Reports abound about people having serious reactions from using essential

<!-- chunk -->

### oils without proper respect. Thomas has seen several cases of elevated liver

<!-- chunk -->

### enzymes, and one case of colitis, from the internal use of essential oils by

<!-- chunk -->

### untrained individuals. Eventually, if these adverse reactions continue, the FDA is

<!-- chunk -->

### likely to step in and enact strict regulations on the sales of essential oils. To

<!-- chunk -->

### continue to have these marvelous remedies available long term, we must become

<!-- chunk -->

### more conservative in their use, and stop the trend of using them undiluted and

<!-- chunk -->

### internally for problems easily treated by more gentle remedies. Carefully follow

<!-- chunk -->

### the guidelines provided below for safely diluting and using essential oils.

# USING ESSENTIAL OILS

<!-- chunk -->

### To get started, we want to acquaint you with some basic terms and techniques for

<!-- chunk -->

### using essential oils (EOs). Although they are natural substances, EOs are highly

<!-- chunk -->

### concentrated, which increases their potential for misuse. So, be sure you

<!-- chunk -->

### understand the following terms and basic guidelines for using these powerful

<!-- chunk -->

### natural substances.

Photo by kerdkanno / CC BY

<!-- chunk -->

## Diluting Oils

<!-- chunk -->

### EOs can be diluted with a fixed vegetable oil (like almond or olive oil) or a

<!-- chunk -->

### natural soap or lotion. EOs can also be added to unscented liquid soap products.

<!-- chunk -->

### Generally, the finished product should be about 2%–3% EOs. For easy reference,

<!-- chunk -->

### use Table 9 to calculate how much oil to add to a liquid for a 2½% and 5%

<!-- chunk -->

### dilution.

## Topical Use

<!-- chunk -->

### The term “neat” is used for oils that can be applied topically in an undiluted

<!-- chunk -->

### form. Oils that are too irritating to be used neat should be diluted as described in

<!-- chunk -->

### the next paragraph. Certain essential oils, such as tea tree or lavender, may be

<!-- chunk -->

### applied neat to wounds to fight infection and stimulate tissue healing and repair.

<!-- chunk -->

### Other essential oils can be diluted (as per Table 9) into a fixed oil or lotion and

<!-- chunk -->

### applied topically for the treatment of skin conditions. People with sensitive skin

<!-- chunk -->

### may want to dilute even neat oils at least 1:1 (equal parts).

<!-- chunk -->

### One can also blend oils in this manner for local use; for example, massaging

<!-- chunk -->

### essential oils diluted in a carrier oil onto the neck for a sore throat, the chest for

<!-- chunk -->

### coughs and congestion, the abdomen for gas, bloating, and infection, and so

<!-- chunk -->

### forth. Essential oils make good additions to many of the topical preparations

<!-- chunk -->

### mentioned in Chapter Eight, including salves, balms, lotions, butters, liniments,

<!-- chunk -->

### and other preparations.

<!-- chunk -->

## Diffusion

<!-- chunk -->

### Diffusing oils can help to freshen the air, enhance mood, and/or kill airborne

<!-- chunk -->

### microbes to promote healing and prevent the spread of infection. It is a very safe

<!-- chunk -->

### and effective way to use EOs. There are many types of diffusers you can

<!-- chunk -->

### purchase on the market that can be used to disperse essential oils into the air.

<!-- chunk -->

### You can also diffuse oils by bringing a pot of water to a boil on the stove, turning

<!-- chunk -->

### the heat down to a simmer, and adding 5–15 drops of one or more essential oils.

<!-- chunk -->

### Let the water simmer uncovered on the stove for 20–30 minutes to diffuse the

<!-- chunk -->

### oil(s).

<!-- chunk -->

### Steam inhalation is an excellent method of application for treating problems

<!-- chunk -->

### of the respiratory system, such as colds, coughs, and sinus conditions, and

<!-- chunk -->

### relieving tension and headaches. The aroma goes straight to the brain while the

<!-- chunk -->

### oils pass from the lungs into the bloodstream.

<!-- chunk -->

### To diffuse oils in this manner, put 5–10 drops of oil in a bowl containing

<!-- chunk -->

### cups boiling hot water. Inhale the steam for 5–10 minutes, keeping your nose

<!-- chunk -->

### about 8 inches above the water. Keep your eyes closed. You can drape a towel

<!-- chunk -->

### over your head to concentrate the steam. Repeat up to 3 times daily.

<!-- chunk -->

### You can make a portable inhaler by putting a few drops of EO on a

<!-- chunk -->

### handkerchief or tissue and taking deep breaths through the cloth.

<!-- chunk -->

### You can also make an EO spray to use as an air freshener. To make your own

<!-- chunk -->

### air purifier, fill a spray bottle halfway with distilled water. Blend 30 drops of

<!-- chunk -->

### vegetable glycerin and about 20 drops of EO(s) for every 30 milliliters (1 fluid

<!-- chunk -->

### ounce) of water and then add this mixture to the spray bottle. Shake well and

<!-- chunk -->

### spray as you would any air freshener.

<!-- chunk -->

## Perfumes

<!-- chunk -->

### One can also get the benefit of EOs by using them as perfumes. Add 10–15

<!-- chunk -->

### drops of an EO to ¼ ounce of a massage oil and place in a bottle with a roll-on

<!-- chunk -->

### applicator to apply topically.

<!-- chunk -->

## Baths and Soaks

<!-- chunk -->

### To disperse EOs into a bath, first add 8–15 drops of the oils to 1 tablespoon of

<!-- chunk -->

### unscented natural liquid soap like Dr. Bronner’s Supermild Baby Soap or

<!-- chunk -->

### Nature’s Sunshine’s Sunshine Concentrate. Hold this mixture under the faucet

<!-- chunk -->

### while drawing the bath. This will disperse the EOs into the bath water. For a foot

<!-- chunk -->

### or hand soak, use about 4 drops in a teaspoon of liquid soap and add it to a

<!-- chunk -->

### container of water. Aromatic baths soothe muscular aches and pains, skin

<!-- chunk -->

### disorders, circulation problems, tension, fatigue, and insomnia.

<!-- chunk -->

## Internal Use

<!-- chunk -->

### There is a big movement, popularized by essential oil companies, to use EOs

<!-- chunk -->

### internally. Although some EOs can be used internally, that is best left to well-

<!-- chunk -->

### trained aromatherapists. In fact, the use of oils internally comes from the French

<!-- chunk -->

### school, where it’s primarily medical doctors, trained in aromatherapy, who are

<!-- chunk -->

### using them internally. There are not many instances that the internal use of EOs

<!-- chunk -->

### offers better results than a cup of herb tea, so we don’t advise it. If you do decide

<!-- chunk -->

### to use them internally, please only use EOs that are GRAS (generally recognized

<!-- chunk -->

### as safe), and know that even then they should be well diluted to avoid mucosal

<!-- chunk -->

### irritation. EOs that are not GRAS should never be used internally, as they can

<!-- chunk -->

### have harmful effects when ingested. When using EOs internally, you must

<!-- chunk -->

### always dilute them. Because they do not dissolve readily in water, taking them

<!-- chunk -->

### with water is not a good idea, as the oils will float to the top of the water.

<!-- chunk -->

### You can also add EOs to herbal extracts to increase their potency, mask

<!-- chunk -->

### disagreeable flavors, or improve the taste. Generally speaking, add no more than

<!-- chunk -->

### 1–2 drops per 2 ounces of extract.

# FLOWER ESSENCES

<!-- chunk -->

### Flower essences are homeopathic-like remedies made from the flowers of plants.

<!-- chunk -->

### They help a person find healing on an emotional, rather than a physical or

<!-- chunk -->

### mental, level. Like homeopathy, flower essences are a form of energy medicine,

<!-- chunk -->

### but are not made to the exacting standards of homeopathics. Also, they are

<!-- chunk -->

### designed to affect a person’s emotional state rather than their physical

<!-- chunk -->

### symptoms.

<!-- chunk -->

### Dr. Edward Bach, an English medical doctor and homeopath, discovered how

<!-- chunk -->

### to use flowers for emotional healing and created the first thirty-eight flower

<!-- chunk -->

### essence remedies. Dr. Bach was frustrated by the symptomatic approach of

<!-- chunk -->

### modern medicine. He felt that medical doctors focused too much on the

<!-- chunk -->

### pathology (the disease symptoms) and not enough on the patient.

<!-- chunk -->

### Bach was a pioneer in understanding gut microflora. He developed

<!-- chunk -->

### homeopathic “vaccines” called Bach nosodes to adjust the friendly flora of the

<!-- chunk -->

### gut to improve health. He also was an advocate of healthy diet and detoxification

<!-- chunk -->

### of the gastrointestinal tract as a route to good health.

<!-- chunk -->

### As he observed patients, however, he began to notice that certain infections

<!-- chunk -->

### and illnesses tended to go with certain personality traits. He also noticed that a

<!-- chunk -->

### patient’s emotional state had a lot to do with their ability to heal. Bach felt that

<!-- chunk -->

### unresolved emotional conflicts within the person created disharmony between

<!-- chunk -->

### the soul and the mind, which eventually led to physical illness. In Bach’s mind,

<!-- chunk -->

### health was created by restoring internal harmony, with health being “the true

<!-- chunk -->

### realization of what we are; we are perfect; we are children of God.”

<!-- chunk -->

### Bach wanted to create a system of healing that wouldn’t destroy living things

<!-- chunk -->

### and that would be gentle and effective in nature. In his search he found that

<!-- chunk -->

### homeopathic preparations of flowers could guide a person to greater emotional

<!-- chunk -->

### balance. He called these preparations flower essences, and more than eighty

<!-- chunk -->

### years later millions of people have benefited from Bach’s remedies. Also, since

<!-- chunk -->

### the time of Dr. Bach, many more flower essences have been discovered, so that

<!-- chunk -->

### today, there are hundreds of flower essences from dozens of suppliers that can

<!-- chunk -->

### help a person with just about any emotional issue one might face in life.

# HOW FLOWER ESSENCES WORK

<!-- chunk -->

### To understand how flower essences work, we need to understand that plants have

<!-- chunk -->

### to overcome challenges, just like we do. They may encounter extremes of

<!-- chunk -->

### temperature or moisture, harsh weather conditions (such as wind or rocky soil),

<!-- chunk -->

### and attacks by insects and animals. Plants, like people, have a “personality,”

<!-- chunk -->

### characteristics that help them rise above these challenges. The plant personality

<!-- chunk -->

### is found in its energy pattern, which can become apparent when we study how

<!-- chunk -->

### the plant grows and the form it takes in meeting its life challenges.

<!-- chunk -->

### Just as associating with a person with positive personality traits can help you

<!-- chunk -->

### learn how to meet life’s challenges in a constructive way, so can associating with

<!-- chunk -->

### the right plant energies. As Matthew Wood says in his book Seven Herbs: Plants

<!-- chunk -->

### as Teachers, every healing plant is the embodiment of a conflict in the

<!-- chunk -->

### environment that the plant has successfully overcome. By capturing the

<!-- chunk -->

### “vibration” of the plant’s personality through the flower, you can take that

<!-- chunk -->

### vibration into your own being and “learn” how to experience that same

<!-- chunk -->

### emotional energy. The plant “teaches” us on a vibrational level how to cope with

<!-- chunk -->

### life in a happy, peaceful, and loving manner.

<!-- chunk -->

### Flower essences are made by soaking flowers in pure spring water, usually in

<!-- chunk -->

### the sunlight, and then preserving the strained water with brandy. The resulting

<!-- chunk -->

### mother tincture is then diluted in a homeopathic-like manner to imprint the

<!-- chunk -->

### plant’s vibration into the flower essence remedy.

<!-- chunk -->

### When we take the flower essence, it floods our body with a positive vibration

<!-- chunk -->

### that can break through emotional blocks and help us to become more aware. This

<!-- chunk -->

### increased awareness helps us make constructive changes in our lives, which, in

<!-- chunk -->

### turn, helps us find our way back to a balanced emotional state of inner peace and

<!-- chunk -->

### happiness.

# MAKING FLOWER ESSENCES

<!-- chunk -->

### You can make your own flower essences using the following process.

<!-- chunk -->

## Step one: Obtain flowers from the plant at the peak of blooming. Place them in a

<!-- chunk -->

### glass bowl filled with clean spring water. Allow them to soak in the sunlight for

<!-- chunk -->

### 3–5 hours. If the plant blooms at night, gather the flowers at the height of their

<!-- chunk -->

### blooming and soak them (preferably under a full moon) for the same 3–5 hours.

Photo by kerdkanno / CC BY

<!-- chunk -->

## Step two: Pour off the water into a storage container, straining out the flowers.

<!-- chunk -->

### Preserve the flower water with an equal amount of brandy. This produces the

<!-- chunk -->

### flower essence mother tincture.

<!-- chunk -->

## Step three: Prepare a second bottle filled with a mixture of water and brandy.

<!-- chunk -->

### This bottle needs to be only about 20–25% brandy. To this bottle, add 5–6 drops

<!-- chunk -->

### of the mother tincture for each ounce of water in the new bottle. Put the lid on

<!-- chunk -->

### this bottle and shake it vigorously up and down against the palm of your hand

<!-- chunk -->

### about 10 times. This creates the stock bottle, the preparation the practitioner

<!-- chunk -->

### keeps on the shelf.

<!-- chunk -->

## Step four: Dilute the stock tincture in the identical manner as described in step

<!-- chunk -->

### three to create a dosage bottle. The dosage bottle is what the practitioner gives to

<!-- chunk -->

### the client. You can add about 1–5 different flower essences to the dosage bottle.


<!-- chunk -->

## CHAPTER ELEVEN

# Formulas and Dosages

<!-- chunk -->

## DESIGNING HERBAL FORMULAS AND USING HERBS

<!-- chunk -->

## EFFECTIVELY

<!-- chunk -->

### his chapter covers two very critical considerations in herbalism—how to

<!-- chunk -->

### combine herbs together effectively and how to determine the effective dose.

<!-- chunk -->

### These topics are important because they help a person get the results they want

<!-- chunk -->

### from herbal remedies. We’ll cover the principles of herbal formulation first and

<!-- chunk -->

### how to determine dosages second.

# PRINCIPLES OF HERB FORMULATION

<!-- chunk -->

### It is frustrating for an experienced herbalist to see books that describe the actions

<!-- chunk -->

### of herbal combinations by listing all the properties of the individual single herbs

<!-- chunk -->

### they contain. Such information gives the illusion that by taking this particular

<!-- chunk -->

### formula one can derive all of the benefits of each herb in the product as if each

<!-- chunk -->

### herb were being taken separately. This is simply not the case.

<!-- chunk -->

### Just as the action of a single herb is different from the individual actions of its

<!-- chunk -->

### constituents or so-called active ingredients, the total action of a formula is

<!-- chunk -->

### different from the action of its individual herbs. It is no different from the effect

<!-- chunk -->

### of cooking with foods; a complete dish is different from its individual

<!-- chunk -->

### ingredients. The ingredients blend to form a new creation.

<!-- chunk -->

### Even if you’re not going to design herbal formulas, you may take more than

<!-- chunk -->

### one herb at the same time. When you do this, you are creating a formula,

<!-- chunk -->

### because the herbs interact with each other. Basically, some herbs will enhance or

<!-- chunk -->

### increase the activity of each other, whereas other herbs will counteract or

<!-- chunk -->

### diminish each other’s effects.

<!-- chunk -->

### This is largely based on simple common sense. For instance, if one mixes an

<!-- chunk -->

### herb that stimulates the peristalsis of the colon with an herb that restrains the

<!-- chunk -->

### peristalsis of the colon, one cannot claim that the mixture will cure both diarrhea

<!-- chunk -->

### and constipation. Quite the contrary: the contradictory actions may result in the

<!-- chunk -->

### formula being good for neither.

# FORMULAS VERSUS SINGLES

<!-- chunk -->

### Herbs can be used as single remedies in much the same way that homeopathic

<!-- chunk -->

### remedies are. Each herb has a unique combination of actions and properties, and

<!-- chunk -->

### when a person’s overall symptom picture very closely matches that of a single

<!-- chunk -->

### herb, very powerful effects can be created using single remedies.

<!-- chunk -->

### Using herbs in this manner requires a great deal of knowledge and skill,

<!-- chunk -->

### however. Most herbalists give their clients more than one remedy, which means

<!-- chunk -->

### they are creating a custom formula for the client. So herbs are more often used in

<!-- chunk -->

### combinations than they are as singles.

<!-- chunk -->

### Most herb books focus primarily on single herbs, but ironically, it’s harder for

<!-- chunk -->

### a beginner to see results with single herbs than it is with well-crafted herbal

<!-- chunk -->

### formulas. It can be likened to the difference between using a rifle and a shotgun

<!-- chunk -->

### to hit a target. Using single herbs is like using a rifle. It has a lot of power, but

<!-- chunk -->

### you have to know how to aim it properly to hit the target.

<!-- chunk -->

### A formula, on the other hand, is like a shotgun. You may not hit the target

<!-- chunk -->

### with as much concentrated force, but your aim doesn’t have to be as good. This

<!-- chunk -->

### is because an herbal formula combines many herbs that work on multiple causes

<!-- chunk -->

### of a problem. Using St. John’s wort, an herb that is often used for depression, as

<!-- chunk -->

### an example, we would find that St. John’s wort would help a person’s depression

<!-- chunk -->

### only if it is the kind of depression that St. John’s wort works best on (depression

<!-- chunk -->

### related to anxiety and intestinal problems).

<!-- chunk -->

### However, if we mix several herbs together that work on several different

<!-- chunk -->

### underlying causes of depression, a person is more likely to get at least some

<!-- chunk -->

### benefit. This is partially because the formula is composed of herbs that address

<!-- chunk -->

### multiple potential root causes of a problem. It is also partly because the synergy

<!-- chunk -->

### of the ingredients often enhances the basic action, and because the multiple

<!-- chunk -->

### ingredients tend to counteract some of the other effects of the single herbs,

<!-- chunk -->

### which also lessens the chance of the remedy throwing the body out of balance in

<!-- chunk -->

### other ways.

<!-- chunk -->

### To paraphrase Michael Moore, a famous southwestern herbalist, single herbs

<!-- chunk -->

### have subtle and deep actions. Combinations tone down those subtleties to a gray

<!-- chunk -->

### background noise and leave the predominant effects of the herb intact. The

<!-- chunk -->

### literature of the Eclectics, a group of medical doctors who used herbs in the

<!-- chunk -->

### nineteenth century, emphasized utilizing the subtle effects of the herbs and

<!-- chunk -->

### matching all of a person’s problems to one herb. However, in actual practice,

<!-- chunk -->

### they frequently resorted to tried and true formulas.

<!-- chunk -->

### Traditional Chinese medicine (TCM) also relies heavily on formulas. Basic

<!-- chunk -->

### formulas are used for certain situations, with the ingredients being modified to

<!-- chunk -->

### fit individual needs.

# CREATING HERBAL FORMULAS

<!-- chunk -->

### People unskilled in herbalism often create “kitchen sink” formulas, where they

<!-- chunk -->

### simply blend together everything that has been historically used for a particular

<!-- chunk -->

### health problem, thinking that this will be highly effective. This does not take into

<!-- chunk -->

### account the idea of herbal energetics, however. The principle of energetics tells

<!-- chunk -->

### us that some herbs will enhance each other’s effects, whereas some herbs will

<!-- chunk -->

### counter each other’s effects. So, randomly mixing herbs together does not

<!-- chunk -->

### always yield more effective results.

<!-- chunk -->

## Herbal Formulas and Energetics

<!-- chunk -->

### Herbal energetics refers to the ability of herbs to move the body’s energies in

<!-- chunk -->

### specific directions. We use a model of energetics based on six tissue states and

<!-- chunk -->

### six basic directions of action. Without going into great detail, here are the basics.

<!-- chunk -->

### First, herbs can affect metabolism in two general directions. Cooling herbs

<!-- chunk -->

### reduce irritation and excess heat (inflammation and fever). Warming herbs

<!-- chunk -->

### relieve depression and cold (underactivity of organs and tissues). Warming herbs

<!-- chunk -->

### and cooling herbs have opposite actions, which means they tend to restrain each

<!-- chunk -->

### other’s effects. We use the term “neutral” to describe herbs that are neither

<!-- chunk -->

### warming nor cooling.

<!-- chunk -->

### Second, herbs affect the balance of minerals and fluids in the body. Drying

<!-- chunk -->

### herbs treat stagnation and water retention, drying up excess fluids, reducing

<!-- chunk -->

### swelling, shrinking swollen lymph nodes, and otherwise tightening tissues by

<!-- chunk -->

### removing excess moisture. Moistening herbs restore flexibility and tissue

<!-- chunk -->

### function in atrophy, which means they are used when tissues are dry, hard,

<!-- chunk -->

### brittle, or inflexible. Again, these two actions move the tissues of the body in

<!-- chunk -->

### opposite directions, so they tend to counteract and restrain each other. We use the

<!-- chunk -->

### term “balancing” to describe herbs that help to bring tissues back to normal from

<!-- chunk -->

### either stagnation or atrophy.

<!-- chunk -->

### Third, herbs can affect muscle tone, relaxing the body to promote flow and

<!-- chunk -->

### secretion, or tightening tissues to reduce flow and secretion. Relaxing herbs ease

<!-- chunk -->

### muscle spasms and improve the flow of energy and fluids by easing constriction.

<!-- chunk -->

### Constricting herbs stop leakage by toning up tissue relaxation.

<!-- chunk -->

### Finally, we use the term “nourishing” to describe herbs that work primarily by

<!-- chunk -->

### providing nutrients that help the body heal itself and restore normal function.

<!-- chunk -->

### These herbs tend to not move the body’s energies strongly in any direction,

<!-- chunk -->

### although they might be slightly warming or cooling, or slightly moistening or

<!-- chunk -->

### drying.

<!-- chunk -->

### So, the first step in creating an herbal formula is to decide the primary

<!-- chunk -->

### energetic you want the formula to have: warming or drying, cooling or

<!-- chunk -->

### moistening, relaxing or nourishing, and so forth. To get this effect, combine

<!-- chunk -->

### herbs into a formula with a similar energetic.

<!-- chunk -->

## Body Systems and Properties

<!-- chunk -->

### In addition to considering energetics, you can also design formulas to target

<!-- chunk -->

### specific organs and body systems. Because herbs have affinity for organs and

<!-- chunk -->

### tissues, you can blend herbs to support the structure and function of specific

<!-- chunk -->

### body systems. For example, you can create digestive formulas, respiratory

<!-- chunk -->

### formulas, nervous system formulas, or a formula targeted specifically to the

<!-- chunk -->

### liver, heart, or brain.

<!-- chunk -->

### Herbs also have certain properties, meaning they affect the structure and

<!-- chunk -->

### function of the body in specific ways. By blending herbs with similar properties,

<!-- chunk -->

### you can create a synergistic effect, making the formula have very specific

<!-- chunk -->

### healing actions. For instance, you could create nervine formulas, diuretic

<!-- chunk -->

### formulas, alterative or blood purifying formulas, or hepatoprotective formulas.

<!-- chunk -->

### In contrast, herbal formulas will be less therapeutic and more nourishing in

<!-- chunk -->

### their effects when herbs are combined in ways that cancel out their energetic

<!-- chunk -->

### effects and properties. This prevents the formula from having a specific action or

<!-- chunk -->

### moving the body’s energies strongly in any direction.

<!-- chunk -->

## A Plan for Designing Formulas

<!-- chunk -->

### We use a system for creating herbal formulas based on four major components.

<!-- chunk -->

### It is not necessary to use all four components in any formula, but we usually

<!-- chunk -->

### create formulas using at least 2–3 of them. These components are key herbs,

<!-- chunk -->

### supporting herbs, balancing or harmonizing herbs, and catalysts.

<!-- chunk -->

## Key Herbs A key herb is one that has the primary action you want the formula to

<!-- chunk -->

### have. So, if you want the formula to have primarily an energetic action, you

<!-- chunk -->

### choose a single herb that has a very strong energetic. For example, if you want a

<!-- chunk -->

### warming formula, you might choose an herb like capsicum or ginger.

<!-- chunk -->

### You can also choose a key herb based on organ affinity. So, if you want your

<!-- chunk -->

### formula to be focused on the heart, you might choose hawthorn as a key herb. If

<!-- chunk -->

### you want your formula to be a liver formula, you might choose milk thistle.

<!-- chunk -->

### You can also choose a key herb that has a specific property you want. For

<!-- chunk -->

### example, if you want to create a stimulating diuretic formula, you might choose

<!-- chunk -->

### juniper berries as a key herb. If you want the formula to help a person go to

<!-- chunk -->

### sleep, you might choose hops or scullcap as a key herb.

<!-- chunk -->

### Your key herb can have a combination of any or all of the above. For example,

<!-- chunk -->

### hawthorn is well known as a heart herb and has cooling and toning effects on the

<!-- chunk -->

### heart. So, you could use it as a key herb for a formula designed to reduce cardiac

<!-- chunk -->

### inflammation and to tone or strengthen the heart.

<!-- chunk -->

### Think of the key herb as the king or emperor of the formula; it directs the

<!-- chunk -->

### action of the other ingredients. Generally speaking, a formula will have only one

<!-- chunk -->

### or possibly two key herbs.

<!-- chunk -->

## Supporting Herbs Supporting herbs are like the advisers to the king or emperor.

<!-- chunk -->

### They may have a similar action to the key herb(s) or may somehow enhance the

<!-- chunk -->

### action(s) of the key herb. A supporting herb may also be used to “fill in” effects

<!-- chunk -->

### wanted in the formula that are not supplied by the key herb(s). So, in the

<!-- chunk -->

### previously mentioned idea of using hawthorn in a heart formula, you might add

<!-- chunk -->

### motherwort to calm the heart rate.

<!-- chunk -->

## Balancing or Harmonizing Herbs These are optional ingredients in any

<!-- chunk -->

### formula. They help to harmonize the action of the whole formula. Balancing or

<!-- chunk -->

### harmonizing herbs may reduce an undesirable side effect of the key herb. For

<!-- chunk -->

### example, cascara sagrada tends to cause intestinal cramping. To counteract this,

<!-- chunk -->

### you might add an antispasmodic like lobelia to the formula.

<!-- chunk -->

### You might also add a balancing herb to help counteract a disagreeable flavor

<!-- chunk -->

### in the key and/or supporting herbs. For instance, licorice root is often added as a

<!-- chunk -->

### balancing herb in bitter formulas, as it adds a sweet flavor that reduces the bitter

<!-- chunk -->

### taste.

<!-- chunk -->

### Traditional Chinese herbalists nearly always use balancing herbs in their

<!-- chunk -->

### formulas. They add just a small amount of herbs that have exactly the opposite

<!-- chunk -->

### effect as the key herb(s) in order to make the formula more balanced. In other

<!-- chunk -->

### words, if they are creating a warming formula, they add a small amount of

<!-- chunk -->

### cooling herbs to balance out the warming nature of their key and supporting

<!-- chunk -->

### herbs.

<!-- chunk -->

## Catalysts These are herbs that help activate the formula or make it work more

<!-- chunk -->

### quickly. In Western herbal formulas, these are usually small amounts of an

<!-- chunk -->

### aromatic or pungent herb like capsicum or ginger. John Christopher used lobelia

<!-- chunk -->

### and capsicum in small amounts as catalysts in nearly all of his formulas.

<!-- chunk -->

### Traditional Chinese herbalists often use licorice root or ginger as catalysts. We

<!-- chunk -->

### often use aromatic herbs as catalysts in our formulas.

<!-- chunk -->

## Determining Parts Once you have selected all the herbs for the formula, use

<!-- chunk -->

### them in parts as follows:

<!-- chunk -->

### 1–2 key herbs (8–16 parts per herb)

<!-- chunk -->

### 2–4 supporting herbs (4–8 parts per herb)

<!-- chunk -->

### 0–3 balancing herbs (2–4 parts per herb)

<!-- chunk -->

### 0–2 catalysts (1–2 parts per herb)

<!-- chunk -->

### Parts are measured in weight, so a part could be a milligram or an ounce in

<!-- chunk -->

### making a small batch, or a kilogram or a pound in making a large batch. So, for

<!-- chunk -->

### example, we might make a formula with proportions as follows.

<!-- chunk -->

### 8 ounces key herb

<!-- chunk -->

### 4 ounces supporting herb

<!-- chunk -->

### 4 ounces supporting herb

<!-- chunk -->

### 2 ounces supporting herb

<!-- chunk -->

### 2 ounces balancing herb

<!-- chunk -->

### 1 ounce balancing herb

<!-- chunk -->

### 1 ounce catalyst

<!-- chunk -->

### If you examine many of the formulas we have included as examples in

<!-- chunk -->

### Chapter Twelve, you will gain greater insight into how to use this system to

<!-- chunk -->

### create effective formulas. We recommend that you learn to adjust formula

<!-- chunk -->

### ingredients by tasting the formula, just as a good chef always tastes the food he

<!-- chunk -->

### is preparing. You can adjust proportions to get just the effects you want, as well

<!-- chunk -->

### as to improve the overall flavor and appeal.

# DETERMINING DOSAGES

<!-- chunk -->

### Part of learning to use herbs correctly is learning how to dose them properly.

<!-- chunk -->

### Herbalists use a broad range of dosages, from single-drop doses of tinctures to

<!-- chunk -->

### bulk doses of herbs in near food quantities.

<!-- chunk -->

### Because of this, dosage, especially of tinctures, sometimes becomes a point of

<!-- chunk -->

### contention in Western herbal medicine. Some professional herbalists favor large

<!-- chunk -->

### doses (5 milliliters), whereas others use drop doses (1–5 drops), of most

<!-- chunk -->

### common tinctures. We believe that both drop doses and substantial doses have

<!-- chunk -->

### their place in herbal medicine, and we’ve listed both types of doses with specific

<!-- chunk -->

### herbs in Chapter Thirteen.

<!-- chunk -->

### The theory is that small doses of tinctured plants contain the essence, or

<!-- chunk -->

### energetic signature, of the plant. When matched to a person correctly, they act in

<!-- chunk -->

### a subtle yet powerful way through an unknown mechanism of action. On the

<!-- chunk -->

### other hand, substantial doses of herbs act through their chemical constituents to

<!-- chunk -->

### influence physiology and pathology.

# THE DROP

<!-- chunk -->

### Although the dosages for many tinctures are given in drops, the drop was never a

<!-- chunk -->

### good way to measure fluid volume. The size of a drop varies depending on the

<!-- chunk -->

### size of the dropper, the technique used to produce the drop, and the density and

<!-- chunk -->

### surface tension of the liquid. In pure water there are about 20 drops per milliliter,

<!-- chunk -->

### in pure alcohol there are about 60 drops per milliliter, and in pure vegetable

<!-- chunk -->

### glycerin there are about 12 drops per milliliter. Most tinctures are a mixture of

<!-- chunk -->

### alcohol and water (a hydroalcoholic extract). For a standard low-proof tincture

<!-- chunk -->

### (25%–40% EtOH) you can assume around 22–28 drops in 1 milliliter. For a

<!-- chunk -->

### high-proof tincture (80%–90% EtOH) there are around 35 drops per milliliter.

<!-- chunk -->

### These differences in drop size led English pharmacists in 1809 to adopt the

<!-- chunk -->

### minim as an alternative to the drop. The minim is measured in a graduated

<!-- chunk -->

### pipette called a minimometer. A minim is described as exactly 61.611519921875

<!-- chunk -->

### microliters.

<!-- chunk -->

### Knowing the exact number of drops in a milliliter or even a minim is not

<!-- chunk -->

### really necessary in modern herbalism. The only time dosing by the drop is

<!-- chunk -->

### important is if you are taking a low-dose (toxic) botanical. Low-dose botanicals

<!-- chunk -->

### should be made according to an official dispensatory and dosed as that

<!-- chunk -->

### dispensatory advises. We prefer either The Dispensatory of the United States of

<!-- chunk -->

### America by Remington, Wood, and colleagues (1918) or King’s American

<!-- chunk -->

### Dispensatory (1898). Drop doses of low-dose botanicals are most often added to

<!-- chunk -->

### a large volume of water and given in teaspoonful doses, making them safer than

<!-- chunk -->

### if they were given in direct drop doses.

# DOSING STRATEGIES FOR ADULTS

<!-- chunk -->

### We have included the standard adult dosages for all of the formulas and single

<!-- chunk -->

### herbs in this book. When purchasing commercial herbal formulas or single

<!-- chunk -->

### herbs, assume that the recommended dose on the bottle is for an average weight

<!-- chunk -->

### person of around 150 pounds.

<!-- chunk -->

### Lower-range dosages are used for people who are smaller than average or who

<!-- chunk -->

### react with sensitivity to herbs and supplements. Use higher-range doses for

<!-- chunk -->

### larger people or people who are less sensitive to herbs and supplements. You can

<!-- chunk -->

### also adjust the dose upward if the results you’re getting aren’t strong enough or

<!-- chunk -->

### the condition is more severe.

<!-- chunk -->

### Develop your intuition when dosing herbs by paying attention to your own

<!-- chunk -->

### body, especially when using liquids. Generally speaking, a formula will be

<!-- chunk -->

### tolerable (or even taste good) only as long as you need it. As soon as you have

<!-- chunk -->

### taken enough, the taste changes and you will not want any more. (An exception

<!-- chunk -->

### to this is digestive tonics: although you might not like the bitter taste to begin

<!-- chunk -->

### with, most of the time you begin to tolerate or even enjoy it within a few days.)

# DOSING STRATEGIES FOR CHILDREN

<!-- chunk -->

### Many herbalists calculate children’s dosages by age, but calculating by weight is

<!-- chunk -->

### just as easy and probably a bit more accurate.

<!-- chunk -->

## Clark’s Rule (for children ages two to seventeen)

<!-- chunk -->

### Divide the child’s weight in pounds by 150 (which is the weight that is assumed

<!-- chunk -->

### for adults when calculating adult doses) and multiply the result by the adult dose

<!-- chunk -->

### to find the equivalent child’s dosage.

<!-- chunk -->

### (Weight in pounds ÷ 150) × adult dose = child’s dose

<!-- chunk -->

### If the adult dose of a tincture is 5 milliliters and the child weighs 30 pounds,

<!-- chunk -->

### divide the weight (30) by 150: 30/150 = ⅕. Multiply ⅕ times the adult dose (5

<!-- chunk -->

### milliliters) to get 1 milliliter.

<!-- chunk -->

## Fried’s Rule (for children under two years old)

<!-- chunk -->

### Divide the age of the child (in months) by 150. Multiply the result by the adult

<!-- chunk -->

### dose.

<!-- chunk -->

### (Age of child in months ÷ 150) × average adult dose = child’s dose

<!-- chunk -->

### If the adult dose of tincture is 2 milliliters and the child is one year (12

<!-- chunk -->

### months) old, divide the age of the child (12 months) by 150 to get 0.08. Multiply

<!-- chunk -->

### this by the adult dose (2 milliliters) to get 0.16 milliliters for the child’s dose.

<!-- chunk -->

### In a normal hydroalcoholic (water plus alcohol) extract there are around

<!-- chunk -->

### drops per milliliter. Multiply 0.16 times 25 to get 4 drops per dose.

# EXPECTED RESULTS

<!-- chunk -->

### It doesn’t take forever to see results. The following are general guidelines for

<!-- chunk -->

### when you should begin to see improvement if you are on the right track.

<!-- chunk -->

### With acute illnesses, small, frequent doses work best. Dose every 15 minutes

<!-- chunk -->

### up to every 2 hours. You should see improvement within 1–3 hours.

<!-- chunk -->

### Subacute illness happens when you don’t treat an acute illness and it lingers.

<!-- chunk -->

### Dose every 2–4 hours or 4–8 times daily. You should see improvement within

<!-- chunk -->

### 24–48 hours.

<!-- chunk -->

### Chronic illness is illness that has been around for more than a week or two.

<!-- chunk -->

### When dealing with chronic conditions, dosing is usually 2–4 times daily. Some

<!-- chunk -->

### improvement should occur within 7–10 days.

<!-- chunk -->

### Degenerative illness occurs when the body is extremely run-down. Dosing

<!-- chunk -->

### strategies are similar to those for chronic illness, but it takes as long as 1–3

<!-- chunk -->

### weeks to see any significant improvement.


<!-- chunk -->

## CHAPTER TWELVE

# Sample Formulas

<!-- chunk -->

## SOME OF OUR FAVORITE FORMULAS

<!-- chunk -->

### he following are some sample formulas designed using the principles

<!-- chunk -->

### outlined in Chapter Eleven. When reading a formula, keep in mind that a

<!-- chunk -->

### part can be anything from a liquid ounce to a pound depending on how big a

<!-- chunk -->

### batch you want to make. In most formulas, start by making a small batch using

<!-- chunk -->

### ounce to equal 1 part.

# LIQUID FORMULAS

<!-- chunk -->

### These are formulas designed to be made as infusions (teas), decoctions,

<!-- chunk -->

### tinctures, glycerites, or other liquid preparations.

# ANTIALLERGY FORMULA

<!-- chunk -->

### This formula reduces allergic reactions in cases of hay fever, red and itchy eyes,

<!-- chunk -->

### watery sinus drainage, and frequent earaches. Mix together:

<!-- chunk -->

### 4 parts eyebright (key herb)

<!-- chunk -->

### 4 parts nettle leaf (key herb)

<!-- chunk -->

### 2 parts goldenrod (supporting herb)

<!-- chunk -->

### 2 parts burdock root (supporting herb)

<!-- chunk -->

### 1 part blessed thistle (supporting herb)

<!-- chunk -->

### 1 part bitter orange peel (catalyst)

<!-- chunk -->

### Prepare from individual tinctures, or macerate at 1:5 in 50% alcohol or as a

<!-- chunk -->

### 1:6 glycerite.

# ANTIFUNGAL FORMULA

<!-- chunk -->

### This is an antifungal formula for oral thrush and gastric or vaginal yeast

<!-- chunk -->

### infections.

<!-- chunk -->

### 4 parts pau d’arco (key herb)

<!-- chunk -->

### 2 parts oregano (supporting herb)

<!-- chunk -->

### 1 part usnea (supporting herb)

<!-- chunk -->

### 1 part reishi mushroom (balancing herb)

<!-- chunk -->

### 1 part black walnut (balancing herb)

<!-- chunk -->

### ½ part thyme (catalyst)

<!-- chunk -->

### Prepare from individual tinctures or make as a 1:6 glycerite.

# ANTISEPTIC GARGLE

<!-- chunk -->

### This is a basic formula for an antiseptic gargle. Mix together:

<!-- chunk -->

### 1 part myrrh gum (key herb)

<!-- chunk -->

### 1 part bayberry root bark (supporting herb)

<!-- chunk -->

### ¼ part clove buds (catalyst)

<!-- chunk -->

### Prepare as a 1:6 glycerite. Mix the finished glycerite with an equal amount (by

<!-- chunk -->

### volume) of Echinacea angustifolia root tincture.

# ANTISEPTIC MOUTHWASH

<!-- chunk -->

### This is a formula for making your own antiseptic mouthwash.

<!-- chunk -->

### 2 parts peppermint (balancing herb)

<!-- chunk -->

### 1 part myrrh (key herb)

<!-- chunk -->

### 1 part Echinacea angustifolia root (supporting herb)

<!-- chunk -->

### ½ part thyme (optional supporting herb)

<!-- chunk -->

### Prepare from individual tinctures, or macerate at 1:5 in 50% alcohol. Add an

<!-- chunk -->

### equal volume of glycerin to the combination of tinctures along with 1 drop

<!-- chunk -->

### peppermint essential oil for every 4 ounces mouthwash.

# ANTISPASMODIC TINCTURE

<!-- chunk -->

### This tincture can be applied topically to relax muscle spasms and ease pain. It

<!-- chunk -->

### can also be used internally at the first sign of a cold or the flu.

<!-- chunk -->

### 1 part lobelia (key herb)

<!-- chunk -->

### 1 part capsicum (supporting herb)

<!-- chunk -->

### ½ part black cohosh (supporting herb)

<!-- chunk -->

### ½ part blue cohosh (supporting herb)

<!-- chunk -->

### ½ part prickly ash (supporting herb)

<!-- chunk -->

### ½ part clove (catalyst)

<!-- chunk -->

### Tincture the herbs in 100-proof alcohol at a 1:5 ratio and macerate for

<!-- chunk -->

### days. This formula can also be made using the sealed simmer glycerite process.

# ANTIVIRAL FORMULA

<!-- chunk -->

### Antiviral herbs typically target one family of viruses. This formula is specific to

<!-- chunk -->

### the influenza virus.

<!-- chunk -->

### 4 parts Chinese scullcap (key herb)

<!-- chunk -->

### 3 parts boneset (supporting herb)

<!-- chunk -->

### 2 parts licorice (balancing herb)

<!-- chunk -->

### 2 parts red root (supporting herb)

<!-- chunk -->

### 1 part lomatium (supporting herb)

<!-- chunk -->

### 1 part isatis (supporting herb)

<!-- chunk -->

### Prepare from individual tinctures, or macerate at 1:5 in 50% alcohol. Take

<!-- chunk -->

### drops (2 milliliters) every hour with fresh ginger juice tea.

# BALANCED BITTERS

<!-- chunk -->

### This is a formula for stimulating digestive secretions in cases of poor appetite or

<!-- chunk -->

### poor digestion. Mix together the following:

<!-- chunk -->

### (which will increase its bitterness, but improve its effectiveness). You can also

<!-- chunk -->

### add 1 part peppermint as a supporting and balancing herb to improve the flavor.

<!-- chunk -->

# CHILDREN’S COMPOSITION

<!-- chunk -->

### This is a formula that has the same effect on children as Herbal Composition and

<!-- chunk -->

### Herbal Crisis do on adults. It is used for colds, flu, fevers, sore throats, upset

<!-- chunk -->

### stomachs, and other acute illnesses. It is antiviral, reduces fever, and stimulates

<!-- chunk -->

### digestion and circulation. It is not as strong a decongestant as Herbal

<!-- chunk -->

### Composition or Herbal Crisis. For serious congestion, also take the Herbal

<!-- chunk -->

### Cough Syrup (Drying) formula listed below.

<!-- chunk -->

### 1 part yarrow (key herb)

<!-- chunk -->

### 1 part elder flower (key herb)

<!-- chunk -->

### 1 part peppermint (balancing herb and catalyst)

<!-- chunk -->

### This formula works best when prepared as a sealed simmer glycerite. It can

<!-- chunk -->

### also be made into a syrup or tincture or prepared as an infusion. The formula can

<!-- chunk -->

### be modified by adding any of the following ingredients.

<!-- chunk -->

### ½ part chamomile (supporting herb for digestion, nerves, and reducing

<!-- chunk -->

### inflammation)

<!-- chunk -->

### ½ part lemon balm (supporting herb for antiviral effect and balancing herb for

<!-- chunk -->

### flavor)

<!-- chunk -->

### ½ part elder berries (supporting herb for antiviral effect and balancing herb for

<!-- chunk -->

### flavor)

<!-- chunk -->

### ½ part catnip (supporting herb for digestion and fever reduction)

<!-- chunk -->

### The formula can be used internally at the first sign of cold or flu. Allow

<!-- chunk -->

### children to take as much as they would like in small doses every 15–20 minutes

<!-- chunk -->

### with plenty of water.

# COMPOUND WINE OF SOLOMON’S SEAL

<!-- chunk -->

### This is a tonic formula, beneficial for digestion and structural inflammation.

<!-- chunk -->

<!-- chunk -->

### 2 parts plantain leaf (supporting herb)

<!-- chunk -->

### 1 part wild cherry bark (supporting herb)

<!-- chunk -->

<!-- chunk -->

### ½ part gentian root (supporting herb)

<!-- chunk -->

### ½ part ginger root (catalyst)

<!-- chunk -->

### To this, add sherry wine (20% fortified wine) 1:8, bring to a boil, remove from

<!-- chunk -->

### heat, and let steep while covered until cool. Once cool, transfer to a jar and

<!-- chunk -->

### macerate for 3 weeks. Strain and drink 1–2 ounces before meals, 3 times daily.

<!-- chunk -->

### Best kept refrigerated.

# ELDER BERRY SYRUP

<!-- chunk -->

### Pick fresh elder berries and remove as much of the stem as possible. Put berries

<!-- chunk -->

### into a pan, adding just enough water to cover the bottom of the pan. Heat berries

<!-- chunk -->

### on a medium-low burner. As the berries start to steam, mash the berries to

<!-- chunk -->

### release the juice. To increase the amount of juice extracted, puree with an

<!-- chunk -->

### immersion blender. Strain the juice through cheesecloth or a jelly bag. Measure

<!-- chunk -->

### the juice and add an equal amount of honey. Process in pint jars by placing in a

<!-- chunk -->

### boiling water bath for 15–20 minutes (adjusting for altitude). Remove from heat,

<!-- chunk -->

### place new, sterilized canning lids on jars, and let them cool, sealing the jars.

<!-- chunk -->

### They will keep for a year on the shelf, or longer in the refrigerator. Use as

<!-- chunk -->

### pancake syrup or as a tonic for colds, coughs, and viral infections.

# FIRE CIDER

<!-- chunk -->

### This is a great cold and flu remedy made as an herbal vinegar. The formula

<!-- chunk -->

### originated with Rosemary Gladstar. Prepare the following ingredients:

<!-- chunk -->

### ½ cup fresh horseradish root, grated (key herb)

<!-- chunk -->

### 1 medium onion, chopped (supporting herb)

<!-- chunk -->

### ½ cup fresh ginger root, grated (key herb)

<!-- chunk -->

### ¼ cup garlic, mashed (key herb)

<!-- chunk -->

### 2 jalapeño peppers, chopped (catalyst)

<!-- chunk -->

### 1 lemon (zest and juice) (balancing herb)

<!-- chunk -->

### 2 tablespoons dried rosemary leaves (optional balancing herb)

<!-- chunk -->

### Place all ingredients in a jar and cover with raw apple cider vinegar. Protect

<!-- chunk -->

### the metal lid with waxed paper or parchment paper before putting the lid on the

<!-- chunk -->

### jar. Keep the jar in a dark place and shake daily for 4 weeks. Strain, add honey to

<!-- chunk -->

### taste, and rebottle. As a cold and flu remedy, take 2 tablespoons up to 8 times

<!-- chunk -->

### daily as needed.

# GARLIC LEMON AID

<!-- chunk -->

### This is a simple, but great formula for fighting colds and respiratory infections.

<!-- chunk -->

### It tastes better than it sounds. Peel 1 lemon to remove the yellow part, leaving

<!-- chunk -->

### the white part of the peel intact. Cut the peeled lemon into quarters and place in

<!-- chunk -->

### a blender. Add 1–2 cloves garlic, ⅛–¼ cup honey or real maple syrup, and

<!-- chunk -->

### quart water. Blend until smooth, strain, and drink ½–1 cup at a time throughout

<!-- chunk -->

### the day.

### improves the function of the colon.

<!-- chunk -->

### prevent the oils in the flaxseed from going rancid.

# GINGER MAGIC

<!-- chunk -->

### Juice fresh ginger using a vegetable juicer. Measure the amount of ginger juice

<!-- chunk -->

### and add an equal amount of glycerin (or honey). Then add ¼ of that amount of

<!-- chunk -->

### brandy or spiced rum. So, if you added 1 cup glycerin, you’d add ¼ cup brandy.

<!-- chunk -->

### This is great for settling an upset stomach or for fighting colds and flu. Note:

<!-- chunk -->

### The resins will settle to the bottom of the jar. This formula is helpful because

<!-- chunk -->

### fresh ginger has anti-inflammatory properties that dry ginger does not.

# HERBAL COMPOSITION

<!-- chunk -->

### Herbal Composition is one of Samuel Thomson’s formulas. It is designed to

<!-- chunk -->

### “scour the bowels and remove the canker [mucus].” It is a decongestant,

<!-- chunk -->

### expectorant, and circulatory stimulant and can be used for most acute illnesses.

<!-- chunk -->

### To make it, mix the following herbal powders together:

<!-- chunk -->

### 4 parts bayberry root bark powder (key herb)

<!-- chunk -->

### 2 parts white pine bark powder (supporting herb)

<!-- chunk -->

### 1 part ginger root powder (supporting herb)

<!-- chunk -->

### ½ part clove powder (catalyst)

<!-- chunk -->

### ½ part capsicum powder (catalyst)

<!-- chunk -->

### Prepare as an infusion using 1–2 grams (¼–½ teaspoon) per cup of water.

<!-- chunk -->

### Drink for colds, flu, sweat baths, congestion, etc.

# HERBAL CRISIS (MODIFIED COMPOSITION)

<!-- chunk -->

### Herbal Crisis is a modified composition formula designed by Edward Milo

<!-- chunk -->

### Millet. It cuts mucus, stimulates circulation, acts as a decongestant, and fights

<!-- chunk -->

### infection.

<!-- chunk -->

### Mix the following together:

<!-- chunk -->

### 4 parts bayberry root bark (key herb)

<!-- chunk -->

### 2 parts white pine bark (supporting herb)

<!-- chunk -->

### 1 part goldenseal or myrrh gum (balancing herb)

<!-- chunk -->

### 1 part lobelia or blue vervain or boneset (balancing herb)

<!-- chunk -->

### 1 part ginger (supporting herb)

<!-- chunk -->

### ½ part clove (catalyst)

<!-- chunk -->

### ½ part capsicum (catalyst)

<!-- chunk -->

### This formula works best when made as a sealed simmer glycerite, although it

<!-- chunk -->

### can also be used as a tea, like Herbal Composition. Use internally for colds, flu,

<!-- chunk -->

### sore throats, respiratory congestion, early stages of infection, or other acute

<!-- chunk -->

### illnesses. The formula can also be used as a compress for insect bites, bee stings,

<!-- chunk -->

### and minor injuries. Use 2 grams (½ teaspoon) per quart of water as an enema

<!-- chunk -->

### solution.

# HERBAL MINERAL TONIC

<!-- chunk -->

### This is a formula of mineral-rich herbs for building the tissues of the body. Mix

<!-- chunk -->

### together the following herbs:

<!-- chunk -->

<!-- chunk -->

<!-- chunk -->

<!-- chunk -->

<!-- chunk -->

<!-- chunk -->

### 1 part horsetail (supporting herb)

<!-- chunk -->

### 1 part oak bark (supporting herb)

<!-- chunk -->

### Moisten the herbs in apple cider vinegar and let soak for 2 hours. Add the

<!-- chunk -->

### mixture to water (1:4) and simmer on low until the water is reduced by half.

<!-- chunk -->

### Then add:

<!-- chunk -->

### ½ part peppermint (catalyst)

<!-- chunk -->

### ½ part chamomile (catalyst)

<!-- chunk -->

### Remove from heat and let steep for 30 minutes. Strain and drink 1–4 cups

<!-- chunk -->

### daily.

<!-- chunk -->

### This formula can be made as a glycerite, simmering for at least 30 minutes to

<!-- chunk -->

### help draw the minerals out of the herbs, or it can be made as a vinegar extract. It

<!-- chunk -->

### isn’t an effective mineral tonic when made as a tincture.

# HOREHOUND COUGH SYRUP

<!-- chunk -->

### Make a horehound decoction using 1 ounce dried horehound or a small handful

<!-- chunk -->

### of fresh horehound leaves in 1 pint water. Simmer for 20 minutes, strain, and

<!-- chunk -->

### reduce to 1 cup. Add 2 cups honey or 1 cup honey and 1 cup dark brown raw

<!-- chunk -->

### sugar. Add the juice of 1 lime and ½ cup brandy. Store in a cool, dark place.

<!-- chunk -->

### Take 1–2 teaspoons as needed.

# BASIC HEALING SALVE

<!-- chunk -->

### more rapid regeneration of tissue.

<!-- chunk -->

### 24 hours. Strain. Add 1 ounce of beeswax for each 8 ounces of oil. See

<!-- chunk -->

### directions for making salves in the “Salves, Ointments, and Balms” section of

<!-- chunk -->

### Chapter Eight.

### 1 part yarrow (supporting herb)

# GARLIC-MULLEIN EAR OIL

<!-- chunk -->

### This oil is a very effective remedy for earache. Mullein flowers are soothing and

<!-- chunk -->

### anodyne, while the garlic fights infection.

<!-- chunk -->

### Pick mullein flowers and pack them into a jar (do not wash them). Fill the jar

<!-- chunk -->

### with olive oil. Crush some garlic cloves and put them in a separate jar. Cover

<!-- chunk -->

### them with olive oil, too. Infuse each mixture for 2 weeks, then strain. Mix the

<!-- chunk -->

### two oils in equal parts and bottle.

<!-- chunk -->

### St. John’s wort flowers can also be added to this oil. They help increase the

<!-- chunk -->

### pain-relieving action of the ear drops.

<!-- chunk -->

## Warning: Datura is a very potent addition to this formula, and makes it an

<!-- chunk -->

### excellent pain reliever. However, datura is very poisonous when taken internally.

<!-- chunk -->

<!-- chunk -->

### foot of skin per application. In some states, datura is illegal to grow and

<!-- chunk -->

### potentially to use. Please check your local laws before using it.

<!-- chunk -->
