# MILK THISTLE

<!-- chunk -->

## Latin name: Carduus marianus, syn. Silybum marianum

<!-- chunk -->

### There is good scientific evidence that the silymarin contained in milk thistle can

<!-- chunk -->

### protect the liver from various chemicals and toxins and aid in healing liver

<!-- chunk -->

### diseases. Milk thistle can prevent liver cell death from toxins like carbon

<!-- chunk -->

### tetrachloride and even the toxic effects of amanita (death cap) mushrooms. The

<!-- chunk -->

### flavonoids in milk thistle can increase the production of glutathione, a strong

<!-- chunk -->

### antioxidant.


<!-- chunk -->

### Moistening and cooling


<!-- chunk -->

### Alterative (blood purifier), cholagogue, galactagogue, and

<!-- chunk -->

### hepatoprotective


<!-- chunk -->

### Dried seeds (1:3, 70% alcohol); 3–8 ml (0.6–1.6 tsp.) up to 4 times


<!-- chunk -->

### 140–180 mg silymarin 3 times daily

# MIMOSA (ALBIZIA, SILK TREE)

<!-- chunk -->

## Latin name: Albizia julibrissin

<!-- chunk -->

### Mimosa is a traditional Chinese medicinal used to calm the spirit, relieve

<!-- chunk -->

### constriction and pain, invigorate the blood, and heal bone fractures. It is used to

<!-- chunk -->

### treat bad temper, depression, insomnia, irritability, and poor memory due to

<!-- chunk -->

### suppressed emotions. Modern Western herbalists use mimosa to relieve

<!-- chunk -->

### heartache, stress, and depression. Mimosa bark is mildly uplifting and

<!-- chunk -->

### grounding; it stretches energy upward and softens the heart. The flowers, on the

<!-- chunk -->

### other hand, concentrate energy in the head, causing mild euphoria and giddiness.

<!-- chunk -->

### It is an instant dose of feel-good that insists you take a walk in nature and enjoy

<!-- chunk -->

### life.


<!-- chunk -->

### Pregnant and breastfeeding women should avoid mimosa.


<!-- chunk -->

### Cooling, moistening, and slightly relaxing


<!-- chunk -->

### Antidepressant, calmative, euphoretic, relaxant, and vulnerary


<!-- chunk -->

### Freshly dried flowers (1:2, 50% alcohol); dried bark (1:5, 50%

<!-- chunk -->

### alcohol); 10 drops to 5 ml (1 tsp.) 1–4 times daily

# MISTLETOE

<!-- chunk -->

## Latin name: Viscum album

<!-- chunk -->

### Mistletoe is a powerful nervine. It is helpful for hypertension (without fluid

<!-- chunk -->

### retention), vasoconstrictive headaches, petit mal seizures, and tinnitus. In large

<!-- chunk -->

### doses, mistletoe induces hypertension. It is oxytocic and is used during labor to

<!-- chunk -->

### strengthen and normalize uterine contractions. Mistletoe is best mixed in small

<!-- chunk -->

### amounts with other nervines to increase the potency of sedative formulas.


<!-- chunk -->

### Use of this herb requires the supervision of a skilled herbalist or a

<!-- chunk -->

### physician.

<!-- chunk -->

### Mistletoe may act as an abortifacient, so pregnant women should not take it.

<!-- chunk -->

### There have been some reports of ingestion of mistletoe leading to adverse

<!-- chunk -->

### reactions and even death in animals and small children; it was determined that

<!-- chunk -->

### the substance ingested in these cases was probably another species of mistletoe

<!-- chunk -->

### that is not used medicinally. Adults need not fear poisoning unless large amounts

<!-- chunk -->

### are ingested.

<!-- chunk -->

### When mixed with prescription medicines that have a monoamine oxidase

<!-- chunk -->

### inhibitor (MAOI), mistletoe may cause a sudden drop in blood pressure.

<!-- chunk -->

### Mistletoe should not be taken with prescription blood pressure medications.


<!-- chunk -->

### Relaxing


<!-- chunk -->

### Cardiac, hypotensive, nervine, and sedative


<!-- chunk -->

### Frontier Herbs, Mountain Rose Herbs, Stony Mountain Botanicals


<!-- chunk -->

### Dried leaf (1:5, 50% alcohol); 15–30 drops 3 times daily

# MOTHERWORT

<!-- chunk -->

## Latin name: Leonurus cardiaca

<!-- chunk -->

### Motherwort is a calming nervine used to lower blood pressure and as a remedy

<!-- chunk -->

### for heart problems that are related to the nervous system. It relieves anxiety and

<!-- chunk -->

### nervousness and contains glycosides that temporarily lower blood pressure and

<!-- chunk -->

### ease the strain on heart muscles. This also makes it useful for tachycardia, heart

<!-- chunk -->

### palpitations, and preventing heart disease. Motherwort is useful for hot flashes,

<!-- chunk -->

### menstrual cramps, scanty menstrual flow, and vaginal pain characterized by

<!-- chunk -->

### weakness, nervous irritability, and stress.


<!-- chunk -->

### Avoid during pregnancy and menstruation with excessive bleeding.


<!-- chunk -->

### Cooling, drying, and relaxing


<!-- chunk -->

### Anti-arrhythmic, cardiac, emmenagogue, hypotensive, sedative,

<!-- chunk -->

### and vasodilator


<!-- chunk -->

### 2–4 ounces 1–4 times daily


<!-- chunk -->

### Fresh leaf (1:2, 95% alcohol); dried leaf (1:5, 60% alcohol); 1–4 ml

<!-- chunk -->

### (0.2–0.8 tsp.) 3 times daily

# MULLEIN

<!-- chunk -->

## Latin name: Verbascum spp.

<!-- chunk -->

### Mullein leaves are most commonly used for respiratory complaints. They have a

<!-- chunk -->

### soothing, hydrating effect on the lungs and contain saponins that loosen mucus.

<!-- chunk -->

### Mullein is often used for chronic lung problems such as asthma and COPD, but

<!-- chunk -->

### is also helpful for colds and coughs, particularly dry coughs. Mullein flowers are

<!-- chunk -->

### used to make ear drops to soothe earache. Mullein root is specific for low back

<!-- chunk -->

### pain and inflammation.


<!-- chunk -->

### Mullein seeds contain the poisonous substance rotenone. Mullein leaf

<!-- chunk -->

### and flowers are generally regarded as safe.


<!-- chunk -->

### Moistening and cooling


<!-- chunk -->

### Demulcent (mucilant), expectorant, and lung tonic


<!-- chunk -->

### 4–8 ounces 2–4 times daily. Prepare leaves in this manner

<!-- chunk -->

### for lung issues.


<!-- chunk -->

### Fresh root (1:2, 95% alcohol); dried root (1:5, 65% alcohol); 10 drops

<!-- chunk -->

### to 3 ml (0.6 tsp.) 3 times daily for back issues


<!-- chunk -->

### Dried leaf (1:8); 2.5–10 ml (0.5–2 tsp.) 3 times daily for lung issues


<!-- chunk -->

### Fresh flowers (1:4, in olive oil); used as ear drops

# MYRRH

### Myrrh is an aromatic and bitter resin with antiseptic and disinfectant qualities.

### Myrrh combines well with goldenseal, echinacea, and other herbs for fighting

<!-- chunk -->

### infection. Like other antiseptic herbs, myrrh must come in contact with the

### bacteria to have an effect. It is especially helpful when used as a gargle,

<!-- chunk -->

### mouthwash, or liniment. Myrrh helps heal wounds, and can be blended with aloe

<!-- chunk -->

### vera gel to form a soothing topical wound protector. It is also a bitter tonic for

<!-- chunk -->

### digestion.

### Avoid taking internally while pregnant.

### disinfectant

# NETTLE (STINGING)

<!-- chunk -->

## Latin name: Urtica dioica

<!-- chunk -->

### Nettles are a nourishing herbal food, rich in iron, calcium, magnesium, protein,

<!-- chunk -->

### and other nutrients. Nettles help to build healthy blood, bones, joints, and skin.

<!-- chunk -->

### Nettles are an excellent remedy for anemia, low blood pressure, and general

<!-- chunk -->

### weakness. They increase excretion of uric acid and help with rheumatism and

<!-- chunk -->

### gout. Nettles have anti-inflammatory and anti-allergenic properties, making them

<!-- chunk -->

### useful for respiratory allergies, asthma, and eruptive skin diseases. A blend of

<!-- chunk -->

### nettles, red raspberry, and alfalfa makes a great tonic tea for pregnancy.

<!-- chunk -->

### Nettle seeds can slow, halt, or even partially reverse progressive renal failure.

<!-- chunk -->

### Studies have shown the root to improve benign prostatic hypertrophy (BPH)

<!-- chunk -->

### symptoms in 81% of men taking the herb (compared with 16% improvement in

<!-- chunk -->

### the placebo group).


<!-- chunk -->

### Nettle is extremely safe. The live plant can cause skin irritation

<!-- chunk -->

### (hence the name “stinging”), but the dried plant does not produce this effect.


<!-- chunk -->

### Neutral and nourishing


<!-- chunk -->

### Anti-allergenic, anti-inflammatory, antihistamine, diuretic, kidney

<!-- chunk -->

### tonic, mast cell stabilizer, mineralizer, and tonic


<!-- chunk -->

### Rolla Thomas: Chronic diarrhea or dysentery with

<!-- chunk -->

### evacuations of mucus; chronic inflammation of bladder with abundant mucus

<!-- chunk -->

### discharge.


<!-- chunk -->

### 8 ounces 1–4 times daily

# NOPAL (PRICKLY PEAR)

<!-- chunk -->

## Latin name: Opuntia streptacantha, O. ficus-indica

<!-- chunk -->

### Nopal is helpful for type 2 diabetes (reducing blood sugar) and has a very low

<!-- chunk -->

### glycemic index. It contains potent antioxidants to reduce inflammation, which is

<!-- chunk -->

### a primary driver of most chronic diseases. The warmed pads make wonderful

<!-- chunk -->

### poultices for infected wounds; make sure to place the goopy inside in contact

<!-- chunk -->

### with the skin. Be sure to remove the smooth, fixed spines and the small, hair-like

<!-- chunk -->

### prickles called glochids before using nopal. Nopal pads are available in many

<!-- chunk -->

### Mexican grocery stores.


<!-- chunk -->

### Analgesic (anodyne), anti-inflammatory, and antidiabetic


<!-- chunk -->

### Remove the spines from the pads with a sharp knife or potato

<!-- chunk -->

### peeler. Remove remaining glochids by holding the pad over a flame. Cut the pad

<!-- chunk -->

### in half, apply warm, and secure in place. Change as needed. The flesh can also

<!-- chunk -->

### be dried on a fruit leather tray in a dehydrator and powdered; reconstitute with

<!-- chunk -->

### water for an instant demulcent.

# OAT

<!-- chunk -->

## Latin name: Avena sativa

<!-- chunk -->

### The milky (unripe) seeds of the oat grain are used as a remedy for a depleted

<!-- chunk -->

### nervous system. Milky oats is a tonic that combines well with almost every other

<!-- chunk -->

### nervine. It works best for people with mental and physical exhaustion who are

<!-- chunk -->

### irritable and lack focus. They may also experience heart palpitations and loss of

<!-- chunk -->

### libido. Milky oats are used to support recovery from drug addiction.

<!-- chunk -->

### Like horsetail, oat straw is rich in silica. It is used as a mineralizer and a mild

<!-- chunk -->

### nervine. Oat bran is used as a bulk laxative and an agent to help lower

<!-- chunk -->

### cholesterol.


<!-- chunk -->

### Use cautiously with gluten sensitivity or allergy.


<!-- chunk -->

### Neutral, moistening, and nourishing


<!-- chunk -->

<!-- chunk -->

<!-- chunk -->

# OSHA

<!-- chunk -->

## Latin name: Ligusticum porteri

<!-- chunk -->

### Osha is a great remedy for viral infections such as the common cold, flu, sore

<!-- chunk -->

### throats, and upper respiratory congestion. It stimulates the digestive and immune

<!-- chunk -->

### systems and expels mucus. It is used for settling the stomach after vomiting and

<!-- chunk -->

### can be used with eyebright to prevent and treat earaches in children.

<!-- chunk -->

### Osha has been overharvested in the wild, and is very difficult if not impossible

<!-- chunk -->

### to cultivate. It is on the United Plant Savers at-risk list. Pine, grindelia, and

<!-- chunk -->

### ginger combined together make a nice replacement for osha. Until populations in

<!-- chunk -->

### the wild are stabilized, please limit your use of this wonderful plant.


<!-- chunk -->

### Antiviral, decongestant, and expectorant


<!-- chunk -->

<!-- chunk -->

<!-- chunk -->

### due to vasoconstriction. Clinical studies suggest periwinkle may be helpful in

<!-- chunk -->

### treating dementia, Alzheimer’s disease, short-term memory loss caused by some

<!-- chunk -->

### medications, high blood pressure, age-related hearing loss, and vertigo, and

<!-- chunk -->

### reducing calcium buildup from dialysis. Periwinkle can also help stop internal

<!-- chunk -->

### bleeding.


<!-- chunk -->

### Do not use during pregnancy. Avoid with low blood pressure and

<!-- chunk -->

### liver and kidney diseases. Periwinkle is best used under professional supervision.


<!-- chunk -->

### Drying and relaxing


<!-- chunk -->

### Astringent, hypotensive, sedative, and styptic


<!-- chunk -->

### 2–4 ounces up to 3 times daily


<!-- chunk -->

### Fresh leaf (1:2, 95% alcohol); dried leaf (1:5, 50% alcohol); 5 drops

<!-- chunk -->

### to 1 ml (0.2 tsp.) up to 3 times daily

# PINE (WHITE)

<!-- chunk -->

## Latin name: Pinus strobus and other species

<!-- chunk -->

### Pine bark is used primarily as an expectorant for coughs. It helps discharge

<!-- chunk -->

### mucus and fight infection. It is particularly helpful in cases of chronic bronchitis,

<!-- chunk -->

### and for coaxing old, thick, green mucus up and out of the lungs and sinuses.

<!-- chunk -->

### Pine gum is a good agent for wound healing and drawing out pus and slivers.

<!-- chunk -->

### Pine pollen also strengthens muscles and tendons and helps tissue regeneration

<!-- chunk -->

### and repair. The pollen contains testosterone and is used as a male glandular

<!-- chunk -->

### tonic.


<!-- chunk -->

### Antiseptic, aromatic, drawing, expectorant, and testosterone-

<!-- chunk -->

### enhancing


<!-- chunk -->

<!-- chunk -->

### Gum (1:4); dried or fresh bark (1:8); 10–20 ml (1–2 tsp.) 3–4 times


# PIPSISSEWA

## Latin name: Chimaphila umbellata

<!-- chunk -->

### Antibacterial and astringent, pipsissewa is used primarily for urinary problems

<!-- chunk -->

### involving inflammation, such as cystitis, prostatitis, and urethritis. It is a great

<!-- chunk -->

### remedy for irritable bladder. It has the same urinary disinfectant compounds as

<!-- chunk -->

# PLANTAIN

## Latin name: Plantago major

### This common lawn and garden weed is a valuable remedy for bruises, insect

<!-- chunk -->

### bites, and injuries when applied topically. Plantain is used internally for ulcers,

<!-- chunk -->

### inflammatory bowel disorders, and coughs. It is helpful for drawing sticky

<!-- chunk -->

<!-- chunk -->

### Cooling, moistening, and slightly constricting


<!-- chunk -->

<!-- chunk -->

<!-- chunk -->

# PLEURISY ROOT

<!-- chunk -->

## Latin name: Asclepias tuberosa

<!-- chunk -->

### Native Americans used pleurisy root for ailments relating to the heart,

<!-- chunk -->

### bronchials, and lungs. As its name implies, it is one of the best remedies for

<!-- chunk -->

### pleurisy. It eases chest pain and is good for hot, dry conditions of the chest.

<!-- chunk -->

### Pleurisy root is an excellent diaphoretic: it relaxes the peripheral capillaries and

<!-- chunk -->

### increases perspiration.


<!-- chunk -->

### Excessive doses may cause vomiting. Not recommended for use

<!-- chunk -->

### during pregnancy.

<!-- chunk -->

### inflammation.

# POKE ROOT

<!-- chunk -->

## Latin name: Phytolacca decandra

<!-- chunk -->

### Poke root is a traditional antitumor remedy that is also used to clear the

<!-- chunk -->

### lymphatic system. It is antiviral and an immune stimulant. Poke root oil is used

<!-- chunk -->

### topically for swollen lymph nodes, mastitis, or breast cancer. Its use is similar to,

<!-- chunk -->

### though stronger than, that of castor oil packs. A tincture of the berries or root is

<!-- chunk -->

### used internally in small doses for severe lymphatic congestion and mastitis.


<!-- chunk -->

### The main toxic compounds in poke are the pokeweed mitogen

<!-- chunk -->

### (lectins) and the glycoside saponin, along with the alkaloid phytolaccin. The

<!-- chunk -->

### alkaloids are mucosal irritants and mitogenic, and they affect the medulla in the

<!-- chunk -->

### brain, causing paralysis, bradycardia, decreased respiration, and decreased

<!-- chunk -->

### skeletal muscle coordination. The alkaloids can build up in the body for up to

<!-- chunk -->

### weeks. If any toxic effects are noticed, stop the use of this herb immediately.

<!-- chunk -->

### Toxic effects include: vomiting, diarrhea, nausea, stomach cramps, dizziness,

<!-- chunk -->

### hypotension, decreased respiration, and headaches. Avoid use for those with

<!-- chunk -->

### kidney disease. For professional use only, because of its toxicity, especially

<!-- chunk -->

### internally. Not for use during pregnancy.


<!-- chunk -->

### Alterative (blood purifier), anti-inflammatory, anticancer, immune

<!-- chunk -->

### stimulant, and lymphatic


<!-- chunk -->

### Fyfe: Enlargement and inflammation of glandular

<!-- chunk -->

### structures; mucous membranes pallid. Impaired glandular secretion and function.


<!-- chunk -->

### Recently dried root (1:10, 45% alcohol); 1–10 drops up to 3 times

<!-- chunk -->

### daily. Take no more than 10 ml a week.


<!-- chunk -->

### Fresh roots (1:4). Caution: Wear rubber gloves while chopping and handling

<!-- chunk -->

### roots. Macerate for 6 weeks.

# POLYGALA

<!-- chunk -->

## Latin name: Polygala tenuifolia

<!-- chunk -->

### Polygala is an underutilized traditional Chinese medicine remedy for anxiety and

<!-- chunk -->

### fear. Its effects are stronger than those of many commonly used anxiolytics, and

<!-- chunk -->

### it’s a valuable addition to nerve sedative formulas. It is beneficial for stress-

<!-- chunk -->

### induced memory loss, and initial studies show promising benefits in age-related

<!-- chunk -->

### cognitive decline and Alzheimer’s disease.


<!-- chunk -->

### Large doses can cause nausea and vomiting. Not for use by people

<!-- chunk -->

### who have gastritis or ulcers. Not for use during pregnancy.


<!-- chunk -->

### Dried root (1:5, 50% alcohol); 1–2 ml (0.2–0.4 tsp.) 3 times daily

# PROPOLIS

<!-- chunk -->

### Propolis is not an herb; it is a plant resin collected by bees. It is warming and

<!-- chunk -->

### stimulating. It has powerful antibiotic and immune-enhancing effects. Its

<!-- chunk -->

### antimicrobial activity is due largely to its richness in phenolic aglycones.


<!-- chunk -->

### Avoid propolis if you have bee allergies.


<!-- chunk -->

### Antibacterial, antifungal, expectorant, and immune amphoteric


<!-- chunk -->

### Propolis gum (1:5, 95% alcohol); 5 drops to 2 ml (0.4 tsp.) up to

<!-- chunk -->

### times daily. Spray directly onto tonsils or wound to create a resinous covering

<!-- chunk -->

### like a liquid Band-Aid. Give in a little honey for internal use. Don’t give in

<!-- chunk -->

### water.


# PSYLLIUM

<!-- chunk -->

## Latin name: Plantago spp.

<!-- chunk -->

### Psyllium is a mucilaginous herb used as a bulk laxative, as an antidiarrhea

<!-- chunk -->

### remedy, or to soothe intestinal irritation. It is best taken first thing in the morning

<!-- chunk -->

### or right before going to bed. Take before meals to help regulate appetite and

<!-- chunk -->

### blood sugar. It also helps to lower cholesterol. Always take psyllium with plenty

<!-- chunk -->

### of water or other liquids.


<!-- chunk -->

### Psyllium is a mild laxative, suitable for use by children, by the

<!-- chunk -->

### elderly, and during pregnancy, but should be avoided in cases of bowel

<!-- chunk -->

### obstruction or perforations. It can cause constipation when a person is

<!-- chunk -->

### dehydrated.


<!-- chunk -->

### Absorbent, antidiarrheal, demulcent (mucilant), and bulk laxative


<!-- chunk -->

### 1,000–5,000 mg up to 3 times daily mixed with water or

<!-- chunk -->

### juice. Drink a large glass of water after consuming.

# PULSATILLA

<!-- chunk -->

## Latin name: Pulsatilla vulgaris

<!-- chunk -->

### Pulsatilla helps promote restful sleep and counteract feeling overwrought,

<!-- chunk -->

### overemotional, high strung, and nervous. It helps with shock, despondency,

<!-- chunk -->

### imagined fears of impending danger, depression, PMS, spontaneous crying, and

<!-- chunk -->

### related emotional states. It’s also helpful for stress-induced headaches and

<!-- chunk -->

### headaches associated with menstruation.


<!-- chunk -->

### Pulsatilla is a strong gastric irritant, and overdoses cause violent

<!-- chunk -->

### gastroenteritis, vomiting, and diarrhea. Overdoses can also cause elevated heart

<!-- chunk -->

### rate, anxiety, and convulsions. This herb is recommended for use by

<!-- chunk -->

### professionals only. Not for use during pregnancy or lactation.


<!-- chunk -->

### Antidepressant, antirheumatic, and nervine


<!-- chunk -->

### Felter: Nervousness with despondency, sadness, and

<!-- chunk -->

### disposition to weep, without being able to tell why, or to weep while asleep;

<!-- chunk -->

### unnatural fear; fear of impending danger or death; insomnia, with nervous

<!-- chunk -->

### exhaustion; pain, with debility; headache, with nervousness, not dependent on

<!-- chunk -->

### determination of blood to the head; amenorrhea with chilliness and mental

<!-- chunk -->

### depression; dysmenorrhea, with gloomy mentality and chilliness; pasty, creamy,

<!-- chunk -->

### or white coating upon the tongue, with greasy taste; thick, bland, and inoffensive

<!-- chunk -->

### mucus discharges; alternating constipation and diarrhea, with venous congestion.


<!-- chunk -->

### Freshly dried herb (1:5, 50% alcohol); 1–3 drops 3 times daily diluted

<!-- chunk -->

### in water, or as a small part of a formula

# REISHI (GANODERMA)

<!-- chunk -->

## Latin name: Ganoderma lucidum

<!-- chunk -->

### Reishi is a medicinal mushroom that has been shown to have immune-enhancing

<!-- chunk -->

### effects as well as acting as a general health tonic. Research suggests that reishi

<!-- chunk -->

### relaxes muscles, improves sleep, eases chronic pain, aids heart function, reduces

<!-- chunk -->

### cholesterol, and has antioxidant effects. Alcohol is the preferred menstruum for

<!-- chunk -->

### extraction of the relaxing and anxiolytic properties. Decoction is preferred for

<!-- chunk -->

### immune support. Dual extracts can be used, but these do not work as well as

<!-- chunk -->

### using either alcohol or water extracts separately.


<!-- chunk -->

### This herb is generally regarded as safe and nontoxic. However, it

<!-- chunk -->

### may be contraindicated with fluid deficiency and dryness.


<!-- chunk -->

# SAGE

<!-- chunk -->

## Latin name: Salvia officinalis

<!-- chunk -->

### Sage can be helpful for colds and fever, especially those involving intermittent

<!-- chunk -->

### chills and fever, hoarseness, or sweating at night. It is best taken as a cool tea for

<!-- chunk -->

### night sweats and as a hot tea to induce perspiration. The tea may be used for sore

<!-- chunk -->

### or hoarse throat and laryngitis and as a mouthwash or gargle for irritation of the

<!-- chunk -->

### throat or mouth. The tea is a nerve tonic, increasing the capacity to handle stress.

<!-- chunk -->

### Sage is antiseptic, used topically to prevent infection and inflammation in

<!-- chunk -->

### wounds.


<!-- chunk -->

### Not recommended as a medicinal herb during pregnancy. Sage will

<!-- chunk -->

### dry up breast milk, so it should be avoided by nursing mothers.

# SCULLCAP (SKULLCAP)

<!-- chunk -->

## Latin name: Scutellaria lateriflora

<!-- chunk -->

### A relaxing nervine, scullcap helps to calm brain function and is helpful for

<!-- chunk -->

### insomnia and chronic stress. It is also a good remedy for vasoconstrictive (or

<!-- chunk -->

### tension) headaches and migraines. It was used by nineteenth-century herbalists

<!-- chunk -->

### for hysteria, epilepsy, convulsions, and schizophrenia. The person who needs

<!-- chunk -->

### scullcap often has an inability to pay attention or a dull headache in the front or

<!-- chunk -->

### base of the skull. Symptoms are worse with noise, odors, and light, but improve

<!-- chunk -->

### with rest. Scullcap also seems to work well when people feel as if every sound,

<!-- chunk -->

### touch, and ray of light is personally attacking them. These people are

<!-- chunk -->

### oversensitive to any stimulation, and are twitchy even during sleep. Scullcap

<!-- chunk -->

### seems to works best as a tonic when tinctured fresh. Dried scullcap has a more

<!-- chunk -->

### sedative action.


<!-- chunk -->

### Analgesic (anodyne), antispasmodic, nervine, sedative, and

<!-- chunk -->

### soporific (hypnotic)


<!-- chunk -->

### Rolla Thomas: Hysteria with inability to control the

<!-- chunk -->

### voluntary muscles; nervousness manifesting itself in muscular action.


<!-- chunk -->

### Fresh aerial parts (1:2, 95% alcohol); recently dried leaf and flower

<!-- chunk -->

### (1:5, 60% alcohol); 10 drops to 5 ml (1 tsp.) 2–4 times daily


<!-- chunk -->

### Dried herb (1:6); 2–5 ml (0.4–1 tsp.) 2–4 times daily

# SLIPPERY ELM

# ST. JOHN’S WORT

<!-- chunk -->

## Latin name: Hypericum perforatum

<!-- chunk -->

### St. John’s wort became a popular herb when research suggested it could be

<!-- chunk -->

### helpful for mild to moderate depression. It is indeed helpful for some cases of

<!-- chunk -->

### depression, especially those accompanied by anxiety, but the herb has many

<!-- chunk -->

### other valuable properties. It is a nervine herb that helps to regulate the solar

<!-- chunk -->

### plexus, which are the nerves that regulate digestion. It can be helpful for

<!-- chunk -->

### insomnia, fear, nerve pain, and nerve damage. It stimulates nerve regeneration

<!-- chunk -->

### and repair and helps heal wounds. St. John’s wort is antiviral and is used for

<!-- chunk -->

### infections such as shingles, herpes, mononucleosis, and flu.


<!-- chunk -->

### The fresh plant is phototoxic. It contains a chemical that changes to a

<!-- chunk -->

### toxin in the body after exposure to sunlight. This does not appear to be a

<!-- chunk -->

### problem when taking St. John’s wort internally. Avoid when taking SSRI

<!-- chunk -->

### antidepressants.


<!-- chunk -->

### Slightly warming


<!-- chunk -->

### Antidepressant, antiseptic, antiviral, digestive tonic, nervine, and

<!-- chunk -->

### vulnerary


<!-- chunk -->

### Rolla Thomas: It is claimed that it exerts a marked

<!-- chunk -->

### influence in relieving irritation in injuries of the spine, and in punctured or

<!-- chunk -->

### lacerated wounds of the extremities, preventing tetanus. Relieves the

<!-- chunk -->

### excruciating pain of such injuries.


<!-- chunk -->

### Company, Starwest Botanicals


<!-- chunk -->

### Fresh plant (1:2, 95% alcohol); 5 drops to 3 ml (0.6 tsp.) 3 times daily

# NOTE:

<!-- chunk -->

### Both tincture and infusion will turn a deep red when you’re using a good-

<!-- chunk -->

### quality herb. If it doesn’t turn red, you probably have old plant material or the

<!-- chunk -->

### wrong species.


<!-- chunk -->

### Fresh flowers (1:4); apply topically.


<!-- chunk -->

### 500–1,500 mg, 3–4 times daily; standardized capsules to 0.3%

<!-- chunk -->

### hypericin, take 300 mg, 3–4 times daily.

# THUJA

<!-- chunk -->

## Latin name: Thuja occidentalis

<!-- chunk -->

### The leaves of thuja are a strong antifungal remedy, useful for candida, athlete’s

<!-- chunk -->

### foot, and jock itch. They also have antiparasitic effects against ringworm,

<!-- chunk -->

### amoebic dysentery, and giardia. The leaves are antiviral.


<!-- chunk -->

### In the 1930s thuja was used as an abortifacient in Europe and North

<!-- chunk -->

### America. It is not recommended for use during pregnancy. Not recommended for

<!-- chunk -->

### long-term use, as the herb may irritate the kidneys. There is a risk of toxicity

<!-- chunk -->

### with the tincture, but not with the infusion.


<!-- chunk -->

### Anthelminthic, antifungal, antiparasitic, aromatic, emmenagogue,

<!-- chunk -->

### and expectorant


<!-- chunk -->

### Rolla Thomas: Syphilitic or other diseases of bad blood,

<!-- chunk -->

### with warty excrescences, or ulceration, showing prominence of papillae.


<!-- chunk -->

### Fresh needles (1:2, 95% alcohol); 0.5–1 ml (0.1–0.2 tsp.) 3 times


<!-- chunk -->

### The essential oil can be diluted 1:5 and applied to venereal warts

<!-- chunk -->

### (HPV), common warts, skin tags, and itchy skin conditions.

<!-- chunk -->

<!-- chunk -->

# TRIPHALA

<!-- chunk -->

## Latin name: Emblica officinalis, Terminalia bellirica, and Terminalia chebula

<!-- chunk -->

### Triphala is not an herb, but a blend of three fruits used in Ayurvedic medicine as

<!-- chunk -->

### a gentle laxative, bowel tonic, and blood purifier. The first fruit in this blend is

<!-- chunk -->

### haritaki (Terminalia chebula). It has a balanced energy, containing five flavors

<!-- chunk -->

### (bitter, sour, astringent, salty, and sweet). It is a mild laxative that also tones the

<!-- chunk -->

### intestinal membranes. It lubricates tissues and relaxes muscle spasms.

<!-- chunk -->

### The second fruit in this blend is bibhitaki (Terminalia bellirica), an

<!-- chunk -->

### antispasmodic herb that is pungent and warming. It is an expectorant and

<!-- chunk -->

### decongestant and is used to treat asthma, bronchial problems, and allergies.

<!-- chunk -->

### The third and final fruit in triphala is amalaki or Indian gooseberry (Emblica

<!-- chunk -->

### officinalis), which also has a balanced energy, containing five flavors (sour,

<!-- chunk -->

### astringent, sweet, pungent, and bitter). It contains a small amount of

<!-- chunk -->

### anthraquinones, but is also astringent, so it has both a laxative action and a

<!-- chunk -->

### bowel toning action. This means it corrects both constipation and diarrhea. It is a

<!-- chunk -->

### cooling remedy and is also used to treat ulcers, intestinal inflammation, burning

<!-- chunk -->

### sensations, skin eruptions, and skin infections.

<!-- chunk -->

### In various formulas these three fruits may also be referred to as belleric

<!-- chunk -->

### myrobalan fruit, chebulic myrobalan fruit, and Indian gooseberry fruit, or as

<!-- chunk -->

### harada fruit, amla fruit, and behada fruit. No matter what it is called, triphala is

<!-- chunk -->

### quite possibly the best formula for normalizing gastrointestinal function

<!-- chunk -->

### available.

<!-- chunk -->

### Besides normalizing colon function, triphala improves liver function, protects

<!-- chunk -->

### the liver against environmental toxins, and improves digestion. It is antioxidant

<!-- chunk -->

### and anti-inflammatory, so it slows aging and protects the body from degenerative

<!-- chunk -->

### disease. It enhances circulation, lowers blood pressure, and protects the heart. It

<!-- chunk -->

### helps expel mucus from the respiratory passages and fights infection. In

<!-- chunk -->

### Ayurvedic medicine, triphala is used for constipation, indigestion, flatulence,

<!-- chunk -->

### poor appetite, digestive headaches, sinus congestion, joint pain, and general

<!-- chunk -->

### toxicity.


<!-- chunk -->

### None


<!-- chunk -->

### Slightly cooling and slightly drying


<!-- chunk -->

### Anti-inflammatory, antioxidant, aperient, decongestant,

<!-- chunk -->

### expectorant, and hepatoprotective


<!-- chunk -->

### 1,000–2,000 mg, 1–2 times daily with food

# TURMERIC

<!-- chunk -->

## Latin name: Curcuma longa, syn. C. domestica

<!-- chunk -->

<!-- chunk -->

<!-- chunk -->

### gallstones. Its potent ability to reduce inflammation and ease chronic pain makes

<!-- chunk -->

<!-- chunk -->

### agitation.

# VALERIAN

<!-- chunk -->

## Latin name: Valeriana officinalis

<!-- chunk -->

### Valerian is a popular and potent nervine with strong tranquilizing effects on the

<!-- chunk -->

### central nervous system. It has been used to treat a wide variety of nervous

<!-- chunk -->

### system conditions, insomnia, and mild pain. Valerian seems to work most

<!-- chunk -->

### consistently on people with sympathetic excess, as seen by their enlarged pupil

<!-- chunk -->

### size. People with parasympathetic excess (with small pupils) are more prone to

<!-- chunk -->

### be stimulated by valerian.


<!-- chunk -->

### Not recommended for persons with “hot” disorders, i.e., high strung,

<!-- chunk -->

### nervous, and excitable. (Scullcap or passionflower are better for such

<!-- chunk -->

### individuals.) Not recommended for long-term use in large doses, although there

<!-- chunk -->

### is no risk of addiction. Does not generally cause drowsiness that could affect

<!-- chunk -->

### driving. Some people react “backward” to valerian and find it stimulating rather

<!-- chunk -->

### than sedating. Low thyroid and the dosage appear to be factors. Some persons

<!-- chunk -->

### using valerian may experience a “light” feeling, as if floating in air, and they

<!-- chunk -->

### may experience hallucinations at night.


<!-- chunk -->

### Relaxing and slightly warming


<!-- chunk -->

### Analgesic (anodyne), antispasmodic, nervine, sedative, and

<!-- chunk -->

### soporific (hypnotic)


<!-- chunk -->

### 4–8 ounces 30 minutes before bed


<!-- chunk -->

### Fresh root (1:2, 95% alcohol); dried root (1:5, 60% alcohol); 10 drops

<!-- chunk -->

### to 3 ml (0.6 tsp.) 30 minutes before bed for insomnia or up to 4 times daily for

<!-- chunk -->

### mild pain or stress


<!-- chunk -->

### 500–1,000 mg, 30 minutes before bed for insomnia or up to 4 times

<!-- chunk -->

### daily for mild pain or stress

# WHITE OAK

<!-- chunk -->

## Latin name: Quercus alba and other species

<!-- chunk -->

### A powerful astringent, white oak bark is used internally for hemorrhoids and

<!-- chunk -->

### varicose veins. A decoction can be used as a rectal injection for hemorrhoids, as

<!-- chunk -->

### a douche to stop bleeding, or as a fomentation for swelling, varicose veins, and

<!-- chunk -->

### other injuries. Use as a gargle for sore throats, or use as a mouthwash for

<!-- chunk -->

### bleeding gums. Use white oak bark powder with black walnut powder as a tooth

<!-- chunk -->

### powder for bleeding gums and loose teeth.


<!-- chunk -->

### Can be constipating when taken internally. Can interfere with

<!-- chunk -->

### digestion (take between meals). Contains large amounts of tannin, which may be

<!-- chunk -->

### associated with mouth and stomach cancer with consistent, long-term use. Use

<!-- chunk -->

### only for short periods internally. No warnings for external use.


<!-- chunk -->

### Antidiarrheal, antiseptic, antivenomous, astringent, hemostatic, and

<!-- chunk -->

### styptic

<!-- chunk -->

### sprinkled on eczema to dry the affected area.

# WILD CHERRY

<!-- chunk -->

## Latin name: Prunus serotina

<!-- chunk -->

### An aromatic and astringent, wild cherry has a long history of use in cough

<!-- chunk -->

### remedies (ever wondered why so many cough remedies are cherry-flavored?). It

<!-- chunk -->

### is a cooling remedy that expels phlegm and soothes and dries out mucous

<!-- chunk -->

### membranes, making it helpful for a variety of respiratory and digestive system

<!-- chunk -->

### problems. It may also help normalize histamine reactions in allergies. In

<!-- chunk -->

### traditional Chinese medicine, it is indicated when there is heart fire blazing,

<!-- chunk -->

### consisting of palpitations, mental restlessness, agitation, insomnia, rapid pulse,

<!-- chunk -->

### and a yellow-coated tongue with a red tip.


<!-- chunk -->

### There is a slight toxicity to wild cherry, so it should not be used in

<!-- chunk -->

### large amounts or for long periods of time. It contains hydrocyanic acid, which, in

<!-- chunk -->

### high doses, may cause spasms and difficulty breathing. Medicinal doses have

<!-- chunk -->

### never proved harmful. Not recommended for use by pregnant women.


<!-- chunk -->

### Astringent and expectorant


<!-- chunk -->

### Fyfe: Irregular or intermittent heart action; convulsive

<!-- chunk -->

### action due to overstrain. Irritation of stomach with cough, bronchial irritation.

<!-- chunk -->

### Impaired appetite. Lack of muscular tone.


<!-- chunk -->

### Many authors insist that a dried bark tincture is the safest and best preparation.

<!-- chunk -->

### After much experimentation, we believe the fresh bark tincture to be superior.

# WILD YAM

<!-- chunk -->

## Latin name: Dioscorea villosa

<!-- chunk -->

### Contrary to popular myth, wild yam is not a source of progesterone and is not a

<!-- chunk -->

### reliable herb for birth control. It contains compounds that are used in the

<!-- chunk -->

### synthetic production of hormones like progesterone, but these compounds are

<!-- chunk -->

### not converted to progesterone in the body, nor do they have a progesterone-like

<!-- chunk -->

### action.

<!-- chunk -->

### Wild yam is, however, a valuable antispasmodic and anti-inflammatory

<!-- chunk -->

### remedy. It has been used to ease menstrual cramps and ovarian pain, and is also

<!-- chunk -->

### helpful for irritable bowel and intestinal cramps (gripping). It has also been used

<!-- chunk -->

### in conditions like arthritis and neuralgia.


<!-- chunk -->

### Overdose may cause nausea, vomiting, and diarrhea.


<!-- chunk -->

### Cooling, slightly moistening, and relaxing


<!-- chunk -->

### Analgesic (anodyne), anti-inflammatory, and antispasmodic


<!-- chunk -->

### Felter: Spasmodic abdominal colic, nausea, with skin and

<!-- chunk -->

### conjunctiva yellow. Twisting, boring distress centered at umbilicus.


<!-- chunk -->

### Simmer long enough for decoction to release soapy

<!-- chunk -->

### bubbles, turn red, and smell like Christmas; 3–6 ounces 3 times daily

# WILLOW

<!-- chunk -->

## Latin name: Salix alba, Salix lucida, and other species

<!-- chunk -->

### Willow bark has long been used for pain, fevers, and inflammation. Its active

<!-- chunk -->

### compound, salicin, is the original source of the synthetic derivative aspirin. The

<!-- chunk -->

### action of white willow is much slower than that of the synthetic drug, but is less

<!-- chunk -->

### likely to cause stomach problems. Willow takes about 8 hours to kick in. It

<!-- chunk -->

### generally works best at easing pain when combined with other analgesic herbs.


<!-- chunk -->

### Not recommended with ulcers or a weak digestive system. Also not

<!-- chunk -->

### recommended during pregnancy.


<!-- chunk -->

### Analgesic (anodyne), anti-inflammatory, antiseptic, and febrifuge


<!-- chunk -->

### 500–2,000 mg up to 3 times daily


<!-- chunk -->

# WINTERGREEN

<!-- chunk -->

## Latin name: Gaultheria procumbens

<!-- chunk -->

### Wintergreen contains salicylic acid (a natural aspirin), which can help to reduce

<!-- chunk -->

### inflammation and pain. It has been taken internally as a tea to ease pain, but it is

<!-- chunk -->

### seldom used internally today. The oil of wintergreen, however, is frequently used

<!-- chunk -->

### as a topical analgesic.


<!-- chunk -->

### The essential oil of wintergreen can trigger contact dermatitis in

<!-- chunk -->

### some people. The essential oil should never be taken internally. People who are

<!-- chunk -->

### sensitive to aspirin should avoid wintergreen.


<!-- chunk -->

### Analgesic (anodyne), anesthetic, and anti-inflammatory


<!-- chunk -->

<!-- chunk -->

### for muscular pain.

# WORMWOOD

<!-- chunk -->

# YARROW

<!-- chunk -->

## Latin name: Achillea millefolium

<!-- chunk -->

### Yarrow’s hemostatic properties (ability to stem bleeding) made it the medication

<!-- chunk -->

### of choice for treating war injuries in ancient times. Yarrow leaves were

<!-- chunk -->

### traditionally applied to wounds to stop bleeding. Its hemostatic effects are not as

<!-- chunk -->

### reliable as proper bandaging and pressure, so it’s rarely used for bleeding now.

<!-- chunk -->

### However, yarrow can be taken internally to stop internal bleeding. Yarrow

<!-- chunk -->

### flowers are also a strong diaphoretic and are used to cool high fevers and help

<!-- chunk -->

### the body fight infection. A warm tea of yarrow is one of the best remedies for

<!-- chunk -->

### inducing a sweat and breaking a fever. Add a little peppermint to improve the

<!-- chunk -->

### flavor. The flowers are highest in the volatile oil during dry weather, so a fresh

<!-- chunk -->

### flower tincture or sealed simmer glycerite made from flowers gathered after a 3-

<!-- chunk -->

### week drought makes a very strong medicine.


<!-- chunk -->

### Yarrow is a safe remedy, but should be reserved for medicinal use

<!-- chunk -->

### and not taken regularly.


<!-- chunk -->

### Cooling, drying, and constricting


<!-- chunk -->

### Anti-inflammatory, antiviral, diaphoretic, febrifuge, hemostatic,

<!-- chunk -->

### and vulnerary


<!-- chunk -->

### Rolla Thomas: In irritative conditions of the urinary

<!-- chunk -->

### apparatus, strangury, and suppression of urine. The best results are obtained from

<!-- chunk -->

### the infusion.

<!-- chunk -->

<!-- chunk -->

## APPENDIX ONE

# Herbal Hydrotherapy

<!-- chunk -->

## COMBINING HERBS AND WATER FOR HEALING

<!-- chunk -->

### Hydrotherapy is a term that means water therapy, which is the use of water for

<!-- chunk -->

### therapeutic purposes. Water can be used as ice, hot or cold water, or steam to

<!-- chunk -->

### help ease pain, improve circulation, reduce inflammation, detoxify the body, or

<!-- chunk -->

### stimulate healing. Hydrotherapy treatments include enemas and douches, baths,

<!-- chunk -->

### foot soaks, and steams.

<!-- chunk -->

### Herbal hydrotherapy combines these water therapies with herbs. Herbs and

<!-- chunk -->

### water are powerful allies in healing a wide variety of ailments. This appendix

<!-- chunk -->

### covers the various ways of combining hydrotherapy and herbs for healing.

# ENEMAS

<!-- chunk -->

### Enemas can be an important therapeutic aid for healing the colon. They can be

<!-- chunk -->

### used to hydrate the bowel and cleanse the body. They are even more effective

<!-- chunk -->

### when herbs are added to the water used in the enema.

<!-- chunk -->

### Herbal enemas can be used to:

<!-- chunk -->

### ▪ Cleanse the colon by removing large intestinal impactions

<!-- chunk -->

### ▪ Soothe colon irritation and inflammation

<!-- chunk -->

### ▪ Promote healing of colon tissues

<!-- chunk -->

### ▪ Relax bowel spasms and ease severe gas and bloating

<!-- chunk -->

### ▪ Reduce fevers (the majority of all fevers in children will come down once the

<!-- chunk -->

### colon is cleared)

<!-- chunk -->

### ▪ Relieve respiratory and lymphatic congestion

<!-- chunk -->

### ▪ Restore healthy intestinal flora (when probiotics or healthy fecal material are

<!-- chunk -->

### used in the enema)

<!-- chunk -->

### ▪ Get rid of intestinal parasites

# HERBAL ENEMA SOLUTIONS

<!-- chunk -->

### Herbal enemas have been used extensively in Ayurveda (basti) and traditional

<!-- chunk -->

### Chinese medicine, with many of the most popular herbs either being nutritive or

<!-- chunk -->

### having strong antimicrobial and anti-inflammatory properties.

<!-- chunk -->

### Start by making the herbal enema solution using either 2 cups of a very strong

<!-- chunk -->

### tea or decoction per quart of water used in the enema, or add ½–2 teaspoons of

<!-- chunk -->

### tinctures or glycerites per quart of water. The water may be warm or cool,

<!-- chunk -->

### depending on the situation.

<!-- chunk -->

### Some good herbs for enemas include catnip (for fevers), pau d’arco (for

<!-- chunk -->

### fungal infections), lobelia or lavender (for spasms), aloe vera (for inflammation

<!-- chunk -->

### and tissue healing), Oregon grape (for infection), and anise (for gas and

<!-- chunk -->

### bloating). Several of the formulas in Chapter Twelve work very well as an

<!-- chunk -->

### enema. These include: Herbal Composition, Children’s Composition, and Herbal

<!-- chunk -->

### Crisis. These formulas are helpful for enemas for colds, fever, and congestion.

<!-- chunk -->

### The Herbal Crisis formula is particularly useful for clearing out sticky fecal

<!-- chunk -->

### matter as it loosens mucus.

<!-- chunk -->

### You can make an enema for infections using raw garlic. Place 1 clove raw

<!-- chunk -->

### garlic per quart of water in a blender. Blend thoroughly, then strain the solution,

<!-- chunk -->

### and use small amounts as a rectal injection. This works well for respiratory

<!-- chunk -->

### infections and fevers.

# HOW TO TAKE THE ENEMA

<!-- chunk -->

### Always test the enema solution to make sure it is warm, not hot or cold. If you

<!-- chunk -->

### place a couple of drops on your wrist (like testing a baby bottle), it should feel

<!-- chunk -->

### neutral, slightly warm, or slightly cool in temperature. Add cool or warm water

<!-- chunk -->

### to adjust the temperature if necessary, then put the enema solution into the

<!-- chunk -->

### enema bag or bucket. Release the catch on the tube and allow the solution to

<!-- chunk -->

### flow through the tube to clear all the air out of the tube, then close the catch.

<!-- chunk -->

### Lubricate the end of the enema tip and the rectal area with a petroleum jelly

<!-- chunk -->

### (Vaseline®), herbal salve, or other lubricant. Lie down on your left side and

<!-- chunk -->

### gently insert the tip into the rectum. Release the catch, and allow the solution to

<!-- chunk -->

### flow into the colon.

<!-- chunk -->

### During this process, anytime you feel pain or discomfort, stop the flow of

<!-- chunk -->

### enema solution. Wait a minute or two and perhaps even lightly massage the area

<!-- chunk -->

### where you feel the pain and discomfort. If it does not go away, then use the

<!-- chunk -->

### toilet, expelling the liquid and waste material. Then start again.

<!-- chunk -->

### Once the fluid flows freely into the colon while you are lying on your left

<!-- chunk -->

### side, you can move to your back and continue the procedure. Finish the

<!-- chunk -->

### procedure by lying on your right side.

<!-- chunk -->

### To do what is called a “high enema,” when you clear the entire colon from

<!-- chunk -->

### ascending to descending, you may need to fill the bag or enema bucket several

<!-- chunk -->

### times. In fact, the first few times you try this, you may not be able to do this at

<!-- chunk -->

### all. You may encounter spasms or other obstructions that won’t want to move.

<!-- chunk -->

### Don’t worry about this; just cleanse the colon as far as it wants to go.

<!-- chunk -->

### Don’t be discouraged; it took me several months of doing an enema once per

<!-- chunk -->

### week before I was able to get fluid past the halfway point in my colon due to a

<!-- chunk -->

### muscle spasm in my transverse colon. I finally learned to use lobelia or lavender

<!-- chunk -->

### oil (mentioned above) to relax that spasm and clean out the entire length of the

<!-- chunk -->

### colon.

# GIVING AN ENEMA TO A CHILD

<!-- chunk -->

### When giving a child or an infant an enema, you use a syringe. There are special

<!-- chunk -->

### syringes made just for giving enemas, but a regular bulb syringe can also be

<!-- chunk -->

### used. Prepare the enema solution and test the temperature as before.

<!-- chunk -->

### Place a towel on the floor and lay the child on his or her back or left side on

<!-- chunk -->

### the towel. When doing this with a baby, place a diaper on top of the towel.

<!-- chunk -->

### Explain to the child that this procedure will be uncomfortable, but it will help

<!-- chunk -->

### him or her feel better. Be gentle and patient. If you were taking the child to the

<!-- chunk -->

### doctor, the child might have to get a shot or have blood drawn, which would hurt

<!-- chunk -->

### far more, but you’d probably make the child hold still for that anyway, so think

<!-- chunk -->

### of it in this same manner. An enema is nowhere near as painful as a shot, and

<!-- chunk -->

### you can explain this to a child.

<!-- chunk -->

### Lubricate the anal opening and the tip of the syringe. Fill the syringe with the

<!-- chunk -->

### enema solution by squeezing the syringe and then sucking up the solution. Turn

<!-- chunk -->

### the syringe upright and squeeze any remaining air out of it. Fill it the rest of the

<!-- chunk -->

### way, so that the syringe is completely full. Gently insert the tip of the syringe

<!-- chunk -->

### into the anus. Then give a gentle squeeze. If you encounter strong resistance, or

<!-- chunk -->

### the child seems to be in pain, stop squeezing and withdraw the syringe. Make

<!-- chunk -->

### sure you don’t “suck” with the syringe as you withdraw.

<!-- chunk -->

### If nothing comes out, repeat the process. It may take several tries before

<!-- chunk -->

### anything passes, but don’t be concerned; just be patient. Putting in a small

<!-- chunk -->

### amount of fluid every 5 minutes will not hurt the bowel. In fact, often small

<!-- chunk -->

### children get dehydrated from not drinking enough fluids when they are feverish

<!-- chunk -->

### or sick, so the body could be absorbing all of the liquid that you have put into the

<!-- chunk -->

### bowel.

<!-- chunk -->

### Tell an older child they can go “potty” if they feel that they need to. If they

<!-- chunk -->

### don’t, repeat the process. Then wait a minute or two and put a little more fluid

<!-- chunk -->

### in.

<!-- chunk -->

### With a baby, on the other hand, put a diaper on the baby’s bottom after putting

<!-- chunk -->

### one syringe-full into the rectum, and then wrap the diaper in a towel. (Enemas

<!-- chunk -->

### can make the stool “runny,” and you don’t want it to leak onto you.) Then cuddle

<!-- chunk -->

### and hold the baby for a few minutes. If nothing comes out after about 10–20

<!-- chunk -->

### minutes, repeat the procedure.

<!-- chunk -->

### The stool should be soft. If only a small amount of hard stool is passed, you

<!-- chunk -->

### may still need to repeat the process until a soft stool passes. The trick is to get

<!-- chunk -->

### the bowel to move freely.

# RECTAL INJECTIONS

<!-- chunk -->

### Rectal injections are similar to enemas, but they are not used to try to clear the

<!-- chunk -->

### colon. Instead, a small amount of herbal fluid is injected into the rectum, and it is

<!-- chunk -->

### left there to be absorbed rather than being expelled.

<!-- chunk -->

### Rectal injections can be used to:

<!-- chunk -->

### ▪ Shrink hemorrhoids or anal fistulas (using liquid preparations of astringent

<!-- chunk -->

### herbs)

<!-- chunk -->

### ▪ Administer an herbal remedy to a child or elderly person who is having trouble

<!-- chunk -->

### swallowing an herb they need

<!-- chunk -->

### ▪ Hydrate a person or child who is severely dehydrated but can’t or won’t drink

<!-- chunk -->

### fluids (using solutions of mineral-rich herbs like alfalfa or oat straw to provide

<!-- chunk -->

### electrolytes)

# DOUCHES

<!-- chunk -->

### Douches can be helpful for vaginal infections, vaginal irritation, and excessive

<!-- chunk -->

### bleeding. For a douche, you will need an enema bag with a vaginal tip. Mix the

<!-- chunk -->

### douche solution the same way that you would mix an enema solution. Hold the

<!-- chunk -->

### liquid in the vagina for 5–15 minutes, using pillows to prop the hips up, before

<!-- chunk -->

### expelling it.

<!-- chunk -->

### Here are some suggestions for herbs to use in douches. For vaginal yeast

<!-- chunk -->

### infections, consider using calendula flowers, pau d’arco bark, or barberry root.

<!-- chunk -->

### Tea tree oil can also be used cautiously (some women find it highly irritating),

<!-- chunk -->

### following the same directions for making an enema solution with essential oils

<!-- chunk -->

### described in the previous section. Use only 1 drop per quart.

<!-- chunk -->

### For bacterial vaginosis, mix 10% povidone iodine and 90% distilled water for

<!-- chunk -->

### a douche solution. For excessive bleeding, use styptic herbs like bayberry root

<!-- chunk -->

### bark, calendula, yarrow, or white oak bark.

<!-- chunk -->

### Regular douching is not recommended and can cause an imbalance of

<!-- chunk -->

### beneficial vaginal microbes.

# BATHS AND SOAKS

<!-- chunk -->

### Baths and soaks invigorate and stimulate circulation, or relax and soothe sore

<!-- chunk -->

### muscles. They can help to ease itching and skin eruptions and to hasten recovery

<!-- chunk -->

### from colds and fevers.

<!-- chunk -->

### Add herbs to a bath by hanging 3–4 tea bags from the tap, or put a tea infuser

<!-- chunk -->

### in the water. A large gauze bag or a cotton sock will hold larger amounts of

<!-- chunk -->

### herbs. You can also make a strong decoction, strain it, and add the liquid to the

<!-- chunk -->

### bath.

<!-- chunk -->

### Essential oils may be added to a bath as well; mix in a few drops of soap to

<!-- chunk -->

### disperse the oil in the bath water. Use caution: essential oils can burn the skin if

<!-- chunk -->

### not well diluted.

<!-- chunk -->

### A sitz bath is useful for hemorrhoids and for prostate, vaginal, and uterine

<!-- chunk -->

### problems. It is also used to relax the pelvic floor in preparation for labor. Fill a

<!-- chunk -->

### large basin as above, and sit down in the water.

<!-- chunk -->

### Foot soaks can help restore circulation to the legs, reduce swelling in the

<!-- chunk -->

### ankles or feet, and heal injuries. Fill a wash basin with hot water and herbs and

<!-- chunk -->

### soak the feet.

# HERBS FOR BATHS AND SOAKS

<!-- chunk -->

### The following are useful herbs for herbal baths and soaks:

<!-- chunk -->

## Itching (Hives, Chicken Pox, etc.) Yellow dock, burdock, Oregon grape,

<!-- chunk -->

### barberry, comfrey, chickweed, lavender, vinegar (hot bath only), clay

<!-- chunk -->

## Swelling and Inflammation Comfrey, plantain, white oak bark, calendula, witch

<!-- chunk -->

### hazel, chamomile

<!-- chunk -->

## Cleansing Skin Pores Ginger, chamomile, vinegar (hot bath only)

<!-- chunk -->

## Relaxing Nerves Kava-kava (seriously, try this as a whole-body soak), lavender,

<!-- chunk -->

### chamomile, rose, catnip, Epsom salts

# SWEAT BATHS

<!-- chunk -->

### Many people in temperate climates the world over have used sweating to both

<!-- chunk -->

### prevent and treat disease. Scandinavians built saunas; Native Americans built

<!-- chunk -->

### sweat lodges. The pioneer herbalist Samuel Thomson would wrap a person

<!-- chunk -->

### sitting in a chair in blankets and place a hot stone in a pail at his feet. By pouring

<!-- chunk -->

### water into the pail, the steam would come up under the blankets until the patient

<!-- chunk -->

### started to perspire.

<!-- chunk -->

### A sweat bath can be used to:

<!-- chunk -->

### ▪ Help break a fever

<!-- chunk -->

### ▪ Speed recovery from colds, flu, or other acute infections

<!-- chunk -->

### ▪ General detoxification of the blood and lymph

<!-- chunk -->

### ▪ Stimulate the immune system

<!-- chunk -->

### This therapy is contraindicated if you have a weak heart or high blood

<!-- chunk -->

### pressure or if you are weak, pale, and feeble. It is also not good for small

<!-- chunk -->

### children or very elderly people.

<!-- chunk -->

### Mildly overheating the body simulates a slight fever and can be a powerful

<!-- chunk -->

### natural healing tool against serious disease. Fever is a natural defense and

<!-- chunk -->

### healing response of the immune system, created and sustained by the body to rid

<!-- chunk -->

### itself of harmful pathogens and to restore health. The high body temperature

<!-- chunk -->

### speeds up metabolism and inhibits the replication of the harmful virus or

<!-- chunk -->

### bacteria.

<!-- chunk -->

### Here’s how to take a sweat bath:

<!-- chunk -->

## Step one: Drink plenty of water, and do not eat for 2 hours prior to the sweat

<!-- chunk -->

### bath.

<!-- chunk -->

## Step two: Prepare an infusion with herbs that enhance perspiration. Drink some

<!-- chunk -->

### of the tea before entering the bath, and sip the tea frequently while in the bath.

<!-- chunk -->

### Examples of sweat-enhancing teas include:

<!-- chunk -->

### ▪ Herbal Composition, Children’s Composition, or Herbal Crisis (see Chapter

<!-- chunk -->

### Twelve)

<!-- chunk -->

### ▪ Yarrow and peppermint tea (equal parts)

<!-- chunk -->

### ▪ Fresh ginger juice tea

<!-- chunk -->

## Step three: Fill the tub with water as hot as can be tolerated comfortably. Epsom

<!-- chunk -->

### salts, Redmond clay, ginger, or essential oils may be added to the bath. If adding

<!-- chunk -->

### essential oils, mix them with a tablespoon of natural salt or ½ cup Epsom salts

<!-- chunk -->

### before adding them to the bath. This helps the oils dissolve in the water. (See

<!-- chunk -->

### more about aromatherapy in Chapter Ten.)

<!-- chunk -->

## Step four: Soak in the tub for at least 20 minutes. Keep the water as hot as you

<!-- chunk -->

### can tolerate comfortably. Keep as much of your body submerged in the water as

<!-- chunk -->

### possible. If you start to feel faint, sit up and drape a cool washcloth over your

<!-- chunk -->

### face. Drain the tub if you become too uncomfortable.

<!-- chunk -->

## Step five: Gentle massage with a skin brush helps bring the blood to the surface

<!-- chunk -->

### of the skin.

<!-- chunk -->

## Step six: Optional: After soaking in the bath, stand up and rinse off with a quick,

<!-- chunk -->

### cool shower. This will enhance the sweating effect.

<!-- chunk -->

## Step seven: Go to bed and pile on the covers. Drink plenty of fluids. Stay in bed

<!-- chunk -->

### for at least an hour. It’s fine to fall asleep. When finished, take a shower to

<!-- chunk -->

### cleanse the skin and close your pores.

<!-- chunk -->

### Don’t put small children into a really hot bath. Use a warm bath and gently

<!-- chunk -->

### wash their body down with some natural soap and a washcloth to make certain

<!-- chunk -->

### their pores are open. Add 3–4 drops lavender oil or tea tree oil (diluted with a

<!-- chunk -->

### natural liquid soap) or a little bit of Dr. Bronner’s peppermint, tea tree, or

<!-- chunk -->

### eucalyptus soap to the bath. The essential oils will help to stimulate the

<!-- chunk -->

### circulation and draw the blood to the extremities.

# COLD-SHEET TREATMENT

<!-- chunk -->

### A cold-sheet treatment is performed using the same basic procedures as for the

<!-- chunk -->

### sweat bath, but is much more effective in inducing perspiration. It is a powerful

<!-- chunk -->

### way to detoxify the body.

<!-- chunk -->

### Here’s how to do the cold-sheet treatment:

<!-- chunk -->

## Step one: Follow steps 1–5 for the sweat bath.

<!-- chunk -->

## Step two: Cover the bed with a piece of plastic.

<!-- chunk -->

## Step three: Soak a sheet in water (warm for a milder effect; cold for a stronger

<!-- chunk -->

### effect). As the person gets out of the bath, immediately wrap them in the damp

<!-- chunk -->

### sheet and have them lie down on the plastic sheet. Wrap the plastic around the

<!-- chunk -->

### damp sheet, and cover the person with blankets to warm up the body and induce

<!-- chunk -->

### perspiration.

<!-- chunk -->

## Step four: After about 1 hour, have the person get up and shower to clean off

<!-- chunk -->

### the skin.

# DRAWING BATHS

<!-- chunk -->

### Sweat baths open the sweat glands to cause perspiration, whereas drawing baths

<!-- chunk -->

### target the sebaceous glands (or oil ducts) in the skin. Drawing baths are useful

<!-- chunk -->

### for skin eruptive diseases, rashes, hives, pox, acne, and so forth. They can also

<!-- chunk -->

### be helpful for detoxification from heavy metals, especially the clay baths.

<!-- chunk -->

### A variety of herbs may be used in a drawing bath. You can also use Epsom

<!-- chunk -->

### salts and/or clay. Herbs that are good for drawing baths include mucilaginous

<!-- chunk -->

### herbs like comfrey leaf, plantain, and various seaweeds and alterative herbs like

<!-- chunk -->

### Oregon grape, goldenseal, burdock, yellow dock, and red clover. Oregon grape,

<!-- chunk -->

### goldenseal, and yellow dock all contain a yellow dye that can color the skin. It

<!-- chunk -->

### wears off rather quickly, but be warned.

<!-- chunk -->

### To make an herbal drawing bath, make a decoction of any of these herbs in a

<!-- chunk -->

### large pot containing 1–2 gallons of water. Use about ¼–½ cup herbs per gallon.

<!-- chunk -->

### Simmer the herbs for at least 20–30 minutes, add this to the bath, then fill the tub

<!-- chunk -->

### with more water, adjusting the temperature to make the bath comfortable.

<!-- chunk -->

### You can also use commercially prepared liquid herbal extracts in a bath, but

<!-- chunk -->

### you need about 2–4 ounces of extract per bath, which can be expensive.

<!-- chunk -->

### Two other agents that are really effective for drawing baths are Epsom salts

<!-- chunk -->

### and fine clays. Epsom salts are available at any supermarket or drugstore. Use

<!-- chunk -->

### about 2 cups Epsom salts per bath. Epsom salts are also helpful for sweat baths.

<!-- chunk -->

### They relax the skin pores and encourage elimination through the skin.

<!-- chunk -->

### Fine clay is even more effective than Epsom salts as a drawing agent. You

<!-- chunk -->

### have probably heard of or maybe even used a clay mask for drawing oil out of

<!-- chunk -->

### skin pores. Clay can be used in a similar manner in a bath, to absorb both fat-

<!-- chunk -->

### and water-soluble toxins from the body. Redmond clay, which is mined in Utah,

<!-- chunk -->

### is a good brand to use, and you can buy it in large boxes; but any fine clay will

<!-- chunk -->

### do.

# HERBAL STEAM BATHS

<!-- chunk -->

### Herbs rich in essential oils, such as basil, eucalyptus, and lavender, will release

<!-- chunk -->

### their properties into steam. Set a hot plate on the floor, and warm a pot of herbs

<!-- chunk -->

### and water on it. Sit on a cushioned metal chair over the hot plate and pot, and

<!-- chunk -->

### wrap a large blanket from your neck down to the floor. Be sure that the blanket

<!-- chunk -->

### doesn’t get too close to the hot plate and catch on fire! This leaves your head out

<!-- chunk -->

### of the steam and keeps it cool, but heats up the rest of your body nicely.

<!-- chunk -->

## Pelvic Steams

<!-- chunk -->

### Pelvic steams are used for vaginal problems and hemorrhoids. Vaginal steams

<!-- chunk -->

### are less invasive than douching, and hemorrhoid steams are a nice pain reliever.

<!-- chunk -->

### Boil 1 gallon of water, add 100 grams of herbs, and let steep for 10 minutes.

<!-- chunk -->

### Place the entire pot of water in your toilet. Test the temperature by holding your

<!-- chunk -->

### inner arm over the steam at toilet-seat level. If it’s too hot, let it cool for a few

<!-- chunk -->

### minutes and retest. When you feel comfortable with the temperature, sit on the

<!-- chunk -->

### toilet. Our favorite herbs for vaginal steams are yarrow, oregano, basil,

<!-- chunk -->

### calendula, and rosemary. Don’t put essential oils into a vaginal steam; they are

<!-- chunk -->

### too irritating to the mucous membranes. For hemorrhoids, a steam of yarrow,

<!-- chunk -->

### calendula, and arnica works well.

# CONTRAST THERAPY (ALTERNATING HOT AND COLD

# SOAKS)

<!-- chunk -->

### Hot water has a relaxing, opening effect, whereas cold water causes constriction

<!-- chunk -->

### and a reduction in blood flow. Alternating hot and cold hydrotherapy stimulates

<!-- chunk -->

### blood circulation, lymphatic drainage, and nerve energy. It can ease spasmodic

<!-- chunk -->

### pain and cramping, improve circulation, enhance muscle tone, and improve

<!-- chunk -->

### mood.

<!-- chunk -->

### One form of this method is to alternate hot and cold showers. Start with a

<!-- chunk -->

### comfortable hot shower for 3 minutes. Follow with a sudden change to cold

<!-- chunk -->

### water for 2 minutes. Repeat this cycle 3 times, ending with cold. After the

<!-- chunk -->

### shower, have a full or partial massage, or a brisk towel rub.

<!-- chunk -->

### Another form of this therapy is to alternate hot and cold soaks for a body part,

<!-- chunk -->

### such as the feet. This therapy has proved helpful for gangrene or other illnesses

<!-- chunk -->

### involving poor circulation to the extremities. Alternate the hot and cold soaks in

<!-- chunk -->

### the same manner as the hot and cold showers. To further enhance the effect,

<!-- chunk -->

### stimulating herbs (like capsicum, garlic, and ginger) may be added to the hot

<!-- chunk -->

### soak, and soothing herbs (like comfrey or plantain) to the cold soak.

# SITZ BATHS

<!-- chunk -->

### Alternating hot and cold sitz baths may provide symptom relief and help speed

<!-- chunk -->

### recovery from a variety of ailments. It may be used as a healing technique for a

<!-- chunk -->

### patient who cannot take a full shower. It is especially beneficial for increasing

<!-- chunk -->

### circulation in the pelvic and urethral area, and is commonly recommended for

<!-- chunk -->

### vaginal yeast infections, hemorrhoids, and the like.

<!-- chunk -->

## Step one: Fill a washtub with warm water so that you can soak your bottom in

<!-- chunk -->

### the water. As an alternative, fill the bathtub with water up to about ½ inch above

<!-- chunk -->

### your navel when seated.

<!-- chunk -->

## Step two: Soak in the bath for 20–30 minutes. Cover the upper body with a

<!-- chunk -->

### towel, and place a cool, wet washcloth on the forehead.

<!-- chunk -->

## Step three: Take a quick, cool rinse in the shower, or splash the body with cool

<!-- chunk -->

### water, before drying off, to further stimulate circulation.

<!-- chunk -->

## BOTTLES, GLYCERIN, AND OTHER SUPPLIES

<!-- chunk -->

### The quality of the plants you use to make your herbal medicines is important.

<!-- chunk -->

### Growing or gathering your own herbs can be a very rewarding experience.

<!-- chunk -->

### Therefore, we encourage you to grow your own medicinal plants if possible.

<!-- chunk -->

### Sources for herb plants and seeds are listed below.

<!-- chunk -->

### You can also learn how to sustainably harvest medicinal herbs growing in

<!-- chunk -->

### your area. To learn to identify local herbs, take a field botany class or plant

<!-- chunk -->

### walks with local herbalists and get to know the plants around you. To look for

<!-- chunk -->

### herbalists who do plant walks, visit http://findanherbalist.com.

<!-- chunk -->

### If you can’t harvest your own plants, purchase them from ethical suppliers.

<!-- chunk -->

### It’s a good thing to know who is picking the herbs you purchase. Are they

<!-- chunk -->

### harvesting these plants with reverence and respect for nature? Are they

<!-- chunk -->

### harvesting them ethically and sustainably? Are the herbs correctly identified and

<!-- chunk -->

### free from contaminants?

<!-- chunk -->

### This appendix contains a list of small companies that grow and collect herbs

<!-- chunk -->

### themselves. Small suppliers don’t have the variety of plants that large suppliers

<!-- chunk -->

### do, but purchasing from them supports small scale family growers and local

<!-- chunk -->

### economies. You’re also supporting people who are passionate about the quality

<!-- chunk -->

### of herbs they provide.

<!-- chunk -->

### Larger bulk herb companies offer a better selection, and sometimes better

<!-- chunk -->

### prices. You may also be able to save on shipping if you’re ordering a wide

<!-- chunk -->

### variety of herbs. Our favorite company in this category is Mountain Rose Herbs.

<!-- chunk -->

### Their commitment to quality herbs, and the time and money they spend

<!-- chunk -->

### supporting multiple free herbal clinics, great educational experiences, herb

<!-- chunk -->

### conferences, and the herbal community as a whole, is amazing.

<!-- chunk -->

### In Chapter Thirteen we’ve listed larger bulk herb companies that supplied

<!-- chunk -->

### each herb at the time this book was written. A more up-to-date database of herb

<!-- chunk -->

### companies and suppliers for individual herbs, herbal formulas, and supplements

<!-- chunk -->

### is found in the member area at http://herbiverse.com.

# Recommended Reading

<!-- chunk -->

### Chevallier, Andrew. Encyclopedia of Herbal Medicine. A nice general reference

<!-- chunk -->

### for 550 common herbs.

<!-- chunk -->

### Foster, Steven and Hobbs, Christopher; Western Medicinal Plants and Herbs. A

<!-- chunk -->

### nice field guide to herbal medicines.

<!-- chunk -->

### Gladstar, Rosemary: Herbal Recipes for Vibrant Health. A great reference for

<!-- chunk -->

### making herbal products, with useful recipes.

<!-- chunk -->

### Hall, Dorothy: Creating Your Herbal Profile. A good reference on personality

<!-- chunk -->

### profiles for medicinal herbs.

<!-- chunk -->

### Kaminski, Patricia and Katz, Richard; Flower Essence Repertory. A great guide

<!-- chunk -->

### to flower essences, both Bach flower remedies and North American remedies.

<!-- chunk -->

### Kuhn, Merrily A. and Winston, David; Herbal Therapy and Supplements: A

<!-- chunk -->

### Scientific and Traditional Approach. An excellent and reliable reference on

<!-- chunk -->

### scientific research, constituents and clinical use of many common herbs.

<!-- chunk -->

### McGuffin, Michael; Hobbs, Christopher; Upton, Roy; Goldberg, Alicia;

<!-- chunk -->

### Botanical Safety Handbook. A reliable, well-recognized safety handbook from

<!-- chunk -->

### the American Herbal Products Association.

<!-- chunk -->

### McIntyre, Anne; Flower Power: Flower Remedies for Healing Body and Soul. A

<!-- chunk -->

### great guide to emotional indications of herbs, flower essences and essential oils.

<!-- chunk -->

### Mills, Simon and Bone, Kerry; The Essential Guide to Herbal Safety. Another

<!-- chunk -->

### good safety reference.

<!-- chunk -->

### Moore, Micheal; Medicinal Plants of the Desert and Canyon West.

<!-- chunk -->

### —— Medicinal Plants of the Mountain West.

<!-- chunk -->

### —— Southwest School of Botanical Medicine Website (www.swsbm.com).

<!-- chunk -->

### Michael Moore is a great resource for dependable herbal information. His

<!-- chunk -->

### website is loaded with valuable material.

<!-- chunk -->

### PDR for Herbal Medicines. A good reference for scientific information on herbs,

<!-- chunk -->

### but not a good clinical resource.

<!-- chunk -->

### Wardwell, Joyce A: The Herbal Home Remedy Book. A nice guide to medicine

<!-- chunk -->

### making.

<!-- chunk -->

### Willard, Terry; Edible and Medicinal Plants of the Rocky Mountains and

<!-- chunk -->

### Neighboring Territories. A nice field guide to wild medicinal plants in the

<!-- chunk -->

### Western U.S.

<!-- chunk -->

### Wood, Matthew; The Book of Herbal Wisdom: Using Plants as Medicine.

<!-- chunk -->

### —— Wood, Matthew; The Earthwise Herbal: A Complete Guide to New World

<!-- chunk -->

### Medicinal Plants. An essential guide to eclectic materia medica.

<!-- chunk -->

### —— Wood, Matthew; The Earthwise Herbal: A Complete Guide to Old World

<!-- chunk -->

### Medicinal Plants.

<!-- chunk -->

### Matthew Wood is a great resource for detailed clinical indications for herbal

<!-- chunk -->

### remedies including emotional profiles.

<!-- chunk -->

### Ellingwood, 1919: The American Materia Medica. A great example of the work

<!-- chunk -->

### of Eclectic Physicians. Ellingwood has great indications not found in other

<!-- chunk -->

### Eclectic books.

<!-- chunk -->

### Culpeper, Nicholas. English Physician and Complete Herbal. A classic that still

<!-- chunk -->

### hold relevance for practitioners wanting a glimpse at herbalism 400 years ago.

<!-- chunk -->

### Felter, Harvey Wickes, MD, and John Uri Lloyd, PhrM, PhD, 1898: King’s

<!-- chunk -->

### American Dispensatory.

<!-- chunk -->

### Scudder, John M, MD, 1898: The American Eclectic Materia Medica. Scudder

<!-- chunk -->

### added valuable contributions to the Eclectic Materia Medica and almost single

<!-- chunk -->

### handedly changed their practice.

<!-- chunk -->

### Fyfe, William, MD, 1935: The Essentials of Modern Materia Medica. The last

<!-- chunk -->

### great Eclectic materia medica.

<!-- chunk -->

### Remington, Wood, 1918: The Dispensatory of the United States of America. An

<!-- chunk -->

### allopathic materia medica from when doctors still used botanical medicine.

<!-- chunk -->

### Shook, Edward. Advanced Treatise in Herbology. A semi-modern classic in the

<!-- chunk -->

### Neo-Thomsonianism style and a key book in the Mormon herbal revival.

<!-- chunk -->

### Grieve, Maude; A Modern Herbal. A classic guide to early 20th century herbal

<!-- chunk -->

### medicine.

<!-- chunk -->

### Alexander, Leslie M., and Linda A. Straub-Bruce. Dental Herbalism: Natural

<!-- chunk -->

### Therapies for the Mouth. The only comprehensive book on taking care of your

<!-- chunk -->

### mouth naturally! Includes great recipes for tooth powders, tooth paste and more.

<!-- chunk -->

### Hoffmann, David. Medical Herbalism: The Science and Practice of Herbal

<!-- chunk -->

### Medicine. A wonderful guide through the body systems from a great herbalist.

<!-- chunk -->

### David’s formulas are lovely!

<!-- chunk -->

### Plant Healer Magazine. Each year the magazine is printed and bound in

<!-- chunk -->

### volumes. These are a wonderful resource for contemporary herbalist and feature

<!-- chunk -->

### great articles from both well-known and up and coming herbalists.

<!-- chunk -->

### Menzies-Trull, Christopher. Herbal Medicine: Keys to Physiomedicalism

<!-- chunk -->

### including Pharmacopoeia. A valuable addition to the modern Physiomedical

<!-- chunk -->

### revival from England.

<!-- chunk -->

### Ganora, Lisa. Herbal Constituents: Foundations of Phytochemistry. The best

<!-- chunk -->

### introduction to phytochemistry for herbalists out there!

<!-- chunk -->

### Romm, Aviva Jill. Botanical Medicine for Women’s Health. An integrative

<!-- chunk -->

### approach to women’s health from a great herbalist and M.D.

<!-- chunk -->

### Masé, Guido. The Wild Medicine Solution: Healing with Aromatic, Bitter, and

<!-- chunk -->

### Tonic Plants. An inspiring guide every herbalist should read.

<!-- chunk -->

### Rogers, Robert Dale. The Fungal Pharmacy: The Complete Guide to Medicinal

<!-- chunk -->

### Mushrooms and Lichens of North America. The authoritative reference work to

<!-- chunk -->

### medicinal mushrooms from a great practitioner.

<!-- chunk -->

### Coffman, Sam; The Herbal Medic. A unique guide to emergency medicine using

<!-- chunk -->

### plants. A must read!

# About the Authors

<!-- chunk -->

### Thomas Easley is founder of the Eclectic School of Herbal Medicine. He is a

<!-- chunk -->

### clinical herbalist and professional member of the American Herbalists Guild.

<!-- chunk -->

### Easley integrates modern science and the deep and rich tradition of Western

<!-- chunk -->

### herbalism into a unified and systematic approach to health and healing. Easley

<!-- chunk -->

### emphasizes foods as primary medicine and uses intensive diets as well as stress

<!-- chunk -->

### reduction techniques, nutritional supplements, exercise and herbs to help people

<!-- chunk -->

### achieve their health goals. His approach draws on Traditional Western

<!-- chunk -->

### Herbalism, Clinical Nutrition, Functional Medicine, and his extensive clinical

<!-- chunk -->

### experience, which spans fifteen years of full-time practice and over 15,000

<!-- chunk -->

### clients.

Terrie Easley

<!-- chunk -->

### Steven Horne is a professional member and former president of the American

<!-- chunk -->

### Herbalists Guild and a professional member of the International Iridology

<!-- chunk -->

### Practioner’s Association, having also served on the board of directors of both

<!-- chunk -->

### organizations. He has spoken at numerous conventions and conferences and has

<!-- chunk -->

### helped to start four different herbal companies, giving him extensive practical

<!-- chunk -->

### experience in formulating and manufacturing herbal extracts. Horne maintains a

<!-- chunk -->

### part-time consulting practice, working one-on-one with clients to help them

<!-- chunk -->

### resolve their health problems.

Sears
